Talaq — Divorce

Ta'liq and its Classification as a Yamin: Hanafi and Zahiri Perspectives
Defines ta'liq and discusses its status as a yamin with inter-madhhab dispute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between ta'liq that constitutes a yamin and ta'liq that does not, and explain the Hanafi-Zahiri dispute regarding the legal effect of ta'liq as an oath.

Core Idea
A ta'liq is a yamin only when it involves a disliked or loved matter, not neutral conditions.

In this passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq, the author defines ta'liq (conditional suspension) and engages with al-Hidayah's choice of terminology. The central question: when does a conditional statement like 'If you enter, my wife is divorced' carry the force of an oath? The passage explores the linguistic and technical meanings of yamin and addresses a key intra-madhhab and inter-madhhab difference.

Guided Path
1
Identify the linguistic and technical definitions of ta'liq
Extract from the passage the lexical meaning from al-Qamus and al-Misbah, and the technical definition: binding the occurrence of one sentence's content to another's content.
The technical definition includes the shart and jazz components.
2
Distinguish types of ta'liq: those that are yamin and those that are not
Note the examples: ta'liq on menses or sunrise is not yamin; ta'liq entering or menstruating (as in 'if she enters') can be yamin. The key is whether the condition is disliked/loved or neutral.
The author points out that 'if she enters' is yamin, but 'if she menstruates' is not, requiring pondering.
3
Understand the Hanafi rationale for calling ta'liq a yamin
Yamin originally means strength. Ta'liq on disliked matters strengthens deterrence; on loved matters strengthens encouragement. Thus, it imparts 'strength' like an oath by Allah.
This reasoning is from al-Fath and al-Diraya as cited in the passage.
4
Recognize the inter-madhhab dispute: Hanafi vs. Zahiri
The common scholars (Hanafis) hold that ta'liq is a yamin, so if one swears 'I will not take an oath' and then uses ta'liq, he breaks his oath. The Zahiris disagree, considering only oaths by Allah as yamin.
The practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf) is whether kaffarah is required.
5
Identify the mufta bihi position and its nuance
The relied-upon Hanafi position is that ta'liq is a yamin in the technical sense when it involves a disliked or loved matter. Neutral conditions (e.g., menses) are not yamin.
This nuance is from the author of al-Bahr and is supported by al-Bada'i'.

نعم
In the name of Allah, the Most Gracious, the Most Merciful. Chapter of Suspension. When he finished explaining the immediate (al-munajjaz), he began with the suspended (al-mu'allaq). Suspension (ta'liq): from 'he suspended it' making it suspended. Thus in al-Qamus. And in al-Misbah: 'I suspended the thing to another, and I suspended it with tashdid and alif, so it became suspended.' End. In technical terminology: binding the occurrence of the content of one sentence to the occurrence of the content of another sentence. His expression 'ta'liq' is preferable to al-Hidayah's expression 'yamin' because it includes formal suspension even if it is not an oath, such as suspension by her menses or purity, or by her menstruation of a single menstruation, or by something he cannot avoid like sunrise and the coming of tomorrow, or by an act of her heart like love and will, or by an act of his heart: for in these cases it is not an oath according to al-Muhit, so he does not break his oath if he swore that he would not swear by it, although some of them are mentioned in this chapter like love and single menstruation, unlike 'if she enters' or 'if she menstruates'. And in Talkhis al-Jami': 'If he swore not...' The chapter of suspension as in swearing. His statement: 'And his expression ta'liq is preferable, etc.' He said in al-Nahr: 'I say there is consideration because he does not break his oath only because it is not an oath customarily, and this does not negate that it is an oath in the terminology of the jurists. Hence it was said in al-Diraya: The name yamin applies to swearing by Allah the Exalted and to ta'liq. Its explanation in al-Fath: Yamin originally means strength. It is called yamin because it gives strength regarding the matter sworn upon. There is no doubt that suspending something disliked for oneself upon a matter gives strength of abstention from that matter when it occurs legally, and suspending something beloved gives urging toward it. So it is an oath. In reality, ta'liq is only a condition and a consequence, so applying yamin to it is metaphorical due to the causal connection; thus the expression ta'liq is preferable.' End. I say: But the import of this is that ta'liq is called an oath only if it is upon a disliked or beloved matter to affirm abstention or urging, unlike ta'liq upon menses or the coming of tomorrow and the like. Ponder. And the author said at the beginning of the Book of Oaths: 'And it is apparent from what is in al-Bada'i' that ta'liq is an oath linguistically as well.' He said: 'Because Muhammad applied the term yamin to it, and his statement is proof in language.' And in al-Bada'i' it is mentioned that the benefit of the difference appears in the case of one who swore 'I will not take an oath' and then took an oath by divorce or emancipation: according to the common scholars he breaks his oath, and according to the Zahiri scholars he does not. End. His statement: 'Unlike if she enters or if she menstruates' — the first is apparent without the second, so ponder. His statement: 'Talkhis...' Yes.
Source pages p. 3

Commentary
This passage is an expansion of the matn's chapter heading 'باب التعليق'. The author defines ta'liq linguistically and technically. Linguistically, it means to suspend something to another. Technically, it is a conditional construct where the consequence (the content of the second sentence) is bound to the condition (the first sentence). The author then addresses the terminological choice between ta'liq and yamin (oath). Al-Hidayah had called this chapter 'al-Yamin', but the author prefers ta'liq because not every ta'liq functions as an oath. For example, a suspension upon a woman's menses or sunrise is not an oath because the condition is beyond one's control or neutral. The author quotes al-Nahr, which argues that even if such ta'liqs are not oaths customarily, they can be considered oaths in juristic terminology because they impart strength (the root meaning of yamin) – either deterrence from or encouragement to an act. However, the author notes that this applies only when the condition is something disliked or loved; neutral conditions like menses do not have this effect. The passage then moves to an inter-madhhab dispute: the 'common scholars' (i.e., Hanafis) hold that ta'liq by talaq or 'itaq is a yamin, so if one swears 'I will not take an oath' and then says 'If I do X, my wife is divorced', he has broken his oath. The Zahiris (أصحاب الظواهر) disagree, holding that such ta'liq is not a yamin. The Hanafi proof is that Imam Muhammad – a luminary of the school – applied the word 'yamin' to ta'liq, and his usage is authoritative in Arabic. Thus the Hanafi position is that ta'liq is a valid oath (yamin) when it carries the sense of deterrence or encouragement. The passage ends with a remark about a nuanced distinction between 'if she enters' and 'if she menstruates'.

Key Terms
التعليق (al-ta‘līq): Technically, binding the occurrence of the content of one sentence (the consequence) to the occurrence of the content of another sentence (the condition).
اليمين (al-yamīn): An oath; originally means strength. In juristic terminology, it includes swearing by Allah and, according to Hanafis, also ta'liq that imparts strength (on disliked/loved matters).
الشرط (al-sharṭ): The condition upon which the consequence depends. In the context of ta'liq, it is the first part of the statement (e.g., 'if you enter').
الجزاء (al-jazā’): The consequence that is bound to the condition (e.g., 'my wife is divorced').

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, when is a ta'liq (suspension) considered a yamin (oath) in the Hanafi school?
A) Only when it involves a condition that is disliked or loved by the person
B) Always, regardless of the condition
C) Only when swearing by Allah's name
D) Never, because ta'liq is not an oath

Key Takeaway
Ta'liq is a yamin only when it involves a disliked or beloved condition, imparting strength; this nuanced Hanafi position contrasts with the Zahiri literal view.

Definition of Ta'liq and Its Classification as a Yamin
Definitions and classification of ta'liq as yamin

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define ta'liq linguistically and technically, and explain when a suspended condition constitutes a yamin according to the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
Not every suspended divorce condition is an oath; Hanafi criteria distinguish ta'liq that binds as a yamin from those that do not.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq defines ta'liq (suspension) and addresses a critical distinction: when does a conditional divorce phrase act as an oath (yamin)? The author argues that the term 'ta'liq' is broader than 'yamin', covering cases where the condition is on natural events or internal states. He then cites Talkhis al-Jami' to establish the Hanafi criteria for when ta'liq breaks a prior oath not to swear by divorce.

Guided Path
1
Understand the linguistic and technical definition
Read the linguistic definitions from al-Qamus and al-Misbah, then the technical definition: tying the occurrence of one sentence to another.
The technical definition is broader than oath, as it includes conditional clauses that are not oaths.
2
Identify the author's preference for 'ta'liq' over 'yamin'
Note that the author prefers 'ta'liq' because it covers formal suspensions that are not oaths, unlike al-Hidayah which uses 'yamin'. List the examples given (menstruation, sunrise, love, etc.).
These examples show that some conditions are not intended as oaths.
3
Analyze the ruling from Talkhis al-Jami'
The passage states that ta'liq is a yamin if it has the rukn (conditional phrase) and is not idafa (temporal addition), except for heart acts and month-based conditions which are not pure ta'liq.
The rukn is the essential element of an oath; idafa lacks it.
4
Apply the examples
For each example (heart acts, kitaba, menstruation, etc.), determine whether it breaks a prior oath not to swear by divorce. Explain why based on the criteria.
The thamarah al-khilaf is the practical difference in oath-breaking.

بسم الله الرحمن الرحيم
باب التعليق
لما فرغ من بيان المنجز شرع في المعلق والتعليق من علقه تعليقاً جعله معلقاً كذا القاموس. وفي المصباح : علقت الشيء بغيره وأعلقته بالتشديد والألف فتعلق اهـ. وفي الاصطلاح ربط حصول مضمون جملة بحصول مضمون جملة أخرى. وتعبيره بالتعليق أولى من تعبير الهداية باليمين لشمول التعليق الصوري وإن لم يكن يميناً كالتعليق بحيضها وطهرها أو بحيضها حيضة أو بما لا يمكنه الامتناع عنه كطلوع الشمس ومجيء الغد أو بفعل من أفعال قلبها كالمحبة والمشيئة أو بفعل من أفعال قلبه فإنه في هذه المواضع ليس بيمين المحيط، فلا يحنث لو كان حلف أن لا يحلف بها مع أن بعضها مذكور في هذا الباب كالمحبة والحيض حيضة بخلاف إن دخلت» أو «إن حضت». وفي تلخيص الجامع : لو حلف لا يحلف يحنث بالتعليق لوجود الركن دون الإضافة لعدمه إلا أن يعلق بأعمال القلب أو بمجيء الشهر في ذوات الأشهر لأنه يستعمل في التمليك أو بيان وقت السنة فلا يتمحض للتعليق ولهذا لم يحنث بتعليق الطلاق بالتطليق لاحتمال حكاية الواقع ولا بـ«إن أديت فأنت حر» و«إن عجزت فأنت رقيق لأنه تفسير الكتابة ولا بـ إن حضت حيضة أو عشرين حيضة لاحتمال تفسير السنة اهـ.
In the name of Allah, the Most Gracious, the Most Merciful. Chapter of Suspension (Ta'liq). When he finished explaining the immediate (munjaz), he began [explaining] the suspended (mu'allaq). Ta'liq is derived from 'alaqa ta'liqan, meaning he made it suspended. Al-Qamus says that. In al-Misbah: 'I attached a thing to another' and 'a'laqtuhu' with tashdid and alif, so it becomes attached. In technical terminology: it is tying the occurrence of the content of one sentence to the occurrence of the content of another sentence. The author's expression 'ta'liq' is better than al-Hidayah's expression 'yamin' because ta'liq includes formal suspension even if it is not an oath, like suspension on her menstruation or purity, or on her menstruation one time, or on something he cannot refrain from like sunrise or the coming of tomorrow, or on an act of her heart like love and will, or on an act of his heart. In these cases, it is not an oath according to al-Muhit, so he does not break his oath if he swore not to swear by them, even though some are mentioned in this chapter like love and one menstruation, unlike 'if you enter' or 'if you menstruate'. In Talkhis al-Jami': If he swore not to swear, he breaks the oath by ta'liq because the essential element (rukn) exists, but not by idafa (conditional attachment to time) because it is absent, unless he suspends on acts of the heart or on the coming of the month in women with months because it is used for ownership or explanation of the term, so it does not become purely ta'liq. For this reason, he does not break the oath by suspending divorce on issuing a divorce itself (because it could be a report of a past event), nor by 'if you pay, you are free' and 'if you become incapable, you are a slave' because it is an explanation of kitaba (contract of manumission), nor by 'if you menstruate once or twenty times' because of the possibility of explaining the term.
Source pages p. 3

Commentary
This passage is a definition and classification passage from the commentary of Kanz al-Daqa'iq. It opens by defining ta'liq linguistically and technically: linguistically from 'alaqa (to suspend), and technically as linking the occurrence of one statement to the occurrence of another. The author then argues that the term 'ta'liq' is more appropriate than 'yamin' used by al-Hidayah because ta'liq includes situations that are not oaths, such as suspending on natural events (sunrise, tomorrow) or internal states (love, will). In such cases, if a person swore 'I will not swear by divorce', and then used such a suspension, he would not be breaking his oath. The passage then cites Talkhis al-Jami' discussing the criteria for when ta'liq constitutes a yamin (oath) for the purpose of breaking a prior oath: it requires the rukn (the conditional phrase) but not idafa (temporal addition). Exceptions are made for acts of the heart and month-based conditions, which are considered for ownership or term explanation, thus not pure ta'liq. The passage concludes with examples where ta'liq does not break an oath: suspending divorce on divorcing itself (possible report), on payment in kitaba, or on a single or multiple menstruations (possible explanation of the term).

Key Terms
التعليق (al-ta‘līq): Linguistically: suspending; technically: tying the occurrence of one sentence to another.
اليمين (al-yamīn): An oath that creates a binding obligation to do or refrain from an action; in this context, it is used for suspended divorce that functions like an oath.
الإضافة (al-idāfa): Temporal addition; a conditional phrase tied to a time period, considered to lack the rukn of an oath.
الركن (al-rukn): Essential element; in the context of a yamin, it is the conditional phrase that makes it binding.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following suspended conditions would NOT cause a person to break a prior oath 'I will not swear by divorce'?
A) If you enter the house, you are divorced.
B) If you menstruate once, you are divorced.
C) If you love me, you are divorced.
D) If I divorce you, you are divorced.

Key Takeaway
Ta'liq is a yamin only when it possesses the rukn and is not an idafa or a heart act; the intention and nature of the condition determine its legal status.

Conditions for Valid Ta'liq (Suspended Divorce)
Lists conditions for valid ta'liq with examples and intra-Hanafi dispute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the four conditions for a valid ta'liq and apply them to determine whether a suspended divorce takes effect immediately, is suspended, or is void.

Core Idea
A conditional divorce is valid only if the condition is possible, not yet realized, properly connected, and free of extraneous separators.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq enumerates the essential conditions for a valid ta'liq (suspended conditional divorce). Understanding these conditions is critical to distinguish between an immediate divorce (tanziz), a valid suspended divorce, and a void statement. We will examine each condition and apply them to the illustrative examples the author provides.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its legal category
Recognize that ta'liq is a suspended conditional divorce. The ruling depends on the nature of the condition.
2
Extract the primary condition: non-existent and possible
The condition must be non-existent at the time of utterance and possible of occurrence (khaṭar al-wujūd). If already realized, the statement becomes immediate (tanziz). If impossible, the divorce does not occur at all.
This is the position of the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad).
3
Understand the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on possibility
Note that Abu Yusuf (in another view) holds that possibility is not a condition. The relied-upon view (mufta bihi) requires it.
The passage sides with the Sahibayn.
4
Identify additional conditions: connector and no extraneous separator
When the consequence is delayed, a connector (rābiṭ) is needed. No extraneous separator may intervene between condition and consequence; appropriate interjections for emphasis or address are allowed.
Examples show 'O adulteress' for emphasis is permissible if placed after the condition.
5
Apply conditions to examples from the passage
Work through the examples: 'If you become healthy' (immediate due to continuous state), 'If you menstruate' (suspended to future), 'If camel enters needle's eye' (void), and complex cases with multiple wives.
Distinguish between continuous states (health, sight) and non-continuous (menstruation, illness).

وشرط صحة التعليق كون الشرط معدوماً على خطر الوجود فخرج ما كان محققاً كقوله «أنت طالق إن كان السماء فوقنا» فهو تنجيز، وخرج ما كان مستحيلاً كقوله «إن دخل الجمل في سم الخياط فأنت طالق فلا يقع أصلاً لأن غرضه منه تحقيق النفي حيث علقه بأمر محال وهذا يرجع إلى قولهما إمكان البر شرط انعقاد اليمين خلافاً لأبي يوسف. وعلى هذا ظهر ما في الخانية لو قال لها إن لم تردي علي الدينار الذي أخذتيه من كيسي فأنت طالق فإذا الدينار في كيسه لا تطلق امرأة. ولو قال إن حضت» وهي حائض أو مرضت وهي مريضة فعلى حيضة مستقبلة. ولو قال للصحيحة إن صححت فأنت طالق» طلقت الساعة، وكذا لو قال إن أبصرت أو سمعت وهي بصيرة أو سميعة لأن الصحة والسمع أمر يمتد فكان لبقائه حكم الابتداء بخلاف الحيض والمرض فإنهما مما لا يمتد. ولو قال لعبده إن ملكتك فأنت حر عتق حين سكت، وتمامه في المحيط من باب الشرط الذي يحتمل الحال والاستقبال. وبهذا علم أن قولهم إن ما كان محققاً تنجيز ليس على إطلاقه بل فيما لبقائه حكم ابتدائه . ومن شرائطه وجود رابط حيث كان الجزاء مؤخراً وسيأتي بيانه. ومن شرائطه أن لا يفصل بين الشرط والجزاء فاصل أجنبي فإن كان ملائماً وذكر لإعلام المخاطبة أو لتأكيد ما خاطبها بمعنى قائم في المنادى فإنه لا يضر كقوله لامرأته أنت طالق يا زانية إن دخلت الدار تعلق الطلاق بالدخول ولا حد ولا لعان لأنه لتأكيد ما خاطبها به كقوله «یا زینب» بخلاف ما إذا قال يا زانية أنت طالق إن دخلت فإنه قاذف وتمامه في المحيط من باب ما يتخلل بين الشرط والجزاء. وفي الخانية : لو قال إن دخلت الدار يا عمرة فأنت طالق ويا زينب فدخلت عمرة الدار طلقت ويسأل عن نيته في زينب؛ فإن قال نويت طلاقها أيضاً طلقت أيضاً، ولو قال ذلك بغير واو فقال نويت طلاقها مع عمرة طلقتا جميعاً، ولو قدم الطلاق فقال يا عمرة أنت طالق إن دخلت الدار و يا زينب فدخلت عمرة الدار طلقتا جميعاً، ولو قال لم أنو طلاق زينب لا يقبل قوله وتمامه فيها. وفي تلخيص الجامع من باب الاستثناء
The condition for the validity of ta'liq is that the condition must be non-existent and on the verge of existence. So what is already realized is excluded, like his saying 'You are divorced if the sky is above us' — that is immediate (tanziz). And what is impossible is excluded, like his saying 'If the camel enters the eye of a needle, you are divorced' — it does not occur at all, because his intent is to emphasize negation by suspending on an impossible matter. This refers to their (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad's) statement that possibility of fulfillment is a condition for the validity of the oath, contrary to Abu Yusuf (in another view). On this basis, what is in al-Khaniyya becomes clear: if he says to her 'If you do not return the dinar you took from my purse, you are divorced' and the dinar is in his purse, the woman is not divorced. If he says 'If you menstruate' while she is menstruating, or 'if you fall ill' while she is ill, then it refers to a future menstruation. If he says to a healthy woman 'If you become healthy, you are divorced,' she is divorced immediately, and similarly if he says 'If you see or hear' while she is sighted or hearing, because health and hearing are continuous matters, so their continuity has the ruling of initiation, unlike menstruation and illness which are not continuous. If he says to his slave 'If I own you, you are free,' the slave becomes free at the moment of silence. The complete discussion is in al-Muhit, in the chapter on conditions that may refer to present or future. From this it is known that their statement 'what is realized is immediate' is not absolute, but rather applies to matters whose continuance has the ruling of initiation. Among its conditions is the presence of a connector (rabiṭ) when the consequence is delayed, and its explanation will come. Among its conditions is that no extraneous separator should intervene between the condition and the consequence. If the separator is appropriate and is mentioned to inform the addressee or to emphasize what was addressed to her with a meaning present in the vocative, it does not harm, like his saying to his wife 'You are divorced, O adulteress, if you enter the house' — the divorce is suspended on entry, and there is no hadd punishment nor li'an, because it is to emphasize what he addressed to her, like saying 'O Zaynab.' This is unlike if he said 'O adulteress, you are divorced if you enter,' because then he is a slanderer (qadhif). The complete discussion is in al-Muhit, in the chapter on what intervenes between condition and consequence. In al-Khaniyya: If he said 'If you enter the house, O Amrah, you are divorced, and O Zaynab,' and Amrah entered the house, she is divorced, and he is asked about his intention regarding Zaynab; if he says 'I intended her divorce also,' she is divorced. If he said that without a waw and said 'I intended her divorce along with Amrah,' both are divorced. If he preceded the divorce and said 'O Amrah, you are divorced if you enter the house, and O Zaynab,' and Amrah entered, both are divorced. If he said 'I did not intend Zaynab's divorce,' his statement is not accepted. The complete discussion is there. And in Talkhis al-Jami', from the chapter on exceptions.
Source pages p. 4

Commentary
This passage enumerates the conditions for a valid ta'liq (suspended conditional divorce). The primary condition is that the condition must be non-existent at the time but possible of occurrence in the future. If the condition is already realized (like saying 'if the sky is above us'), the statement is immediate (tanziz) and divorce occurs instantly. If the condition is impossible (like 'if a camel enters a needle's eye'), the divorce does not occur at all, because it negates the intent. The passage notes an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf: Abu Yusuf holds that possibility is not a condition for the validity of the oath (i.e., even impossible conditions might be considered), while Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (in another narration) require possibility. The relied-upon view is that possibility is necessary. The passage then gives examples from al-Khaniyya: if a man tells his wife 'If you do not return the dinar from my purse' and the dinar is actually in his purse, the condition is impossible (she cannot return it because she didn't take it), so no divorce occurs. If he says 'If you menstruate' while she is menstruating, it refers to a future menstruation (because menstruation does not extend over time). Conversely, if he says to a healthy woman 'If you become healthy,' she is divorced immediately because health is continuous and its continuance has the effect of initiation. Similarly for seeing and hearing. Then the passage discusses the requirement of a connector (rabiṭ) when the consequence is delayed, and prohibits an extraneous separator between condition and consequence. It gives examples where an appropriate interjection (like 'O adulteress' or 'O Zaynab') does not invalidate the ta'liq because it is for emphasis or address, whereas calling her 'O adulteress' before the condition makes him a slanderer (qadhif) and incurs hadd. Further examples from al-Khaniyya show how multiple wives can be included in one conditional statement, and the importance of intention (niyya) in determining the scope of divorce.

Key Terms
خطر الوجود (khaṭar al-wujūd): The condition must be possible of occurrence; i.e., it must be on the verge of existence rather than impossible or already realized.
رابط (rābiṭ): A connector phrase that links the condition to the consequence, often needed when the consequence is delayed.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A man says to his healthy wife: 'If you become healthy, you are divorced.' According to the passage, what is the ruling?
A) The divorce occurs when she becomes healthy in the future.
B) The divorce occurs immediately upon utterance.
C) The divorce does not occur at all because the condition is already realized.
D) The divorce is suspended and takes effect only if he intended a future health.

Key Takeaway
A valid ta'liq requires the condition to be non-existent, possible, properly connected, and without extraneous separators; failure results in immediate divorce, void statement, or other consequences.

Talaq — Divorce
intermediate
12 min
Conditions for Valid Conditional Divorce (Ta'liq al-Talaq)
Explaining conditions for valid ta'liq

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the seven conditions for a valid ta'liq and apply them to distinguish between a valid suspensive divorce, immediate divorce (tanziz), and void condition.

Core Idea
A valid conditional divorce requires a possible, unrealized condition — otherwise it is immediate or void.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq expands on the conditions that make a conditional divorce (ta'liq) valid. Unlike an immediate divorce, ta'liq suspends the effect until a future event. The Hanafi school has detailed rules to prevent misuse. This lesson walks through the key conditions, focusing on what makes a condition valid or invalid, the role of intent, and common intra-Hanafi disputes.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core condition: ma'dum 'ala khatar al-wujud
The condition must be non-existent at the time of utterance but possible to occur. If already realized, it is immediate (tanziz). If impossible, it is void.
Examples: 'if the sky is above us' (realized → immediate), 'if a camel enters a needle's eye' (impossible → no divorce).
2
Apply the continuous vs. non-continuous distinction
For continuous states (health, hearing), a condition that is already true triggers immediate divorce. For non-continuous states (menstruation, illness), it refers to a future occurrence.
If a healthy wife is told 'if you become healthy', it is immediate because health is continuous.
3
Check continuity (ittisal) and separators
No extraneous pause or separator between condition and consequence. Suitable separators (e.g., calling her name for emphasis) are allowed.
Exception: those with speech impediments may pause.
4
Distinguish genuine condition (shart) from punishment (mujazah)
If the apparent intent is to harm or threaten (e.g., after insults), the statement is treated as immediate divorce. Inner intention matters but is not accepted outwardly per Bukhara fatwa.
If the husband is angry, it is mujazah; otherwise it is a condition.
5
Identify the rukn and the incomplete condition dispute
The rukn includes a conditional particle, condition, and consequence. Saying only 'anta taliq in' (you are divorced if) is incomplete: Abu Yusuf says no divorce (mufta bihi), Muhammad says immediate.
The fatwa follows Abu Yusuf because the statement is not fully expressed.

إنما يصح في الملك كقوله لمنكوحته إن زرت فأنت طالق أو مضافاً إليه كأن يكون على الجميع والبعض يا زانية إن تخلل الشرط والجزاء أو الإيجاب والاستثناء لم يكن قذفاً في الأصح ، وإن تقدم أو تأخر كان قذفاً لأنه للاستحضار عنه عرفاً ولإثبات الصفة وضعاً فلاءم من وجه دون آخر فعلق خللاً ونجز طرفاً عملاً بهما كيا طالق، وقد يعلق الخبر للنفي كالإقرار اهـ. ومن شرطه أن لا يكون الظاهر قصد المجازاة فلو سبته بنحو قرطبان وسفلة فقال إن كنت كما قلت فأنت طالق تنجز ، سواء كان الزوج كما قالت أو لم يكن، لأن الزوج في الغالب لا يريد إلا إيذاءها بالطلاق، فإن أراد التعليق يدين وفتوى أهل بخارى عليه كما في فتح القدير. ومن شرطه الاتصال فلو ألحق شرطاً بعد سكوته لم يصح. وفي الظهيرية : رجل له فأفأة أو ثقل في لسانه لا يمكنه إتمام الكلام إلا بعد مدة فحلف بالطلاق وذكر الشرط والاستثناء بعد تردد وتكلف، إن كان معروفاً بذلك جاز استثناؤه وتعليقه اهـ. وركنه أداة شرط وفعله وجزاء صالح فلو اقتصر على أداة الشرط لم يكن تعليقاً اتفاقاً واختلفوا في تنجيزه فلذا قال في الظهيرية لو قال أنت طالق إن ولم يزد تطلق للحال في قول محمد ولا تطلق في قول أبي يوسف والفتوى على قول أبي يوسف لأنه ما أرسل الكلام إرسالاً ذكره في الجامع العتابي. وكذلك لو قال أنت طالق ثلاثاً لولا أو قال وإلا، أو قال إن كان، أو قال إن لم يكن، لا تطلق في قول أبي يوسف وبه أخذ محمد بن سلمة اهـ .

القديمة
Book of Divorce, Chapter of Conditional Divorce. He swears and is bound by conditional divorce because the essential element exists, unlike the absolute form which lacks it. Except if he conditions it on acts of the heart or the coming of the month for women with menses, because that is used for ownership or setting a time for the year, so it does not become purely conditional. Hence, he is not bound by conditioning divorce on 'divorcing' due to possibility of narrating a past event, nor by 'if you pay you are free' and 'if you fail you are a slave' because that is an interpretation of Kitabah, nor by 'if you menstruate once or twenty menstruations' due to possibility of interpreting the year. The condition for validity of a conditional statement is that the condition be non-existent at the time but possible of occurrence. Thus excluded is what is already realized, like his saying 'You are divorced if the sky is above us' – that is an immediate divorce. Also excluded is what is impossible, like his saying 'If a camel enters a needle's eye, you are divorced' – it does not occur at all, because his purpose is to assert the negation by conditioning it on an impossibility. This returns to their view (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) that the possibility of fulfillment is a condition for the validity of an oath, contrary to Abu Yusuf. Based on this, what is in al-Khaniyyah becomes clear: if he says to her 'If you do not return the dinar you took from my purse, you are divorced,' and the dinar is in his purse, the woman is not divorced. If he says 'If you menstruate' while she is menstruating, or 'If you are ill' while she is ill, then it refers to a future menstruation. If he says to a healthy woman 'If you become healthy, you are divorced,' she is divorced immediately. Similarly, if he says 'If you see' or 'If you hear' while she sees or hears, because health and hearing are continuous matters, so the continuation has the ruling of initiation, unlike menstruation and illness which are not continuous. If he says to his slave 'If I make you owner, you are free,' the slave becomes free as soon as he is silent. The complete discussion is in al-Muhit, in the chapter on a condition that can apply to the present or future. From this it is known that their statement that what is realized is immediate divorce is not absolute, but rather applies where the continuation has the ruling of initiation. Among its conditions is that there be a link when the consequence is delayed, as will be explained. Among its conditions is that no extraneous separator intervene between the condition and the consequence. If the separator is suitable and mentioned to inform the addressee or to emphasize what was addressed with a meaning present in the vocative, it does not harm, like his saying to his wife 'You are divorced, O adulteress, if you enter the house' – the divorce is conditioned on entry, and there is no hadd nor li'an, because it is for emphasizing what he addressed her with, like his saying 'O Zaynab'. Contrary to if he says 'O adulteress, you are divorced if you enter' – then he is a slanderer. The complete discussion is in al-Muhit, in the chapter on what intervenes between condition and consequence. In al-Khaniyyah: If he says 'If you enter the house, O Amra, you are divorced, and O Zaynab,' and Amra enters the house, she is divorced, and he is asked about his intention regarding Zaynab. If he says 'I intended her divorce too,' she is also divorced. If he said that without a waw (and) and said 'I intended her divorce with Amra,' both are divorced. If he placed the divorce first and said 'O Amra, you are divorced if you enter the house, and O Zaynab,' and Amra enters the house, both are divorced. If he says 'I did not intend Zaynab's divorce,' his statement is not accepted. The complete discussion is in it. In Talkhis al-Jami' from the chapter of Istithna... The matn 'Al-Jami' law halafa...' the explanation of this statement preceded in the section on adding divorce to time. His statement: 'Contrary to menstruation and illness...' its explanation is as in al-Khaniyyah: since the Lawgiver attached rulings to the whole that do not attach to each part, He made the whole a single thing. His statement: 'And in Talkhis al-Jami' from the chapter of Istithna...' we have presented the summary of its explanation.

Book of Divorce, Chapter of Conditional Divorce. It is only valid if in ownership, like his saying to his wife 'If you visit, you are divorced,' or attached to ownership, like his saying 'If I marry you, you are divorced,' i.e., conditional on ownership, like his saying to a stranger woman 'If I marry you' i.e., if I wed you, because marriage is a cause for ownership, so the cause is used metonymically for the effect, i.e., if I make you owned by marriage, like his saying 'If I buy a slave...' this statement is the first section of divorce before consummation. His statement: 'And the fatwa of the people of Bukhara is upon...' i.e., upon that it is for punishment. And his expression: 'And some have stated that the fatwa of the people of Bukhara is that it is for punishment, not for condition.' I say: In al-Dhakhirah, quoting from some fatawa, that the fatawa of the people of Bukhara are that it is for punishment, not condition. The chosen view and fatwa is that if he is in a state of anger, it is for punishment; otherwise, it is for condition. The same is in al-Fatawa al-Khaniyyah from al-Muhit. In al-Walwalijiyyah: If he intended conditional divorce without punishment, it does not occur as long as he is not a safilah. They discussed 'safilah' regarding Abu Hanifah – rahimahullah – that a Muslim cannot be a safilah; only a disbeliever is a safilah. From Abu Yusuf: he is the one who does not care what he says and what is said to him. It is narrated from Muhammad that he is the one who plays with pigeons and gambles. Khalaf said: he is the one who, when invited to food, carries something from there. The fatwa is upon what is narrated from Abu Hanifah because it is absolute. In al-Misbah: al-qurtuban is what the common people say for the one who has no ghirah, so it is altered from its original. Al-Asma'i said: Its origin is kaltuban from kalb (dog), which means pimping, and the ta and nun are extra. He said: This word is Arabic, and the common people changed it first to qaltuban, then lower commoners came and changed it further to qurtuban. Meaning: safilah is the old one.
Source pages p. 4

Commentary
This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq expands on the conditions for valid conditional divorce (ta'liq al-talaq) in the Hanafi school. The author first distinguishes between a true condition (ta'liq) and other expressions that are not conditional, such as those used for ownership or time specification. He then states a key condition: the condition must be non-existent at the time of utterance but possible to occur (ma'dum 'ala khatar al-wujud). If the condition is already realized (like 'if the sky is above us'), the statement is not conditional but an immediate divorce (tanziz). If the condition is impossible (like 'if a camel enters a needle's eye'), the divorce does not occur at all because the intent is to negate the divorce by means of an impossibility. This latter point reflects the view of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad that the possibility of fulfillment is a condition for the validity of an oath (yamin), contra Abu Yusuf (a different Abu Yusuf? Actually the text says 'khilafan li-Abi Yusuf' but earlier it said 'qauluhuma' referring to the two students; so Abu Yusuf is included in 'qauluhuma' but then it says 'khilafan li-Abi Yusuf'? This is likely a scribal confusion; the intended meaning is that Abu Yusuf and Muhammad hold possibility as a condition, while another figure (possibly Abu Hanifah?) disagrees. The commentator then applies these principles to various examples: if a husband conditions divorce on the wife returning a dinar that is actually in his purse, no divorce occurs because the condition is impossible. If he says 'if you menstruate' while she is already menstruating, it refers to a future menstruation because menstruation does not last continuously. If he says 'if you become healthy' to a healthy woman, she is divorced immediately because health is a continuous quality. Similarly, if he says to his slave 'if I make you owner' while the slave is already owned, the slave is freed immediately because ownership is continuous. The author then discusses other conditions: there must be a link (rabi) between condition and consequence when the consequence is delayed, and no extraneous separator, though a suitable separator (like addressing her by name or even calling her an adulteress for emphasis) does not break the condition. If the separator is purely for informing, it is fine; if it is unrelated, it invalidates the condition. The commentator also discusses cases with multiple women and the effect of intervening names. He then addresses the scenario where the apparent intent is punishment (mujazah) rather than true conditioning; if the husband insults his wife and then says 'if you are as you say, you are divorced,' it is an immediate divorce because the prevalent custom is to harm her, unless he truly intends a condition, in which case his inner intention is considered (yudin) but not accepted outwardly according to the fatwa of the people of Bukhara. Another condition is continuity (ittisal): if he pauses and then adds a condition, it is invalid, except for those with speech impediments. The essential elements (rukn) of a conditional statement are the conditional particle (adatu sharṭ), the condition, and a valid consequence. If he says only the particle, like 'anta taliq in' (you are divorced if), there is a dispute: according to Muhammad, it is an immediate divorce; according to Abu Yusuf, it is not, and the fatwa is on Abu Yusuf's view because the statement is not complete. Finally, the passage discusses that conditional divorce is only valid when the wife is in his ownership (at the time of the condition) or if the condition itself leads to ownership (like 'if I marry you'). The discussion of 'safilah' and 'qurtuban' relates to the context of insults that trigger punishment-based divorce.

Key Terms
تعليق (ta'liq): Conditional suspension of a legal effect (here divorce) upon occurrence of a future condition.
تنجيز (tanziz): Immediate execution of a divorce or other legal effect without condition.
شرط صحة التعليق (shart sihhat al-ta'liq): Conditions that must be present for a conditional statement to be validly suspensive.
إمكان البر (imkan al-birr): Possibility of fulfilling the condition (literally 'possibility of righteousness' in oaths).
مجازاة (mujazah): Punishment or retribution; used when a husband's conditional statement is intended to harm or threaten divorce rather than as a genuine condition.
سفلة (safilah): A base person; in this context, someone who habitually uses foul language or has no self-respect.
قرطبان (qurtuban): A term used in abusive language, originally meaning a pimp (from kalb), corrupted from qaltuban.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In Hanafi fiqh, if a husband says to his wife 'You are divorced if the sky is above us,' what is the ruling?
A) The divorce is suspended until the condition occurs (ta'liq).
B) The divorce occurs immediately (tanziz) because the condition is already realized.
C) The divorce does not occur at all because the condition is impossible.
D) The divorce is considered a revocable divorce (raj'i).

Key Takeaway
For a valid conditional divorce, the condition must be non-existent at utterance but possible: if already realized, it is immediate; if impossible, it is void.

Conditional Divorce upon Acquiring Ownership: Validity and Conditions
Clarifying the validity of conditional divorce upon ownership and the role of cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which conditional divorce upon acquiring ownership is valid in the Hanafi school and distinguish valid from invalid conditions.

Core Idea
Conditional divorce upon future ownership is valid only if the condition is a cause of ownership, like purchase or captivity.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq addresses a key question in Hanafi divorce law: can a divorce be conditioned on a future event that will bring ownership? The author clarifies that such conditional divorces are valid provided the event is a legitimate cause of ownership, and he discusses nuances of phrasing and the types of ownership that count.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Determine what the author is ruling upon: a conditional divorce (or manumission) tied to acquiring ownership. The ruling is that it is valid (sahih) if the condition is a cause of ownership.
2
Locate the dalil
The text alludes to a hadith often cited by al-Shafi'i: 'No divorce in what one does not own.' The Hanafi response distinguishes between direct divorce on unowned property and conditional divorce when ownership is later acquired.
The hadith is from Abu Dawood and al-Tirmidhi.
3
Analyze the distinction between valid and invalid causes
Valid causes include purchase (shira') and captivity (saby); invalid causes include death because death nullifies ownership, not creates it.
The author contrasts purchase with death of the master for an heir's conditional manumission.
4
Understand the difference in phrasing
Pure conditional (e.g., 'if I marry you') is valid; addition to marriage without specifying the doer (e.g., 'with your marriage') is invalid due to incomplete speech, but 'with my marrying you' is valid because it metaphorically indicates ownership.
This is a subtle linguistic point in Hanafi jurisprudence.
5
Apply the principle to real and virtual ownership
Ownership includes real ownership (ongoing marriage) and virtual ownership (during the waiting period). Conditional divorce is valid in both, except for some cases of final divorce during a final waiting period.
Refer to al-Bada'i for the exception.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الطلاق باب التعليق فهو حر أي إن ملكته بسبب الشراء بخلاف ما لو قال الوارث لعبد مورثه إن مات سيدك فأنت . فإنه لا يصح التعليق لأن الموت ليس بموضوع للملك بل موضوع لإبطاله بخلاف الشراء. وفي كشف الأسرار : ولو قال لحرة أن ارتديت فسبيت فملكتك فأنت حرة صح اهـ. لأن السبي من أسباب الملك الموضوعة، ولو مثل بقوله أنت طالق يوم أتزوجك لكان أولى وفي المعراج : وتمثيله غير مطابق لأنه تعليق محض بحرف الشرط ولو إضافة إلى النكاح لا يقع كما لو قال أنت طالق مع نكاحك أو في نكاحك - ذكره في الجامع - بخلاف أنت طالق مع تزوجي إياك فإنه يقع وهو مشكل. وقيل الفرق أنه لما أضاف التزوج إلى فاعله واستوفى مفعوله جعل التزويج مجازاً عن الملك لأنه سببه وحمل مع على بعد تصحيحاً له وفي نكاحك لم يذكر الفاعل فالكلام ناقص فلا يقدر بعد النكاح فلا يقع ويصح النكاح اهـ. أطلق الملك فأفاد أنه يشمل الحقيقي كالملك حال بقاء النكاح والحكمي كبقاء العدة والتعليق يصح فيهما، وقدمنا عند شرح قوله آخر الكنايات والصريح يلحق الصريح أن تعليق طلاق المعتدة فيهما صحيح في جميع الصور إلا إذا كانت معتدة عن بائن وعلق بائناً كما في البدائع اعتباراً للتعليق بالتنجيز . وفي المصباح زاره يزوره زيارة وزوراً قصده فهو زائر وزور وزوار مثل سافر وسفر وسفار، ونسوة زور أيضاً وزوار وزائرات والمزار يكون مصدراً وموضع الزيارة والزيارة في العرف قصد المزور إكراماً له واستئناساً به .اهـ. وقدمنا في أول كتاب الحج أنه لو حلف لا يزوره فلقيه من غير قصد فإنه لا يحنث وينبغي تقييدها بما قاله في المصباح من الإكرام والاستئناس للعرف فلا يحنث في مسئلة الكتاب إلا مع القصد للإكرام، فلو كان الشرط زيارتها فذهبت من غير قصد الإكرام لم يحنث، وفي عرفنا زيارة المرأة لا يكون إلا بطعام معها يطبخ عند المزور. وفي المحيط : حلف ليزورن فلاناً غداً ليعودنه فأتى بابه واستأذنه فلم يؤذن له لا يحنث فإن اتى بابه ولم يستأذنه يحنث حتى يصنع في الزائر والعائد من الاستئذان والفرق أن في الأول لم يتصور البر فلم ينعقد اليمين، وفي الثاني يتصور وهكذا ذكر في العيون وعلى قياس من قال إن لم أخرج من هذا المنزل اليوم فمنع أو قيد حنث يجب أن يحنث هنا في الوجهين وهو الختار لمشايخنا وفي النوازل: حلف يزور فلاناً لا حياً ولا ميتاً فشيع جنازته لا يحنث وإن زار قبره يحنث هو المختار لأن زيارة الميت زيارة قبره عرفاً لا تشييع جنازته .اهـ.
The Book of Divorce, Chapter of Conditional Divorce: 'So he is free' — meaning if he acquires ownership through purchase, contrary to if the heir says to the slave of his deceased: 'If your master dies, you are free' — it is not valid because death is not a cause for ownership but for nullifying it, unlike purchase. In Kashf al-Asrar: If he says to a free woman: 'If you apostatize and are captured and I own you, you are free' — it is valid, because captivity is a cause of ownership. If he had said 'You are divorced the day I marry you' it would be more appropriate. In al-Mi'raj: His example is not matching because it is pure conditional with the particle of condition; if it is added to marriage it does not occur, as if he says 'You are divorced with your marriage' or 'in your marriage' — mentioned in al-Jami' — unlike 'you are divorced with my marrying you' which does occur, and it is problematic. It is said the difference is that when he attached the marriage to its doer and completed its object, he made marriage a metaphor for ownership because it is its cause, and he interpreted 'with' loosely to validate it. In 'your marriage' the doer is not mentioned so the speech is incomplete; it is not interpreted after marriage so it does not occur, and the marriage is valid. He mentioned ownership absolutely, so it includes real ownership as in ongoing marriage, and virtual ownership as in the waiting period; conditional divorce is valid in both. We stated earlier in the explanation of the last kinayah that conditional divorce of a woman in waiting period is valid in all cases except if she is in a waiting period of a final divorce and he conditioned a final divorce, as in al-Bada'i, by analogy to immediate divorce. In al-Misbah: He visited him, visiting and visitation: he intended him, so he is a visitor; women also are visitors; the place of visit can be source or location; visitation in custom is intending the visited for honor and companionship. We stated earlier in the beginning of Hajj that if he swore not to visit him and he met him unintentionally, he does not break the oath. It should be restricted to what al-Misbay said about honor and companionship; so he does not break the oath in the book's issue except with intention of honor. If the condition was visiting her and she went without intention of honor, he does not break the oath. In our custom, a woman's visit only occurs with food she brings to cook at the visited. In al-Muhit: He swore to visit so-and-so tomorrow to return him; he came to his door and asked permission but was not given permission; he does not break the oath. If he came to his door and did not ask permission, he breaks the oath; the difference is that in the first, fulfillment was not possible so the oath was not concluded; in the second it is possible. And thus it is mentioned in al-Uyun, and by analogy to one who said 'if I have not left this house today' and was prevented or tied, he breaks the oath; it must be that he breaks the oath in both cases, and that is the chosen view of our scholars. In al-Nawazil: He swore to visit so-and-so alive nor dead; he followed his funeral; he does not break the oath; if he visited his grave, he breaks the oath; that is the chosen, because visiting the dead is visiting his grave customarily, not following his funeral.
Source pages p. 6

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq discusses conditional divorce when tied to acquiring ownership. The author first clarifies that if a master says to a slave 'If I purchase you, you are free,' the conditional divorce (or manumission) is valid because purchase is a cause of ownership. However, if an heir says to a slave of his deceased 'If your master dies, you are free,' it is invalid because death nullifies ownership, not creates it. The author then cites a case from Kashf al-Asrar: a man says to a free woman 'If you apostatize and are captured and I own you, you are free' — this is valid because captivity is also a cause of ownership. There follows a discussion about the phrasing of conditional divorce: whether it is a pure condition (using 'if') or an addition to marriage (e.g., 'with your marriage'). The text notes that saying 'you are divorced with my marrying you' works because 'marrying' here metaphorically indicates ownership, but 'with your marriage' does not because the doer is unspecified. The author then explains that ownership in conditional divorce includes both real ownership (during marriage) and virtual ownership (during the waiting period), and conditional divorce is valid in both. The second half of the passage defines 'visitation' (ziyarah) in oaths. Citing al-Misbah, visitation customarily means intending the visited for honor and companionship. Therefore, in an oath not to visit, merely meeting without such intention does not break the oath. The passage gives various scenarios from al-Muhit and al-Nawazil, concluding that visiting a grave counts as visiting the dead, but following a funeral does not. Throughout, the commentary emphasizes the importance of custom (urf) in determining the meaning of terms in oaths.

Key Terms
ملك (milk): Ownership includes real ownership (during marriage) and virtual ownership (during the waiting period); both are valid for conditional divorce.
سبب الملك (sabab al-milk): A cause that brings about ownership, such as purchase or captivity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, which of the following conditions makes a conditional divorce upon acquiring ownership invalid?
A) If the condition is purchase
B) If the condition is captivity
C) If the condition is the death of the master
D) If the condition is marriage

Key Takeaway
A conditional divorce upon acquiring ownership is valid only when the condition is a legitimate cause of ownership, such as purchase or captivity.

Defining 'Visitation' in Oaths: The Role of Custom
Defining visitation in oaths and applying custom to determine oath-breaking.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine whether an oath not to visit is broken based on the intention and custom (urf) as per Hanafi fiqh.

Core Idea
Visitation in oaths is defined by custom: intending the visited for honor and companionship.

This passage defines 'visitation' (ziyarah) in the context of oaths. The Hanafi school emphasizes that the legal meaning of terms in oaths is determined by custom (urf), not literal dictionary definitions. The passage illustrates this with practical scenarios, such as following a funeral versus visiting a grave.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition of visitation
From al-Misbah: visitation (ziyarah) means intending the visited for honor and companionship. This is the baseline definition.
The literal meaning is 'to intend' (qasd), but the legal meaning in oaths is restricted by custom.
2
Apply the principle of urf to oaths
In oaths, the meaning of terms follows custom (urf). Therefore, 'visitation' in an oath means intending the person with honor, not just any meeting.
The passage mentions that in custom, a woman's visit often includes bringing food.
3
Analyze the scenarios from al-Muhit
If a man swears to visit someone tomorrow to return him (i.e., for a follow-up visit), and he goes to the door but is not given permission, he does not break the oath because fulfillment was not possible. But if he does not ask permission, he breaks the oath because it was possible.
The difference is based on whether the act was practically possible to complete.
4
Distinguish between visiting a grave and following a funeral
If a man swears to visit someone alive nor dead, following the funeral does not break the oath because it is not considered visitation in custom, but visiting the grave does break it.
The chosen view (al-mukhtar) is that visiting the dead means visiting his grave customarily.
5
Extract the rule
The key rule: For an oath not to visit to be broken, the meeting must be intentional and for the purpose of honor/companionship according to custom.

Source Text

Adding Divorce to Marriage: Hanafi Position and Refutation of Malik and Shafi'i
Distinguishing between conditional divorce and adding divorce to marriage, and refuting the objections of Malik and Shafi'i.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the validity of adding divorce to future marriage according to the Hanafi school, differentiate between conditional and immediate divorce based on how the woman is defined, and apply the Hanafi responses to the objections of Imam Malik and Imam al-Shafi'i.

Core Idea
Adding divorce to marriage is valid conditional divorce; the woman is divorced upon marriage, and the objections of Malik and Shafi'i are refuted by distinguishing immediate from conditional and by qiyas on manumission.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq addresses a specific form of conditional divorce where the condition is the marriage itself. It refutes criticisms from Imam Malik and Imam al-Shafi'i, and details how the phrasing and definition of the woman affect the ruling. Understanding these distinctions is crucial for correctly applying Hanafi law in cases of conditional divorce.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish 'addition' from 'conditional' in divorce phrasing
The passage begins by noting that saying 'You are divorced the day I marry you' is an addition (إضافة), not a condition (تعليق). The ruling differs: an addition may imply immediate divorce, while a condition delays it. The commentary stresses that the chapter's topic is conditional divorce.
The author uses this to set up the proper context for the subsequent rulings.
2
Identify the Hanafi ruling on adding divorce to marriage
The Hanafi school holds that if a man says 'Every woman I marry is divorced' (or similar), the divorce is valid and occurs upon marriage. This is because the condition (marriage) triggers the divorce, analogous to conditional manumission.
This applies whether the woman is indefinite or defined, with nuances about definition.
3
Analyze Malik's objection and the Hanafi response
Imam Malik argued that such a condition blocks the door of marriage (انسداد باب النكاح). The Hanafi response: the man can still marry through a guardian, or after the divorce occurs (since 'كل' does not require repetition).
The key is that the condition does not render marriage impossible; it merely attaches a consequence.
4
Understand the effect of definition (تعريف) on the condition
If the woman is defined by indication (e.g., 'this woman'), the condition is meaningless and divorce is immediate. If defined by name/lineage and the woman is absent, the condition is operative. If present, definition by name is like indication and the condition becomes meaningless.
This distinction is based on whether the definition is definite or indefinite.
5
Analyze Shafi'i's hadith objection and the Hanafi response
Imam al-Shafi'i cites the hadith 'لا طلاق فيما لا يملك'. The Hanafi response: this hadith applies to immediate, unconditional divorce on unowned property. Conditional divorce on future ownership is different, as seen in the parallel of conditional manumission (qiyas).
The Hanafi use qiyas: just as one can say 'if I own this slave, he is free,' one can say 'if I marry you, you are divorced'.

His statement: (If he had said 'You are divorced' etc.) — meaning it would be added, not conditional, matching his statement 'or added to it.' Al-Nahr said: In al-Fath he answered that he used 'addition' in the linguistic sense and in other senses; it is not hidden that the objection here is invalid as al-Ramli said; indeed it applies to what is in al-Hidayah where he said: 'Chapter of oaths in the Book of Divorce, Chapter of Conditional Divorce' — a prevention from its blockage either due to his religion fearing his injustice or due to his worldly life due to lack of wealth; and its blockage is prevented because it is possible that a guardian marries him and he validates by action like bringing the obligatory to her, and by the possibility that he marries her after the divorce has occurred because the word 'every' does not require repetition, except that its validity does not differ between conditioning with the particle of condition or its meaning if the woman is indefinite. If she is definite, it is required that it be with an explicit condition; so if he says 'this woman whom I marry is divorced' and he marries her, she is not divorced because he defined her by indication, so the attribute 'I marry' has no effect on her; rather the attribute is لغو (meaningless) as if he said 'this is divorced' like his statement to his wife 'this woman who enters this house is divorced' — she is immediately divorced whether she enters or not, unlike his statement 'if I marry this' which is valid. In al-Dhakhirah: Definition by name and lineage is like definition by indication; so if he says 'So-and-so daughter of so-and-so whom I marry is divorced' and he marries her, she is not divorced. An objection is raised based on what is in al-Jami': A man named Muhammad ibn Abdullah had a slave; he said: 'If anyone speaks to the slave of Muhammad ibn Abdullah this, then his wife is divorced' — the swearer pointed to the slave, not to himself. Then the swearer himself spoke to the slave; his wife is divorced. If definition by name were like definition by indication, his wife would not be divorced as if he pointed to himself. The answer: Definition of the present is by indication, and of the absent by name and lineage. In the case of Muhammad ibn Abdullah, the swearer is present, so his definition should be by indication or addition, which were not present; thus it remains indefinite and falls under the name of indefinite. In the case of divorce, the name and lineage are for the absent, not the present; thus definition occurs with them, and the attribute becomes لغو (meaningless). Even in the case of divorce, if the woman is present with the swearer, mentioning her name and lineage does not constitute definition and the attribute is not لغو; the divorce is tied to marriage. This is what Shaykh al-Islam mentioned in al-Jami'. If he says 'Every woman I marry as long as Amrah is alive' or 'until Amrah dies' she is divorced, and he marries Amrah — Muhammad mentioned in the book that she is not divorced. The general Mashayikh hold that the interpretation of the case is that Amrah was pointed to; if she was not pointed to, she is divorced and falls under the name of indefinite. On the analogy of what Shaykh al-Islam mentioned, it should be said: If Amrah is present, she is divorced; if absent, she is not divorced. The full discussion is in al-Dhakhirah. He prioritized conditioning on ownership because there is no disagreement about it, and delayed the conditioned thing because al-Shafi'i holds it invalid whether specific or general, based on the hadith of Abu Dawood and al-Tirmidhi, which they deemed hasan (sound) as marfu: 'There is no vow for the son of Adam in what he does not own, no manumission for him in what he does not own, no divorce for him in what he does not own.' And our (Hanafi) argument: Divorce, if he adds divorce to marriage, it occurs after the marriage, like if he says to a woman 'if I marry you, you are divorced' — contrary to here because the chapter is for conditional divorce, and the pronoun 'صبح' refers to it, and his statement 'added' is a circumstantial accusative from it.
Source pages p. 6

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq continues the discussion of conditional divorce, focusing on the distinction between adding divorce to a future marriage (إضافة إلى النكاح) and pure conditional divorce. The author first comments on an earlier suggestion that it would be better to say 'You are divorced the day I marry you' rather than 'if I marry you' — the commentator explains that the former is an addition, not a condition, and the ruling differs. The passage then addresses an objection from Imam Malik: If one says 'Every woman I marry is divorced,' Malik argues it is invalid because it blocks the door of marriage. The Hanafi response is that it does not necessarily block marriage; one can marry through a guardian, or after the divorce occurs (since 'كل' does not imply repetition). The text then discusses the effect of defining the woman. If she is defined by indication (like 'this woman'), then the condition is ignored and divorce is immediate. If defined by name and lineage, the same applies if she is present; if absent, the condition is effective. There is a detailed example from al-Jami' involving a man named Muhammad ibn Abdullah and his slave, illustrating the difference between definition by name for present vs. absent individuals. The passage concludes with the Hanafi refutation of al-Shafi'i's hadith about no divorce in what one does not own. The Hanafi position is that the hadith refers to immediate, unconditional divorce, not conditional divorce upon future ownership. Adding divorce to a future marriage is valid, and the chapter is specifically about conditional divorce (تعليق), which is the proper context for these rulings.

Key Terms
إضافة إلى النكاح (idafah ila al-nikah): Adding divorce to a future marriage, e.g., 'every woman I marry is divorced' — this is a type of conditional divorce where the condition is the act of marriage.
تعريف بالإشارة (ta'rif bil-isharah): Defining a person by pointing (e.g., 'this woman') — such definition makes the condition irrelevant and the divorce immediate.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The marriage is invalid because the condition prevents its validity.
B) The wife is divorced immediately upon marriage.
C) The statement is invalid and the marriage proceeds without divorce.
D) The wife is divorced only if he intends divorce at the time of marriage.

Key Takeaway
Adding divorce to marriage is a valid conditional divorce in the Hanafi school; the woman is divorced upon marriage, and objections from Malik and Shafi'i are refuted by distinguishing between immediate and conditional divorce and by analogy to manumission.

Definition by Gesture vs Name in Conditional Divorce
Distinguishing gesture vs name in defining a woman for conditional divorce

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between definition by gesture and definition by name/lineage in conditional divorce, state the Hanafi ruling for present vs absent women, and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute.

Core Idea
For a present woman, only gesture defines; name and lineage leave the condition effective.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq clarifies a subtle point in Hanafi talaq: how a woman is defined in a conditional divorce depends on whether she is present or absent. The distinction affects whether the condition is operative or the divorce takes effect immediately. Understanding this helps avoid common errors in applying conditional divorce.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two modes of definition
Recognize that 'definition by gesture' (al-ta'rīf bi al-ishārah) is used for present persons, while 'definition by name and lineage' (al-ta'rīf bi al-ism wa al-nasab) is used for absent persons.
These are the only recognized modes in this Hanafi discussion.
2
Apply to present vs absent women
For a present woman, pointing (gesture) is necessary to define her; naming her is insufficient. For an absent woman, naming and lineage suffice.
The key distinction is presence/absence at the time of the utterance.
3
Trace the objection from al-Jami'
The passage presents an objection: In a case from al-Jami', when a man says 'If anyone speaks to Muhammad ibn Abdullah's slave' and points to the slave, then if he himself speaks to the slave, his wife is divorced. This seems to contradict the rule that name/lineage equals gesture.
The objection highlights a potential inconsistency.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response
The response distinguishes: The swearer is present, so definition must be by gesture or addition. Since he pointed to the slave (not himself), the definition is by gesture for the slave, but for himself (the swearer) no definition occurred, so the condition remained indefinite. In the divorce case, the woman is absent, so name/lineage works.
Thus, the two cases are consistent when presence/absence is considered.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
Shaykh al-Islam and the majority hold that for a present woman, gesture is required; name/lineage does not define. For an absent woman, name/lineage suffices. This is the relied-upon position.
A minority view equates name/lineage to gesture even for present women, but this is not adopted.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And in al-Dhakhira: Definition by name and lineage is like definition by gesture. If someone says 'Flanah bint Flan whom I marry is divorced' and then marries her, she is not divorced. An objection was raised based on what is mentioned in al-Jami': A man named Muhammad ibn Abdullah has a slave and says, 'If any person speaks to Muhammad ibn Abdullah's slave, then my wife is divorced,' and the swearer pointed to the slave, not himself. Then the swearer himself spoke to the slave; his wife is divorced. If definition by name were like definition by gesture, his wife would not be divorced, just as if he pointed to himself. The answer: Definition of a present person is by gesture, and of an absent person by name and lineage. In the case of Muhammad ibn Abdullah, the swearer is present, so definition is by gesture or addition, and they did not occur, so it remained indefinite and fell under the indefinite noun. In the divorce case, name and lineage are for the absent, not the present, so definition is achieved by both and the description becomes irrelevant. Even if Flanah were present at the time of the utterance, mentioning her name and lineage does not achieve definition, and the description is not irrelevant; divorce is attached to marriage. This is what Shaykh al-Islam mentioned in al-Jami'. Some differentiated: definition by addition and gesture does not admit indefiniteness at all, while definition by name and lineage admits indefiniteness. If someone says, 'Every woman I marry as long as Amrah is alive,' or 'until Amrah dies,' she is divorced, and then he marries Amrah. Muhammad said in al-Kitab that she is not divorced, and the majority of mashayikh hold that the interpretation of the case is that Amrah was pointed to. So if she were not pointed to, she would be divorced and fall under the indefinite noun. According to what Shaykh al-Islam mentioned, it should be said: If Amrah is present, she is divorced; if absent, she is not. The full discussion is in al-Dhakhira.
Source pages p. 7

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute concerning how a woman is defined in a conditional divorce. The general rule is that if a man says to a specific woman, 'You are divorced if I marry you,' it takes effect immediately (tanziz). But when the woman is described by a condition (like 'the woman I marry'), the ruling differs based on whether she is defined by gesture (present) or by name/lineage (absent). The author of al-Dhakhira equates definition by name/lineage to definition by gesture, meaning the condition is ineffective and the woman is divorced immediately if she is known. However, the objection from al-Jami' shows that definition by name for a present person does not actually define because present persons are defined by gesture; thus, the description remains relevant. The passage resolves this by distinguishing between present and absent women: for an absent woman, name and lineage define her, and the condition is attached to marriage; for a present woman, she must be pointed to for the definition to be effective. The majority opinion (mufta bihi) is that if a specific woman like Amrah is present and not gestured towards, she is not divorced upon marriage; if she is absent, she is. This preserves the validity of conditional divorce while respecting the linguistic norms of definition.

Key Terms
التعريف بالإشارة (al-ta'rīf bi al-ishārah): Defining a person by pointing to them when they are present; this is the only effective way to define a present person in conditional divorce.
التعريف بالاسم والنسب (al-ta'rīf bi al-ism wa al-nasab): Defining a person by mentioning their name and lineage; effective only for absent persons in this context.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the preferred Hanafi view, if a man says to a present woman (without pointing) 'Fatimah bint Ali, if I marry you, you are divorced,' what is the ruling when he marries her?
A) She is not divorced because the condition is invalid.
B) She is divorced upon marriage because the condition is effective.
C) She is divorced immediately because she is named.
D) The conditional divorce is invalid because the woman is present.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi conditional divorce, a present woman must be defined by gesture for the condition to be void; otherwise, the condition stands and divorce occurs upon the specified event.
