Timing of the Condition in Suspended Divorce
Ruling on conditional divorce with multiple wives and timing of condition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on which wife is divorced when a man suspends divorce on a condition and marries multiple wives at different times relative to the condition.

Core Idea
In suspended divorce, the timing of the condition determines which wife is affected: the wife married before the condition is fulfilled is divorced.

This passage from Al-Muhit clarifies the Hanafi position on a tricky scenario: a man says "Every woman I marry is divorced if I speak to so-and-so." He then marries two women, one before speaking and one after. Which one is divorced? The answer depends on the order of the condition and the marriage. Understanding this ruling helps avoid confusion when applying conditional divorce in real-life situations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the wording of the oath
Look at how the condition is phrased: is it before or after the oath? In the first case, the oath comes first: 'Every woman I marry is divorced if I speak to so-and-so.'
2
Determine the sequence of events
Identify when the condition is fulfilled (e.g., speaking) and when the marriages occur. Here, the man marries one woman before speaking and another after speaking.
3
Apply the Hanafi rule of ta'liq
The divorce becomes effective at the moment the condition is fulfilled. It applies to the wife who is already married at that time. So the wife married before the condition is divorced; the wife married after is not.
The condition applies to future marriages, but once the condition is fulfilled, future marriages are not affected because the condition already occurred.
4
Consider the reversed order
If the condition is stated first, e.g., 'If I speak to so-and-so, then every woman I marry is divorced,' then the wife married after speaking is divorced. The condition was stated before the marriage, so it applies to marriages after the condition is fulfilled.
5
Generalize the principle
The key is to track the timing of the condition's fulfillment relative to the marriage. The divorce only applies to the wife who is married before the condition occurs when the condition is stated after the oath, and to the wife married after the condition occurs when the condition is stated before the oath.
This ruling prevents contradictory outcomes and ensures consistency in ta'liq.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي المحيط من باب تعليق اليمين بالشرط: لو قال كل امرأة تزوجها فهي طالق إن كلمت فلاناً فتزوج امرأة قبل الكلام وامرأة بعده طلقت التي تزوجها قبل الكلام، ولو قدم الشرط بأن قال إن كلمت فلاناً فكل امرأة أتزوجها فهي طالق طلقت التي تزوجها بعد الكلام وكذا إذا وسطه .اهـ.
In al-Muhit, from the chapter of suspending an oath on a condition: If he said, 'Every woman I marry is divorced if I speak to so-and-so,' and he married a woman before speaking and a woman after speaking, the woman he married before speaking is divorced. If he put the condition first by saying, 'If I speak to so-and-so, then every woman I marry is divorced,' the woman he married after speaking is divorced. Similarly, if the condition is in the middle.
Source pages p. 12

Commentary
This passage is a ruling from al-Muhit on conditional divorce where the oath is suspended on a condition (ta'liq al-yamin bi al-shart). The specific scenario involves a man who says, 'Every woman I marry is divorced if I speak to so-and-so.' The ruling distinguishes three cases based on the sequence of the condition and the action. (1) If the condition is stated after the oath (i.e., he says the oath first, then the condition), and he marries one woman before speaking and another after speaking, only the woman married before speaking is divorced. This is because the condition applies to future marriages after the statement, but the first marriage occurred before the condition was stated, so it is not affected. However, the second marriage is not divorced because the condition was not fulfilled before the marriage. (2) If the condition is stated first (e.g., 'If I speak to so-and-so, then every woman I marry is divorced'), and he marries after speaking, the woman he married after speaking is divorced. (3) Similarly, if the condition is in the middle, the ruling follows the same principle: the divorce applies to the wife who is married after the condition is fulfilled. The key is that the divorce is linked to the occurrence of the condition, and the timing determines which wife is affected.

Key Terms
تعليق اليمين بالشرط (ta‘līq al-yamīn bi al-sharṭ): Suspending an oath (divorce) on the occurrence of a future condition
الشرط (al-sharṭ): The condition upon which the divorce is suspended

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A man says, 'Every woman I marry is divorced if I enter this house.' He marries Fatima before entering, then enters the house, then marries Aisha. Which wife is divorced?
A) Fatima is divorced
B) Aisha is divorced
C) Both are divorced
D) Neither is divorced

Key Takeaway
In suspended divorce, only the wife married before the condition is fulfilled is divorced when the oath is stated first; if the condition is first, the wife married after is divorced.

Compound Oaths at Marriage: Sequential vs. Simultaneous Descent
Rulings on compound oaths in idafat al-talaq with intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the Hanafi rulings on compound marriage oaths and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn regarding the descent of multiple oaths.

Core Idea
Abu Hanifa gives divorce priority, making wives separated before other oaths apply; the Sahibayn treat all oaths as simultaneous.

This passage from al-Muhit deals with cases where a man links divorce, zihar, and ila to the act of marriage. The key issue is whether these oaths take effect sequentially or simultaneously, leading to significant differences in practical outcomes. Understanding this ikhtilaf sharpens your ability to predict the effects of complex marital pronouncements.

Guided Path
1
Identify the oath combinations
List the different scenarios: single condition, repeated condition, compound oaths (divorce+zihar+ila), and separate conditions.
Focus on the compound case with divorce, zihar, and ila.
2
Analyze Abu Hanifa's reasoning
Explain why divorce descends first: it instantly separates the wife, removing the basis for zihar and ila.
His principle: the strongest oath (divorce) takes precedence.
3
Analyze the Sahibayn's reasoning
They hold that all oaths are effective simultaneously because they are all triggered by the same condition (marriage).
No inherent priority among the oaths.
4
Apply to different orders
If zihar/ila are mentioned first, then divorce, what happens? The passage indicates that if zihar/ila come first, they are valid because the wife is still a wife at that moment; divorce then occurs too.
Order matters: the first valid oath takes effect, then the second.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
Later Hanafi authorities (e.g., al-Hidayah) adopt Abu Hanifa's view as the relied-upon position.
Fatwa is given according to Abu Hanifa in most cases.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي باب إضافة الطلاق إلى الملك : لو قال إذا تزوجت امرأة فهي طالق فتزوج امرأتين تطلق إحداهما والبيان إليه ولو كان قال وحدها لا يقع شيء فإن تزوج أخرى بعدهما وقع عليها ولو قال يوم أتزوجك فأنت طالق قال ذلك ثلاث مرات فتزوجها يقع الثلاث لأن هذه أيمان ولو قال إذا تزوجتك فأنت طالق وأنت علي كظهر أمي ووالله لا أقربك ثم تزوجها وقع الطلاق ويلغو الظاهر والإيلاء عند أبي حنيفة خلافاً لهما لما عرف أن عنده ينزل الطلاق أولاً فتصير مبانة وعندهما ينزلن جملة. ولو قال إن تزوجتك فوالله لا أقربك وأنت علي كظهر أمي وأنت طالق فتزوجها وقع الطلاق وصح الطهار والإيلاء لأنها بنزول الظهار والإيلاء لا تصير مبانة، وكذا لو قال إن تزوجتك فأنت طالق إن تزوجتك فأنت علي كظهر أمي ثم تزوجها صحا لأنهما يمينان ذكر لكل واحدة شرطاً على حدة وهو التزوج فنزلا معاً اهـ.
In the chapter on adding divorce to ownership: If he said, 'Whenever I marry a woman, she is divorced,' and he married two women, one of them is divorced and the choice is his. If he said 'one' [i.e., 'one is divorced'], nothing occurs; if he then marries another after them, the divorce falls on her. If he said, 'On the day I marry you, you are divorced,' and said that three times, then married her, three divorces occur because these are oaths. If he said, 'If I marry you, you are divorced, and you are like my mother's back, and by Allah I will not approach you,' and then married her, divorce occurs and the zihar and ila are void according to Abu Hanifa, contrary to the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), because according to him divorce descends first so she becomes separated, while according to them they descend together. If he said, 'If I marry you, by Allah I will not approach you, and you are like my mother's back, and you are divorced,' and then married her, divorce occurs and the zihar and ila are valid because by the descent of zihar and ila she does not become separated. Similarly, if he said, 'If I marry you, you are divorced; if I marry you, you are like my mother's back,' and then married her, both are valid because they are two oaths, each with its own condition (marriage), and they descend together.
Source pages p. 12

Commentary
This passage is a set of rulings from the chapter on adding divorce to ownership (idafat al-talaq ila al-milk) in al-Muhit. It covers multiple scenarios involving compound oaths and disputes between Abu Hanifa and the two companions (Sahibayn). The first scenario: a man says 'Whenever I marry a woman, she is divorced' then marries two women. He must choose which one is divorced; if he says 'one is divorced,' nothing happens until he marries another, on whom the divorce falls. Next: saying 'On the day I marry you, you are divorced' three times results in three divorces because each is an independent oath. Then a compound case: saying 'If I marry you, you are divorced, and you are zihar, and I swear not to approach'—Abu Hanifa says the divorce occurs first, making her separated, so the zihar and ila are void. The Sahibayn say all descend together, so the divorce is valid but the zihar and ila also apply? Actually, the text says 'yulghu' (void) at Abu Hanifa, and 'yatatlq' (divorce occurs) with the other elements valid? The passage is complex: the key is that according to Abu Hanifa, the divorce descends first, making her a non-wife, so subsequent oaths (zihar, ila) are meaningless. According to the Sahibayn, all oaths descend simultaneously, so each takes effect. The last case shows that if the oaths are separate with distinct conditions, they all take effect.

Key Terms
إضافة الطلاق إلى الملك (iḍāfat al-ṭalāq ilā al-milk): Adding divorce to ownership; i.e., linking divorce to the act of acquiring ownership (marriage)
الظهار (al-ẓihār): A pre-Islamic form of divorce where a man says to his wife 'You are to me like my mother's back'
الإيلاء (al-īlā'): A vow of abstinence from sexual relations with one's wife

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Divorce occurs, but zihar and ila are valid
B) Divorce occurs, and zihar and ila are void
C) Only zihar occurs
D) Nothing occurs because the statements contradict

Key Takeaway
In compound oaths at marriage, the Hanafi school prioritizes divorce, making the wife separated before other oaths can apply, reflecting the preemptive nature of divorce.

Oath on Marriage vs. Purchase of a Minor
Distinction between marrying and buying a minor in oaths

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi ruling that the word 'woman' in oaths includes minors in marriage contexts but not in purchase or speech contexts.

Core Idea
The same word can have different scope in oaths depending on whether the act is exclusive to women.

This passage from al-Muhit explores a subtle but important distinction in Hanafi oath jurisprudence. The ruling hinges on whether the act in question (marriage vs. purchase) is inherently linked to women. Understanding this distinction helps you grasp how lexical ambiguity is resolved in the madhhab based on context and custom.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two main scenarios
The passage presents two oaths: one about marrying a woman and one about buying a woman. In each, the person performs the act with a minor girl.
The minor is below puberty but can be married in Hanafi law.
2
Note the divergent rulings
For the marriage oath, marrying a minor breaks the oath. For the purchase oath, buying a minor does not break the oath.
The same word 'woman' yields opposite results.
3
Analyze the reasoning
The difference is that marriage is exclusively for women, so the term 'woman' expansively includes minors. Purchase can involve men or women, so the term is interpreted literally and does not include minors.
The context of the oath determines the scope.
4
Apply to the third case
The passage also addresses an oath about speaking to a woman: speaking to a minor does not break the oath because customarily minors are not the subject of such oaths.
This reinforces that context and custom (urf) guide interpretation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي باب الحلف على التزويج إن تزوجت امرأة فعبدي حر فتزوج صبية حنث، ولو حلف لا يشتري امرأة فاشترى صغيرة لم يحنث. والفرق أن اسم المرأة مطلقاً لا يتناول الصغيرة إلا أن في الشراء اعتبر ذكر المرأة لأن الشراء قد يكون للرجل وقد يكون للمرأة ولم يعتبر ذلك المرأة في النكاح لأن النكاح لا يكون إلا للمرأة فلغا ذكرها. ولو قال إن كلمت امرأة فكلم صبية لا يحنث لأن الصبي مانع عن هجران الكلام فلا تراد الصبية في اليمين المعقودة على الكلام عادة ولا كذلك التزوج .اهـ.
In the chapter of oaths on marriage: If he said, 'If I marry a woman, my slave is free,' and he marries a minor girl, he breaks the oath. If he swore not to buy a woman and bought a minor girl, he does not break the oath. The difference is that the term 'woman' absolutely does not include a minor, except that in purchase, the mention of woman is considered because purchase can be for a man or a woman, but that is not considered in marriage because marriage is only for a woman, so her mention is redundant. If he said, 'If I speak to a woman,' and he speaks to a minor girl, he does not break the oath because the minor is a preventive against the cessation of speech, so minor girls are not intended in oaths concerning speech usually, unlike marriage.
Source pages p. 12

Commentary
This passage is a ruling from the chapter of oaths on marriage (al-halaf 'ala al-tazwij) in al-Muhit. It addresses the interpretive scope of the word 'woman' (imra'a) in different contexts. In a marriage oath, marrying a minor girl counts as breaking the oath because marriage is exclusively with women, so the word 'woman' is considered to include minors by default. However, in a purchase oath, buying a minor girl does not break the oath because the word 'woman' is not automatically inclusive of minors; purchase can involve women or men, so the word is interpreted literally, and a minor is not a 'woman' in the common sense. Similarly, in an oath about speaking to a woman, speaking to a minor does not break the oath because minors are not typically the subject of such oaths—speaking to a minor is not considered 'speaking to a woman' in customary usage. The key distinction is that marriage inherently requires a female, so the term 'woman' in that context is expansive; purchase and speech do not, so the term is restricted to adult females.

Key Terms
الحلف على التزويج (al-ḥalaf ‘alā al-tazwīj): An oath concerning marriage, e.g., swearing to marry a woman
الصبية (al-ṣabiyya): A minor girl (below the age of puberty)
حنث (ḥanth): Breaking an oath, which incurs expiation (kaffarah)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A man swears, 'If I buy a woman, my slave is free.' He buys a minor girl. What is the ruling?
A) He breaks the oath
B) He does not break the oath
C) The oath is invalid
D) It depends on his intention

Key Takeaway
The scope of the word 'woman' in oaths varies: in marriage it includes minors; in purchase and speech it does not, due to the exclusive nature of marriage.

6 min
Multiple Divorces from Two Oaths on One Person
Ruling on one person subject to two separate oaths causing multiple divorces

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when a person is subject to two independent conditional oaths (divorce) and the same wife fulfills both conditions, and apply the principle to similar scenarios.

Core Idea
Two independent oaths both apply: the wife receives two divorces if she meets both conditions.

This passage from al-Dhakhira illustrates a key principle in Hanafi divorce law: when a husband makes two separate conditional divorce declarations, and the same wife triggers both conditions, both divorces take effect cumulatively. We will dissect the examples given and understand the reasoning behind this ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two oaths
Read the scenario carefully: the husband makes two distinct conditional statements. In the first example, he says (1) 'If I marry so-and-so, she is divorced' and (2) 'Every woman I marry is divorced.' In the second, (1) 'If I speak to so-and-so, you are divorced' and (2) 'If I speak to any human, you are divorced.'
The oaths are independent; neither cancels the other.
2
Determine the overlap
Notice that the wife who triggers the first condition also falls under the second condition. In example 1, the specific woman (so-and-so) is also a 'woman' covered by the second oath. In example 2, so-and-so is also a 'human'.
The same act (marrying or speaking) fulfills both conditions.
3
Apply the principle of cumulative effect
Since both oaths are valid and independent, each takes effect whenever its condition is met. There is no concept of one oath being subsumed by the other when the conditions are different in wording but the subject matter overlaps.
This is based on the literal application of the husband's words.
4
Conclude the number of divorces
Each oath triggers one divorce, so the wife receives two divorces (taliqatayn).
If the oaths were contradictory or one was conditional on the other, the result might differ.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي الذخيرة في نوع آخر في دخول شخص واحد تحت اليمينين : إذا قال إن تزوجت فلانة فهي طالق ثم قال كل امرأة أتزوجها فيه طالق ثم تزوج فلانة طلقت تطليقتين بحكم اليمينين لأنها فلانة وامرأة، وكذلك. لو قال إن كلمت فلاناً فأنت طالق وإن كلمت انساناً فأنت طالق فكلمت فلاناً تطلق تطليقتين بحكم اليمينين اهـ.
In al-Dhakhira, in another type concerning a single person falling under two oaths: If he said, 'If I marry so-and-so, she is divorced,' then he said, 'Every woman I marry in it is divorced,' and then he married so-and-so, she is divorced by two divorces by the force of the two oaths because she is both so-and-so and a woman. Similarly, if he said, 'If I speak to so-and-so, you are divorced,' and 'If I speak to any human, you are divorced,' and then he spoke to so-and-so, she is divorced by two divorces by the force of the two oaths.
Source pages p. 12

Commentary
This passage from al-Dhakhira covers a situation where a person is subject to two separate oaths, and the same wife falls under both. The first example: a man makes two conditional divorce statements: first, 'If I marry so-and-so (a specific woman), she is divorced'; second, 'Every woman I marry (in a certain context) is divorced.' Then he marries the specific woman. She fulfills both conditions (she is the specific woman and also a woman), so two divorces occur. Similarly, in the second example, a man says to his wife, 'If I speak to so-and-so, you are divorced,' and also 'If I speak to any human, you are divorced.' He then speaks to so-and-so, triggering both oaths, so the wife receives two divorces. The key principle is that if two independent oaths are both applicable to the same act, both take effect, resulting in cumulative divorces.

Key Terms
اليمينين (al-yamīnayn): Two oaths; in this context, two separate conditional divorce declarations
تطليقتين (taṭlīqatayn): Two divorces; the number of divorces that fall due to the two oaths

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A man says to his wife, 'If I enter this house, you are divorced,' and also says, 'If I enter any house, you are divorced.' He enters his own house. How many divorces occur?
A) One divorce
B) Two divorces
C) Three divorces
D) No divorce because the oaths are contradictory

Key Takeaway
Two independent conditional divorce oaths both take effect when the same act satisfies both conditions, resulting in cumulative divorces.

Conditional Divorce to a Non-Wife: Why It Is Ineffective
Ruling with reasoning on conditional divorce to a non-wife

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that a conditional divorce pronounced before marriage does not take effect, and explain the dual rationale of absence of marital relationship (mahal) and lack of deterrence (ikhafa).

Core Idea
A conditional divorce before marriage fails because there is no marital relationship to act upon and no deterrent force to constitute a valid vow.

In this passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq, the commentator explains a specific scenario: a man says to a non-wife, 'If you visit me, you are divorced,' then later marries her. Does the divorce occur when she visits? According to the Hanafi school, it does not. The reasoning involves two fundamental legal principles: a divorce pronouncement requires an existing marriage (mahal), and a vow (yamin) requires a deterrent effect (ikhafa). Both are absent here.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is the pronouncement? What is the ruling? The passage states the divorce does not occur.
The act is a conditional pronouncement made before marriage.
2
Analyze why it is not an actual divorce (iqa')
The pronouncement cannot be an actual divorce because when it was uttered, there was no marriage (mahal).
Mahal is a necessary condition for any divorce pronouncement.
3
Analyze why it is not a valid vow (yamin)
A valid vow requires deterrence (ikhafa) to prevent a sin or compel good. Here, the consequence (divorce) was not connected to the action (visiting) at that time, so no deterrence exists.
Ikhafa is assessed at the time of the pronouncement.
4
Address the counterexample
The text raises an objection: 'If you menstruate, you are divorced' is considered a vow despite no apparent deterrence. The answer is that the general case matters, not a rare exception.
The general case is that menstruation does not deter; the majority of cases determine the ruling.
5
Examine the variant wording
The phrase 'whenever she becomes permissible, she becomes forbidden' is also ineffective because either it is not a proper conditional or it becomes meaningless after marriage (since she becomes a non-wife again).
This reinforces that the pronouncement is legally inert.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (فلو قال لأجنبية إن زرت فأنت طالق فنكحها فزارت لم تطلق) لأنه حين صدر يصح جعله إيقاعاً لعدم المحل ولا يميناً لعدم معنى اليمين وهو ما يكون حاملاً على البر لعل وجهه أن قوله وكلما حلت حرمت ليس بتعليق في الملك ولا مضافاً إليه لأنه لا يلزم من حلها أن يكون بعقد النكاح لجواز أن ترتد ثم تسترق تأمل أو يقال إنه لما تزوجها طلقت ثلاثاً وصارت أجنبية لأنه ينزل الطلاق أولاً فينزل قوله وكلما حلت حرمت بعد أن صارت أجنبية وهو لغو لما قلنا تأمل . لإخافته لأنه لم يصدر مخيفاً لعدم ظهور الجزاء عند الفعل وهو الزيارة هنا لعدم ثبوت المحلية عند وجود الشرط، ومعنى الإخافة هنا لزوم نصف المهر إن تزوجها لأنه حينئذ يقع الطلاق فيجب المال فيمتنع عن التزوج خوفاً من ذلك، وقد أورد على هذا قوله إذا حضت فأنت طالق فإنه يمين مع أنه لا حمل فيه ولا منع وأجيب بأن العبرة فيه للغالب لا للشاذ كذا في فتح القدير.
His statement: (And if he said to a non-wife, 'If you visit me, you are divorced,' then he married her and she visited, she is not divorced) because when the statement was made, it cannot be considered an actual pronouncement (iqa') due to the absence of a marital context (mahal), nor a vow (yamin) because the meaning of a vow, which is to compel oneself to do good, is absent. It is also said that his statement 'and whenever she becomes permissible, she becomes forbidden' is not a conditional in marriage nor attached to it, because her becoming permissible does not necessarily occur through a marriage contract, as she could apostatize then be enslaved. Or it is said that when he married her, she became divorced thrice, becoming a non-wife, so his statement 'whenever she becomes permissible, she becomes forbidden' applies after she is a non-wife, which is meaningless. Because it lacks deterrence (ikhafa), as the consequence (divorce) does not appear at the time of the action (visiting) due to the absence of a marital relationship at the time of the condition. The meaning of deterrence here is the obligation of half the dower if he marries her, because then divorce occurs and wealth is owed, so he refrains from marriage out of fear. An objection was raised: his statement 'If you menstruate, you are divorced' is considered a vow despite having no burden or prevention. The answer is that the general case is considered, not the rare one, as in Fath al-Qadir.
Source pages p. 13

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with reasoning (ta'lil) from the commentary on Kanz al-Daqa'iq. The issue: a man says to a woman who is not his wife, 'If you visit me, you are divorced.' He later marries her, and she visits. Does divorce occur? The answer is no. The reason is twofold. First, when the statement was made, there was no marital relationship (mahal), so it cannot be an actual divorce (iqa'). Second, it is not even a valid vow (yamin) because a vow is meant to deter one from sin or compel good; here there is no deterrence because the penalty (divorce) was not immediately apparent at the time of the condition—the woman was not his wife, so divorce had no effect. However, the commentator notes a potential deterrence: the man might fear that if he marries her, divorce would occur and he would have to pay half the dower, so he might refrain. But this is not the primary meaning. The passage also addresses a variant wording: 'and whenever she becomes permissible, she becomes forbidden.' Two interpretations are given: either it is not a valid conditional because 'becoming permissible' could be through non-marriage (e.g., apostasy and enslavement), or if it is taken as a conditional, then upon marriage she is divorced thrice and becomes a non-wife, making the subsequent condition meaningless. This ruling is from the Hanafi school, as stated in Fath al-Qadir.

Key Terms
الإخافة (al-ikhāfah): Deterrence or fear that prompts one to fulfill a vow; a necessary element for a valid oath.
المحل (al-mahall): The context or subject of a legal act; here, the marital relationship required for divorce to be effective.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) She is divorced immediately upon visiting.
B) The divorce is suspended until the condition is met, then it takes effect.
C) The divorce does not occur because the statement was made before marriage.
D) The man must pay half the dower but no divorce occurs.

Key Takeaway
A conditional divorce pronounced before marriage is legally void under Hanafi law because it lacks both a proper marital context (mahal) and the deterrent effect (ikhafa) required for a valid vow.

The Principle of Attachment in Conditional Divorce (Ta'liq)
Ruling on conditional divorce and manumission tied to meeting in bed or intercourse.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that a conditional divorce (ta'liq) is valid only when attached to a marriage contract (milk al-nikah) and distinguish valid from invalid conditions.

Core Idea
A conditional divorce or manumission is void if not attached to the relevant legal relationship at the time of utterance.

Conditional statements can easily lead to confusion. In Hanafi fiqh, a statement like 'Every woman I share a bed with is divorced' does not trigger divorce upon marriage because the condition was not linked to a specific marriage. This lesson explores the crucial principle of attachment (ta'liq) through several cases from classical texts, helping you distinguish valid from invalid conditional divorces and manumissions.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the threshold question
Ask: Is the condition attached to a valid legal relationship (marriage for talaq, ownership for itq)?
2
Analyze the 'every woman in bed' case
The statement lacks attachment to a specific marriage at the time of utterance. Hence the talaq is not triggered upon marriage.
Al-Muhit confirms the ruling.
3
Analyze the 'every slave-girl I have intercourse with' case
The condition (intercourse) is not linked to ownership of a particular slave. The emancipation does not occur upon purchase and intercourse.
Same principle applies.
4
Distinguish the Walwaljiyya cases
If the statement is made to a non-mahram woman ('If I divorce you, my slave is free'), it is valid because it implicitly anticipates marriage. But saying 'If I divorce you, you are divorced three times' is invalid because divorce cannot be conditioned on itself.
The principle of necessity applies.
5
Formulate the ta'liq rule
A conditional talaq or itq is valid only if the condition is attached to a specific marriage or ownership that exists or can exist in the future in a legally relevant way.
This is a core Hanafi doctrine.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد أورد على هذا قوله إذا حضت فأنت طالق فإنه يمين مع أنه لا حمل فيه ولا منع وأجيب بأن العبرة فيه للغالب لا للشاذ كذا في فتح القدير . وأشار المصنف إلى مسائل : الأولى لو قال كل امرأة أجتمع معها في فراش فهي طالق فتزوج امرأة لا تطلق، ومثله كل جارية أطؤها حرة فاشترى جارية فوطئها لا تعتق لأن العتق لم يضف إلى الملك كأنه كذا في المحيط . وفي الولوالجية : إذا قال الرجل لأجنبية إن طلقتك فعبدي حر يصح ويصير قال إن تزوجتك وطلقتك فعبدي حر، ولو قال لها إن طلقتك فأنت طالق ثلاثاً لا . يصح لأن ذكر الطلاق ذكر النكاح الذي لا يستغنى عنه الطلاق لا ذكر لما يستغنى عنه الجزاء اهـ.
And it was objected to this saying: 'If you menstruate, you are divorced' is an oath, even though it contains neither compulsion nor prevention, and it was answered that consideration is given to the prevalent, not the rare, as in Fath al-Qadir. And the author pointed to issues: the first: if he said 'Every woman with whom I share a bed is divorced', then he marries a woman, she is not divorced. Similarly, if he said 'Every slave-girl I have intercourse with is free', then he buys a slave-girl and has intercourse with her, she is not free, because the emancipation was not added to ownership, as if it were so in al-Muhit. And in al-Walwaljiyya: if a man says to a non-mahram woman 'If I divorce you, my slave is free', it is valid, and it becomes as if he said 'If I marry you and divorce you, my slave is free'. And if he said to her 'If I divorce you, you are divorced three times', it is not valid, because mentioning divorce mentions marriage, which divorce cannot do without, not mentioning that which the penalty can do without. End.
Source pages p. 14

Commentary
This passage begins by addressing an objection raised against the earlier ruling regarding an oath like 'If you menstruate, you are divorced'. The objection is that this does not appear to be an oath (yamin) because it does not compel or prevent an action. The response given is that consideration is given to the general tendency, not the exceptional case. Then the author introduces several issues from the matn. The first issue: If a man says 'Every woman with whom I share a bed is divorced', then later marries a woman, she is not divorced by this statement. Why? Because the divorce was not attached to a marriage (milk al-nikah). The condition 'meeting in bed' does not qualify as a valid condition for divorce since it is not linked to the marriage contract. Similarly, if he says 'Every slave-girl I have intercourse with is free', and later buys a slave-girl and has intercourse with her, she is not freed because the emancipation was not attached to ownership at the time of the oath. The passage then quotes from al-Walwaljiyya a different case: If a man says to a non-related woman 'If I divorce you, my slave is free', it is valid because it implies that he will marry her then divorce her. But if he says 'If I divorce you, you are divorced three times', it is invalid because the condition of divorce (talaq) cannot be mentioned without marriage being understood; divorce presupposes marriage. The reasoning here is based on the principle that a conditional statement (ta'liq) must be attached to a valid cause.

Key Terms
تعليق (ta'liq): Conditional phrasing; suspending the occurrence of a legal effect (like divorce) upon the occurrence of a future condition.
يمين (yamin): An oath; a statement invoking a condition that would trigger a consequence, often used in the context of conditional divorce (yamin bi'l-talaq).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The woman is divorced immediately.
B) The woman is not divorced because the condition was not attached to marriage.
C) The divorce occurs only if he intends it.
D) The divorce occurs but can be revoked.

Key Takeaway
A conditional divorce or manumission is void if the condition is not attached to a marriage or ownership at the time the statement is made.

Conditional Divorce with Sequence: The Hanafi Ikhtilaf
Third mas'alah on conditional divorce with sequential marriage and intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two Hanafi positions on whether the second woman is divorced in a conditional divorce with sequential marriage and explain the reasoning behind each position.

Core Idea
A conditional divorce only divorces the woman whose marriage is directly referenced; the second woman's divorce is disputed.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra’iq presents a nuanced conditional divorce scenario involving two women linked by sequence. The ruling on the first woman is clear, but the status of the second woman generates an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf. Understanding this ikhtilaf trains you to parse conditional statements with precision.

Guided Path
1
Parse the Conditional Statement
Analyze the husband's utterance: 'If I marry Zaynab before Amra, both are divorced.' Identify the key elements: the marriage acts (Zaynab and Amra) and the result (divorce for both).
The condition is the marriage of Zaynab before Amra; the divorce is the consequence.
2
Apply to the First Marriage
When the husband marries Zaynab, the condition is met: he married her before Amra. According to all Hanafis, Zaynab is divorced immediately upon marriage.
The divorce does not relate back to the oath; it occurs at the moment the condition is fulfilled.
3
Examine the Second Marriage
The second woman, Amra, is mentioned in the outcome ('both are divorced') but her marriage is not the trigger. The condition only directly references the marriage of the first woman. There is an ikhtilaf on whether this triggers her divorce.
Al-Muhit holds that she is also divorced. Another view says she is not, because her marriage is not explicitly mentioned in the condition.
4
Analyze the Time-Limited Example
Consider the variant: 'If I marry Zaynab before Amra by a month, both are divorced.' If he marries Zaynab, then Amra after a month, Zaynab is divorced immediately. Amra is not divorced because her divorce was not attached to her own marriage—only to the sequence.
This example clarifies that the second woman's divorce is not automatic; it supports the view that she is not divorced.
5
Identify the Thamarah al-Khilaf
The practical difference: if a mufti follows al-Muhit, the second woman is divorced upon her marriage; if he follows the alternative view, she remains married.
The text does not give a definitive tarjih, so a mufti must weigh the evidence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالثة لو قال إن تزوجت فلانة قبل فلانة فهما طالقان فتزوج الأولى طلقت، واختلفوا فيما إذا تزوج الثانية فقال في المحيط : تطلق أيضاً . وقيل : ينبغي أن لا تطلق لأن نكاح الثانية غير مذكور صريحاً ولا ضرورة. ولو قال إن تزوجت زينب قبل عمرة بشهر فهما طالقتان فتزوج زينب ثم عمرة بعدها بشهر طلقت زينب للحال لوجود الشرط ولا يستند ولا تطلق عمرة لأنه ما أضاف طلاقها إلى نكاحها لأن تزوجها لم يصر مذكوراً وتمامه في المحيط .
The third: if he said 'If I marry Zaynab before Amra, both are divorced', then he marries the first, she is divorced. They differed regarding when he marries the second. In al-Muhit: she is also divorced. And it is said that she should not be divorced, because the marriage of the second is not explicitly mentioned nor necessary. And if he said 'If I marry Zaynab before Amra by a month, both are divorced', then he marries Zaynab, then Amra after a month, Zaynab is divorced immediately due to the existence of the condition, and it does not relate back, and Amra is not divorced because he did not attach her divorce to her marriage, as her marriage was not mentioned, and its completion is in al-Muhit.
Source pages p. 14

Commentary
This lesson discusses a conditional divorce where the husband links two women in a sequence. If he says 'If I marry Zaynab before Amra, both are divorced', and he marries the first (Zaynab), she is divorced. There is disagreement about the second (Amra). According to al-Muhit, she is also divorced. But another view says she is not divorced because her marriage was not explicitly stated in the condition—only the sequence was mentioned, and her marriage is the later event, but the condition only directly references the marriage of the earlier woman. The more detailed example with a time limit ('by a month') clarifies: if he marries Zaynab and then Amra after a month, Zaynab is divorced immediately (because the condition is met: he married Zaynab before Amra). The divorce of Zaynab occurs immediately upon marriage, and it does not relate back to the oath. Amra is not divorced because her marriage is not the trigger; the condition was about marrying Zaynab before Amra, so the divorce applies only to Zaynab. The reasoning: the husband did not attach the divorce to Amra's marriage directly; her marriage is only a measure of time. This ikhtilaf shows a nuance in interpreting conditional phrases.

Key Terms
شرط (shart): Condition; the event upon which the divorce is contingent.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) Zaynab is divorced, but Amra is not.
C) Amra is divorced, but Zaynab is not.
D) Neither is divorced.

Key Takeaway
In conditional divorce with sequential marriage, the second woman's divorce is disputed within the Hanafi school based on whether her marriage is explicitly referenced.

Conditional Divorce by Parents' Marriage: The Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Command
Intra-Hanafi dispute on conditional divorce with parents' marriage.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the intra-Hanafi dispute on the validity of a conditional divorce when parents marry on the husband's command, identify the reasoning of each position, and apply the preferred view (Muhammad ibn al-Fadl).

Core Idea
A conditional divorce is valid only when attached to a valid marriage contract; the dispute hinges on whether the husband's command makes the marriage his own.

In this lesson, we examine a nuanced scenario: a man tells his parents, 'If you marry me a woman, she is divorced three times.' What happens if the parents marry him with his command? The Hanafi tradition records an internal disagreement. The general view (from al-Muhit and al-Mi'raj) holds that the condition is invalid and no divorce occurs. However, Imam Abu Bakr Muhammad ibn al-Fadl argues that the condition is valid and divorce does occur, interpreting the condition as 'If I own a woman through your marriage.' This lesson unpacks the reasoning behind each view and identifies the preferred position.

Guided Path
1
State the general Hanafi position
Identify the ruling given initially: the conditional divorce is invalid and the wife is not divorced, regardless of whether the parents acted with or without the husband's command.
This is the view recorded in al-Muhit and al-Mi'raj.
2
Identify the opposing view within the school
Recognise that Imam Abu Bakr Muhammad ibn al-Fadl holds a different opinion: if the parents marry with the husband's command, the divorce does occur because the condition is valid.
The text indicates this view is considered correct (wa huwa al-sahih).
3
Trace the reasoning of each position
The general view argues that the ta'liq is not attached to milk al-nikah because the parents' marriage without command is invalid, and even with command it is not the husband's own contract. Muhammad ibn al-Fadl interprets the condition as 'If I own a woman through your marriage,' which is a valid condition attached to ownership.
Al-Maqdisi's critique highlights the hermeneutic shift.
4
Analyse the preference (tarjih)
The text explicitly states, 'wa huwa al-sahih' (this is correct), preferring Muhammad ibn al-Fadl's view. This suggests that the condition is effectively about acquiring ownership, which the husband does when the parents marry with his command.
The tarjih is based on legal consequence (milk al-nikah) rather than literal wording.
5
Determine the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
According to the preferred view, if the parents marry him with his command, the wife is immediately divorced upon the marriage contract. According to the general view, she remains married.
This difference affects the wife's marital status and rights.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثانية لو قال لوالديه إن زوجتماني امرأة فهي طالق ثلاثاً فزوجاه امرأة بغير أمره لا تطلق لأن التعليق لم يصح لأنه غير مضاف إلى ملك النكاح لأن تزويج الوالدين له بغير أمره غير صحيح لأنه غير مضاف إلى ملك النكاح لأنه لم يأمرهما بالتزويج عند التعليق كذا في المحيط . ولا فرق في حق هذا الحكم بين أن يزوجاه بأمره أو بغير أمره لما في المعراج : ولو قال لغيره إن زوجتني امرأة فهي طالق فزوجه بامره أو بغير أمره لا تطلق لأن التعليق لم يصح .اهـ. قوله : غير صحيح لأنه غير مضاف إلى ملك النكاح، هذا التعليل غير ظاهر وكأنه تكرار من الناسخ بل التعليل قوله « لأنه لم يأمرهما الخ» تأمل قوله : (لا تطلق لأن التعليق لم يصح) قال المقدسي : يخالف ظاهر ما في الفتح وقد كنت بحثت فيه بأنه ينبغي أن يقع إذا زوجه بأمره لأن التزويج إذا علق به الطلاق يراد به المسبب عنه وهو الملك فكأنه قال : إن ملكت امرأة بتزويجك فهي طالق وهو صحيح. فإذا وقع يقع طلق المعلق به . وقد وجدت بحثي منقولاً صحيحاً في التتارخانية عن الخانية بعد نقل المسألة فلينظر .اهـ. قلت وعبارة التتارخانية عن الخانية : ولو قال لوالديه إن زوجتماني امرأة فهي طالق فزوجاه امرأة بأمره :قالوا لا تصح هذه اليمين ولا تطلق . وقال الشيخ الإمام أبو بكر محمد بن الفضل : يصح وتطلق وهو الصحيح.
The second: if he said to his parents 'If you marry me a woman, she is divorced three times', and they married him a woman without his command, she is not divorced because the conditional statement is not valid, as it is not attached to the marriage contract, because the parents' marriage for him without his command is not valid since it is not attached to the marriage contract because he did not order them to marry at the time of the conditional statement, as in al-Muhit. And there is no difference in this ruling between their marrying him with his command or without his command, as in al-Mi'raj: if he said to someone else 'If you marry me a woman, she is divorced', and he marries him with his command or without, she is not divorced because the conditional statement is not valid, end. His saying: 'invalid because it is not attached to the marriage contract'—this reasoning is not apparent and seems to be a repetition from the copyist; rather, the reasoning is his saying 'because he did not order them', etc. Consider his saying: 'she is not divorced because the conditional statement is not valid'. Al-Maqdisi said: This contradicts the apparent meaning in al-Fath, and I had researched that it should occur if he marries him with his command, because when divorce is attached to marriage, the intended meaning is the legal consequence, which is ownership, so it is as if he said 'If I own a woman through your marriage, she is divorced', and that is valid. So if it occurs, the attached divorce falls. And I found my research correctly quoted in al-Tatarkhaniyya from al-Khaniyya after the transmission of the issue, so consider. End. I said: and the expression in al-Tatarkhaniyya from al-Khaniyya: and if he said [then the note:] Regarding 'if you marry me a woman, she is divorced', and they married him a woman with his command, they said: this oath is not valid and she is not divorced. And the Imam Abu Bakr Muhammad ibn al-Fadl said: it is valid and she is divorced, and this is the correct view.
Source pages p. 14

Commentary
This lesson addresses a common scenario: a man tells his parents, 'If you marry me a woman, she is divorced three times.' If the parents then marry him a woman without his command, the divorce does not occur because the conditional statement is invalid. The reason is that the divorce was not attached to a valid marriage contract, since the parents' action without his command is not a valid marriage. Interestingly, the text initially states that there is no difference between the parents acting with or without his command—both result in no divorce. However, a commentary by al-Maqdisi challenges this, citing a different opinion from al-Fath that suggests if he ordered them, the divorce should occur because the condition is effectively 'If I own a woman through your marriage' (milk al-nikah), which is a valid attachment. The later note records a dispute: the general view is that the oath is invalid, but Imam Abu Bakr Muhammad ibn al-Fadl holds it is valid and the divorce occurs, which is considered the correct position (al-sahih). This shows that within the Hanafi school, there is ikhtilaf on whether the command of the husband is necessary for the validity of the conditional marriage.

Key Terms
ملك النكاح (milk al-nikah): Ownership of the marriage bond; the husband's right over the wife through the marriage contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the preferred view (Muhammad ibn al-Fadl), what is the ruling if a man tells his parents 'If you marry me a woman, she is divorced' and they marry him with his command?
A) The divorce does not occur because the condition is invalid.
B) The divorce occurs immediately upon marriage.
C) The divorce occurs only if the parents acted without command.
D) The oath is completely void.

Key Takeaway
The intra-Hanafi dispute reveals that the validity of a conditional divorce depends on whether the condition is attached to the husband's own milk al-nikah; the preferred view holds that the husband's command makes the marriage his own, thus the divorce is effective.

Conditional Divorce with 'Or': One Oath or Two?
Ruling on conditional divorce with 'or' and command to marry

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between a single oath and separate oaths in conditional divorce using 'or', and apply the Hanafi ruling when one condition is fulfilled without immediate penalty.

Core Idea
A conditional divorce with 'or' dissolves upon the first possible action without immediate divorce if that action cannot legally trigger it.

This lesson tackles a tricky scenario: a man swears a conditional divorce with two alternatives linked by 'or', like 'If I marry or propose...'. The legal outcome depends on whether the oath is one or two, and which action occurs first. You'll learn the Hanafi position and the intra-madhhab dispute over the validity of the oath when one condition seems impossible to fulfill as a penalt.

Guided Path
1
Identify the structure of the conditional statement
Determine whether the wording indicates one oath with two conditions joined by 'or', or two separate oaths. This determines whether the violation occurs at the first or both conditions.
If separate oaths, each is independent; if one oath, the condition is fulfilled by either action.
2
Apply the analogy from Abu Yusuf's ruling
Consider the case of 'If I propose to so-and-so or marry her' — proposal fulfills the condition but does not trigger divorce because she is not his wife; the oath dissolves without penalty.
The concept of 'hanth' (violation) here means the oath is broken, but the penalty (talaq) does not occur.
3
Distinguish the command scenario
If the condition is 'If I marry or order someone to marry me', and he orders without marrying, the same principle applies: the order is the triggering event, but since the woman is not yet his wife, no immediate divorce.
The order counts as a violation of the oath, dissolving it, so later marriage does not cause divorce.
4
Analyze the refutation of the invalid view
Some scholars argue the oath is invalid because one alternative (proposing) can never lead to divorce. The Hanafi response: the oath is valid, and the benefit is shown when an officious person marries him and he ratifies — then divorce occurs.
This confirms the oath's validity and the importance of timing.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
According to the correct view, the oath is valid and breaks upon the first possible action; according to the opposing view, it is void. The practical consequence: if the man later marries after proposing, the correct view says no divorce; the opposing view would consider the oath null, so no effect either way, but the ruling differs in other scenarios.
The mufta bihi is validity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابعة لو قال إن تزوجت امرأة أو أمرت إنساناً بالتزوج لي امرأة فهي طالق ثم أمر غيره أن يزوجه امرأة ففعل المأمور لا تطلق امرأة الحالف لأن حنث بالأمر لا إلى جزاء وهو نظير ما روي عن أبي يوسف : لو قال رجل إن تزوجت فلانة أو خطبتها فهي طالق فخطب امرأة وتزوجها لا يحنث في يمينه لأنه حنث بالخطبة كذا في الخانية. وحاصل ما ذكره في الذخيرة أنه إذا قال إن تزوجت فلانة فهي طالق وإن أمرت من يزوجنيها فهي طالق فأمر إنساناً فزوجها منه طلقت لأنهما يمينان فانحلال أحدهما لا يجوب انحلال الأخرى. ولو قال إن تزوجت وإن أمرت من يزوجنيها تطليقة تصرف إلى أيهما شاء فهي طالق فأمر رجلاً فزوجها منه لم تطلق لأن اليمين واحدة والشرط شيان الأمر والتزويج، فبمجرد الأمر لا تنحل اليمين ولذا لو تزوجها من غير أن يأمر أحداً بذلك لا تطلق لأنه بعض الشرط، فإن أمر بعد ذلك رجلاً فقال زوجني فلانة وهي امرأته على حالها طلقت لأنه كل الشرط. ولو قال إن خطبت فلانة أو تزوجتها فهي طالق فخطبها ثم تزوجها لا تطلق لأن شرط حنثه أحد شيئين، فإذا خطبها فقد وجد شرط الحنث والمرأة ليست في نكاحه فانحلت اليمين لا إلى حنث، فإذا تزوجها بعد ذلك واليمين منحلة فلا تطلق . وقوله لأنه حنث بالخطبة يدل على أنها يمين منعقدة وفائدتها لو زوجه فضولي فبلغه فأجاز طلقت ونظيرها إن تزوجت فلانة أو أمرت من يزوجنيها فأمر غيره فزوجها منه لا تطلق وتمامه فيها من فصل التعليقات. وفي تتمة الفتاوى في مسألتي الأمر والخطبة باو وهذا رد على من يقول اليمين غير منعقدة لأن الشرط أحدهما وأحدهما بعينه صالح والآخر لا فإنه نص على الحنث حتى لو تزوج قبل الأمر في المسألة الأولى أو قبل الخطبة في المسألة الثانية لو تصور فإنها تطلق اهـ.
The fourth: if he said 'If I marry a woman or order a person to marry a woman for me, she is divorced', then he ordered someone else to marry him a woman and the agent did so, the woman of the oath-taker is not divorced because he violated the oath by the order, which is not a penalty. This is similar to what is reported from Abu Yusuf: if a man said 'If I marry so-and-so or propose to her, she is divorced', then he proposed to a woman and married her, he does not break his oath because he broke it by the proposal, as in al-Khaniyya. The summary of what is in al-Dhakhira is that if he said 'If I marry so-and-so, she is divorced, and if I order someone to marry me her, she is divorced', then he ordered a person and he married her to him, she is divorced because they are two oaths, and the dissolution of one does not necessitate the dissolution of the other. And if he said 'If I marry and if I order someone to marry me' [with the intent of] one divorce, it is applied to whichever he wishes. So she is divorced, so he ordered a man and he married her from him, she is not divorced because the oath is one and the condition is two things: the order and the marriage. By mere order, the oath is not dissolved. Similarly, if he married her without ordering anyone, she is not divorced because it is part of the condition. If he later ordered a man saying 'Marry me so-and-so' while she is still his wife, she is divorced because it is the whole condition. And if he said 'If I propose to so-and-so or marry her, she is divorced', then he proposed to her then married her, she is not divorced because the condition of his violation is one of two things. When he proposed, the condition of violation occurred, and the woman is not in his marriage, so the oath is dissolved without a penalty. When he later married her, the oath is already dissolved, so she is not divorced. And his statement 'because he violated by the proposal' indicates that it is a valid oath, and its benefit is if the officious person marries him and he ratifies it, she is divorced. And its analogy: if he said 'If I marry so-and-so or order someone to marry me her', then he ordered someone else and he married her from him, she is not divorced. And its completion is in the section on conditional statements. In Tatimma al-Fatawa, regarding the two issues of command and proposal with 'or', it is a refutation of those who say the oath is not valid because the condition is one of two, and one of them is valid and the other is not. For he explicitly states the violation, so even if he marries before the order in the first issue or before the proposal in the second issue, if conceivable, she would be divorced. End.
Source pages p. 14

Commentary
This lesson deals with a complex scenario where a man makes a conditional divorce that includes two alternative actions: marrying a woman himself or ordering someone to marry him a woman. The ruling distinguishes between one oath and separate oaths. If the condition is phrased as one oath with 'or', then the fulfillment of only one part (e.g., just ordering) does not trigger divorce because the oath is not dissolved until the condition is fully met? Actually, the text explains: if he says 'If I marry or order...' and he orders someone and they marry him, she is not divorced because breaking the oath by the order is not a penalty (i.e., the divorce does not occur until both are fulfilled? Wait, the text says he breaks the oath by the order, but since the condition is 'or', the oath is broken upon the first occurrence? Let's parse carefully. The first ruling: if he says 'If I marry or order...', and he orders someone and they perform, his wife is not divorced because 'he broke the oath by the order, which is not a penalty.' The phrase 'حنث بالأمر لا إلى جزاء' means he violates the oath by the order, but not to a penalty (i.e., the divorce is not triggered?). Actually this is a bit confusing. The analogy with Abu Yusuf's case: if he says 'If I marry or propose...', and he proposes, then later marries, he does not break his oath because he already broke it by the proposal? The text says 'لا يحنث في يمينه لأنه حنث بالخطبة' meaning he does not break his oath (for the second action) because he already broke it by the proposal. So the first occurrence (proposal) triggers the penalty (divorce) but since she is not yet his wife, the divorce does not take effect immediately? The benefit is that later if he marries her, she is not divorced because the oath is already dissolved. So in the first example, ordering is analogous to the proposal; it dissolves the oath without immediate penalty, so the later marriage does not trigger divorce. However, if he makes two separate oaths, one for marriage and one for order, then each is independent. If he orders and she is married, the order oath is fulfilled but the marriage is separate. The text then discusses if he says 'If I marry and if I order...' with one talaq (one divorce penalty), then it applies to whichever he wishes. The later portion elaborates on the 'one oath' scenario: the condition has two parts (order and marriage), both must be fulfilled for the oath to be broken. So just ordering is not enough. But if he later orders and marries (from the same agent), it fulfills the condition. The passage ends with a refutation of those who say the oath is invalid because one part is valid and the other not; the author argues that it is valid and a violation occurs so even if the order comes before the marriage, it counts. The key is the precise wording and whether the oath is one or two.

Key Terms
حنث (hanth): Violation of an oath; breaking the condition of a yamin.
فضولي (fuduli): An officious person; someone who acts without authorization, especially in concluding a contract that requires ratification.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) She is divorced upon marriage.
B) She is divorced upon proposal.
C) She is not divorced because the oath was invalid.
D) She is not divorced because the oath was already dissolved by the proposal.

Key Takeaway
A conditional divorce with 'or' is valid even if one alternative cannot immediately result in divorce; the oath dissolves upon the first action that occurs.

Intention and Generality in Conditional Divorce Oaths
Intra-Hanafi dispute on intention and future-born inclusion in divorce oaths.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the admissibility of intention in general oaths and distinguish between the positions of Abu Hanifa/Abu Yusuf and Imam Muhammad regarding inclusion of future-born individuals in conditional divorce oaths.

Core Idea
Private intention cannot limit a general oath in court; inclusion of future-born depends on the oath's motivation.

Divorce oaths often involve conditions and general wording. This lesson examines two key issues: whether a husband's private intention can restrict a broadly worded oath in court, and whether children born after the oath are included when the oath references a specific person's offspring. The Hanafi school presents an internal disagreement on the latter, resolved by analyzing the motivating factor.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two legal issues
First, the role of intention (niyya) in general oaths: can a specific intended meaning limit the literal generality? Second, whether future-born individuals are included when the oath uses 'daughters of so-and-so'.
These are distinct but related through the principle of interpretation.
2
Apply the rule on intention
Recall that in zahir al-riwaya, a general expression is taken literally in court (qada'); private intention is inadmissible. Verify this with the example of 'every woman I marry'.
Intention may matter in conscience but not in judicial ruling.
3
Analyze the future-born dispute
Compare the analogy from Abu Hanifa/Abu Yusuf (including future children based on the 'son of so-and-so' case) with Muhammad's distinction based on motivation (anger vs. general trait).
The key is whether the oath is personal or impersonal.
4
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: if a man marries a later-born daughter, does divorce occur? Under Abu Hanifa/Abu Yusuf, yes; under Muhammad, no.
Both views are valid within the school, though no single preference is stated here.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي الخانية : قال كل امرأة أتزوجها فهي طالق ونوى من بلد كذا أو نوى امرأة حبشية أو غيرها لا يكون مصدقاً في ظاهر الرواية قضاء ولو قال أي امرأة أتزوجها فهي طالق كانت على امرأة واحدة إلا أن ينوي جميع النساء. ولو قال إن تزوجت امرأة من بنات فلان فهي طالق وليبي لفلان بنت ثم ولد له بنت فتزوجها الحالف قالوا لا يحنث في يمينه ويشترط قيام البنت وقت اليمين ولا يدخل في اليمين ما يحدث بعد اليمين كما لو حلف أن لا يتزوج من أهل هذه الدار وليس لتلك الدار أهل ثم سكنها قوم فتزوج الحالف امرأة لا يحنث في يمينه ويشترط وجود الأهل عند اليمين إلا أن هذا الجواب يوافق قول محمد، وأما قياس قول أبي حنيفة وأبي يوسف يدخل في هذا اليمين من كان موجوداً وقت اليمين ومن يحدث بعده كمن حلف أن لا يكلم ابن فلان وليس لفلان ابن ثم ولد له ابن فكلمه الحالف حنث في قول أبي حنيفة وأبي يوسف ولا يحنث في قول محمد ولو قال والله لا أتزوج امرأة من أهل الكوفة فتزوج امرأة من أهل الكوفة ولدت بعد اليمين حنث، فرق محمد بين هذا وبين بنت فلان لأن أهل الكوفة قوم لا يحصون فلم يكن الحامل على اليمين غيظ لحقه من جهة الأهل بل الحامل على اليمين معنى في الكوفة فيدخل الموجود والحادث. بخلاف بنت فلان لأن الحامل على اليمين غيظ لحفه من جهة فلان فيدخل فيه الموجود لا الحادث.
And in al-Khaniyya: He said 'Every woman I marry is divorced' and intended [a woman] from a certain town or intended an Ethiopian woman or other, he is not believed in apparent ruling (zahir al-riwaya) judicially. And if he said 'Any woman I marry is divorced', it applies to one woman unless he intends all women. And if he said 'If I marry a woman from the daughters of so-and-so, she is divorced', and so-and-so had a daughter, then later a daughter was born to him, and the oath-taker married her, they said: he does not violate his oath, and the existence of the daughter at the time of the oath is required, and what occurs after the oath is not included in the oath, just as if he swore not to marry from the inhabitants of this house and that house had no inhabitants, then people settled in it, and the oath-taker married a woman, he does not violate his oath, and the existence of the inhabitants at the time of the oath is required, except that this answer agrees with the saying of Muhammad. As for the analogy of the saying of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, it includes in this oath those who existed at the time of the oath and those who come after, like one who swore not to speak to the son of so-and-so, and so-and-so had no son, then a son was born to him, and the oath-taker spoke to him, he violates in the saying of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, and does not violate in the saying of Muhammad. And if he said 'By Allah, I will not marry a woman from the people of Kufa', then he married a woman from the people of Kufa who was born after the oath, he violates. Muhammad distinguished between this and the daughter of so-and-so because the people of Kufa are countless, so the motivation for the oath was not anger from the people, rather the motivation was a meaning in Kufa, so it includes the existing and the later. Unlike the daughter of so-and-so because the motivation was anger from so-and-so, so it includes the existing only, not the later.
Source pages p. 15

Commentary
This lesson covers two important points. First, the role of intention (niyya) in general oaths: If a man says 'Every woman I marry is divorced' but internally intends only a specific type (e.g., from a certain town), his intention is not accepted in court (qada'), based on the apparent narrations (zahir al-riwaya). He is considered to have made a general oath applying to all women, unless he explicitly says 'Any woman...' which is interpreted as one woman unless he intends all. Second, the issue of whether future-born individuals are included in an oath. The example: a man swears 'If I marry a daughter of so-and-so, she is divorced.' At the time of the oath, so-and-so has a daughter, but later another daughter is born. If he marries the later-born daughter, according to Imam Muhammad, he does not break the oath because the oath only applies to those existing at the time. However, Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf analogize this to the case of swearing not to speak to the son of so-and-so: if a son is born later, speaking to him does break the oath. So they would include the later-born daughter. Imam Muhammad distinguishes based on the motivation: if the oath stems from anger toward a specific person (like so-and-so), it only covers existing individuals. If it stems from a general trait (like being from Kufa), it covers future individuals as well. The text notes that Muhammad's view is applied in the first example.

Key Terms
ظاهر الرواية (zahir al-riwaya): The apparent and well-known transmission; the established ruling in the school.
قضاء (qada'): Judicially; in a court of law, as opposed to fi al-batin (in conscience).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the judicial ruling (qada') if a man says 'Every woman I marry is divorced' but intended only Ethiopian women?
A) He is believed; the oath applies only to Ethiopian women.
B) He is not believed; the oath applies to all women.
C) The oath is invalid due to conflicting intention.
D) The oath is suspended until his intention is verified.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, a general oath is interpreted literally in court regardless of private intention; and whether future-born children are included depends on the oath's motivation—personal anger excludes them, while a general trait includes them.

Oath with Two Conditions and Dissolution Without Breach
Explains that fulfilling one condition dissolves the oath without breach, so no divorce upon later marriage.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that an oath with two conditions dissolves upon fulfillment of the first condition without triggering divorce, and identify when such an oath is valid.

Core Idea
Fulfilling one of two conditions dissolves the oath; later fulfillment of the other does not cause divorce.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq addresses a nuanced scenario in conditional divorce oaths. A man makes a single oath with two sequential conditions. The text clarifies that meeting the first condition ends the oath's effect, so the second condition, even if met later, does not trigger divorce. This illustrates the Hanafi principle that an oath is a single entity and cannot be broken twice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the oath and its conditions
Read the oath: 'She is divorced' with two conditions: commanding a man and marriage. Understand that the oath is one, not two separate oaths.
2
Understand the principle: one oath cannot be broken twice
The Hanafi rule: a single oath dissolves upon partial fulfillment of its conditions. Only full fulfillment of the entire condition triggers breach (hanth).
3
Apply the distinction between dissolution and breach
Fulfilling the first condition (command) dissolves the oath (inhlal) without breach (hanth). Therefore, later marriage does not cause divorce.
4
Analyze the refutation of invalidity
Some argue the oath is invalid because one condition is impossible. The text refutes this by showing the oath is valid and its benefit appears in cases like a marriage broker (fuduli).
5
Note the thamarah (practical difference)
If the oath were invalid, no effect. But it is valid: if a broker marries without command and the husband approves, divorce occurs; if the command alone is given, no divorce upon later marriage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الطلاق باب التعليق فهي طالق فأمر رجلاً فزوجها منه لم تطلق لأن اليمين واحدة والشرط شيان الأمر والتزويج، فبمجرد الأمر لا تنحل اليمين ولذا لو تزوجها من غير أن يأمر أحداً بذلك لا تطلق لأنه بعض الشرط، فإن أمر بعد ذلك رجلاً فقال زوجني فلانة وهي امرأته على حالها طلقت لأنه كل الشرط. ولو قال إن خطبت فلانة أو تزوجتها فهي طالق فخطبها ثم تزوجها لا تطلق لأن شرط حنثه أحد شيئين، فإذا خطبها فقد وجد شرط الحنث والمرأة ليست في نكاحه فانحلت اليمين لا إلى حنث، فإذا تزوجها بعد ذلك واليمين منحلة فلا تطلق . وقوله لأنه حنث بالخطبة يدل على أنها يمين منعقدة وفائدتها لو زوجه فضولي فبلغه فأجاز طلقت ونظيرها إن تزوجت فلانة أو أمرت من يزوجنيها فأمر غيره فزوجها منه لا تطلق وتمامه فيها من فصل التعليقات. وفي تتمة الفتاوى في مسألتي الأمر والخطبة باو وهذا رد على من يقول اليمين غير منعقدة لأن الشرط أحدهما وأحدهما بعينه صالح والآخر لا فإنه نص على الحنث حتى لو تزوج قبل الأمر في المسألة الأولى أو قبل الخطبة في المسألة الثانية لو تصور فإنها تطلق اهـ.
The Book of Divorce, Chapter of Suspension: 'She is divorced' and he then commanded a man and that man married her to him? She is not divorced because the oath is one and the condition is two things: the command and the marriage. So by the mere command the oath is not dissolved; hence if he married her without commanding anyone to do so, she is not divorced because it is part of the condition. If he then commanded a man saying 'Marry so-and-so to me' while she is still his wife, she is divorced because it is the complete condition. And if he said 'If I betroth so-and-so or marry her, she is divorced', and he betrothed her then married her, she is not divorced because the condition of his breaking the oath is one of two things. When he betrothed her, the condition of breaking the oath occurred while the woman is not in his marriage; so the oath dissolves without breach. Then when he marries her after that, with the oath dissolved, she is not divorced. His statement 'because he broke the oath by betrothal' indicates that this is a valid oath; its benefit is that if a marriage broker marries her to him and he becomes aware and accepts, she is divorced. And its analogue: 'If I marry so-and-so or command someone to marry me to her', and he commands another who marries her to him, she is not divorced. Its completion is in the chapter of suspensions. And in Tatimmat al-Fatawa regarding the two issues of command and betrothal: 'Bao'? and this is a refutation of those who say the oath is invalid because the condition is one of them, and one of them is specifically valid and the other is not. For he has stated clearly that breaking occurs even if he married before the command in the first issue or before the betrothal in the second issue, if that is conceivable; then she is divorced. End.
Source pages p. 15

Commentary
This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq presents two related rulings about oaths with two conditions. The author explains that if a man says 'She is divorced' (as an oath) and specifies two conditions, e.g., 'commanding someone to marry her' and 'the actual marriage', the oath is single and cannot be broken twice. If only one condition (the command) is fulfilled, the oath dissolves without breach (hanth), so later marriage does not trigger divorce. Similarly, if the oath says 'If I betroth or marry her', betrothal alone dissolves the oath, so subsequent marriage is not a breach. The passage then refutes a claim that such oaths are invalid; rather, they are valid, and the benefit is shown in cases like a marriage broker (fuduli) acting without prior command—if the man later accepts, divorce occurs. This ruling is a foundational principle in Hanafi ta'liq law.

Key Terms
اليمين (al-yamin): An oath, here specifically a conditional divorce oath (yamin bi al-talaq).
شرط الحنث (shart al-hanth): The condition whose occurrence breaks the oath.
انحلت اليمين (inhallat al-yamin): The oath dissolves without breach (hanth).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A man says to his wife, 'If I command someone to marry you to me and you marry me, you are divorced.' He commands a man but does not yet marry her. What is the ruling?
A) She is divorced immediately.
B) The oath dissolves without divorce, and if he later marries her, she is not divorced.
C) The oath is invalid.
D) He must perform kaffarah.

Key Takeaway
When a single oath contains two conditions, fulfillment of the first dissolves the oath without trigger divorce, and only the completion of both conditions (if cumulative) or the occurrence of any one condition (if alternative) can cause divorce—but once dissolved, later actions are irrelevant.
