Intra-Hanafi Dispute: Including Future Women in Marriage Oaths
Intra-Hanafi dispute on scope of oaths with groups.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the positions of Abu Hanifa/Abu Yusuf and Muhammad regarding inclusion of future-born women in marriage oaths and apply the criterion of motive (hamil) to determine the scope.

Core Idea
The scope of an oath on a group depends on motive: if against a specific person, only existing members; if against an attribute, all members past and future.

This passage from Al-Khaniyya presents a classic Hanafi internal dispute on whether women born after an oath are included when the oath mentions daughters of a specific person. The key is the concept of hamil ala al-yamin (motive). By analyzing the different scenarios, you will learn to apply this principle to determine the scope of oaths.

Guided Path
1
State the majority Hanafi position
Identify the ruling of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf: that oaths on groups include both existing and future members, based on analogy with an oath not to speak to a son who does not yet exist.
This is the default in general groups like 'people of Kufa'.
2
Identify Imam Muhammad's view
Muhammad holds that only existing individuals are included when the group is limited and the motive is anger at a specific person, like 'daughters of so-and-so'.
His proof is the motive (ghayz) behind the oath.
3
Examine the analogy (qiyas) used by Abu Hanifa
Abu Hanifa analogizes to swearing not to speak to a non-existent son: if a son is later born, speaking breaks the oath. This shows future persons are included.
Muhammad rejects this analogy for limited groups.
4
Apply Muhammad's distinction
Muhammad distinguishes between countless groups (like Kufans) where the motive is the attribute, and limited groups (like a man's daughters) where the motive is anger at the individual.
In countless groups, future members are included; in limited, they are not.
5
Determine the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
If a man swears not to marry a woman from the daughters of Zayd, and Zayd has a daughter after the oath, marrying her breaks the oath per Abu Hanifa but not per Muhammad.
This shows how a single fact pattern yields different rulings.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي الخانية : قال كل امرأة أتزوجها فهي طالق ونوى من بلد كذا أو نوى امرأة حبشية أو غيرها لا يكون مصدقاً في ظاهر الرواية قضاء ولو قال أي امرأة أتزوجها فهي طالق كانت على امرأة واحدة إلا أن ينوي جميع النساء. ولو قال إن تزوجت امرأة من بنات فلان فهي طالق وليبي لفلان بنت ثم ولد له بنت فتزوجها الحالف قالوا لا يحنث في يمينه ويشترط قيام البنت وقت اليمين ولا يدخل في اليمين ما يحدث بعد اليمين كما لو حلف أن لا يتزوج من أهل هذه الدار وليس لتلك الدار أهل ثم سكنها قوم فتزوج الحالف امرأة لا يحنث في يمينه ويشترط وجود الأهل عند اليمين إلا أن هذا الجواب يوافق قول محمد، وأما قياس قول أبي حنيفة وأبي يوسف يدخل في هذا اليمين من كان موجوداً وقت اليمين ومن يحدث بعده كمن حلف أن لا يكلم ابن فلان وليس لفلان ابن ثم ولد له ابن فكلمه الحالف حنث في قول أبي حنيفة وأبي يوسف ولا يحنث في قول محمد ولو قال والله لا أتزوج امرأة من أهل الكوفة فتزوج امرأة من أهل الكوفة ولدت بعد اليمين حنث، فرق محمد بين هذا وبين بنت فلان لأن أهل الكوفة قوم لا يحصون فلم يكن الحامل على اليمين غيظ لحقه من جهة الأهل بل الحامل على اليمين معنى في الكوفة فيدخل الموجود والحادث. بخلاف بنت فلان لأن الحامل على اليمين غيظ لحفه من جهة فلان فيدخل فيه الموجود لا الحادث. ولو حلف أن لا يتزوج من نساء أهل البصرة فتزوج جارية ولدت بالبصرة ونشأت بالكوفة واستوطنت بها حنث الحالف في قول أبي حنيفة لأن المعتبر عنده في هذه الولادة، ولو حلف أن لا يتزوج من لديه إن زوجتماني امرأة فهي طالق فزوجاه امرأة بأمره :قالوا لا تصح هذه اليمين ولا تطلق . وقال الشيخ الإمام أبو بكر محمد بن الفضل : يصح وتطلق وهو الصحيح.
And in Al-Khaniyya: He said 'Every woman I marry is divorced' and he intended from such a country or intended a Habesha woman or other, he is not believed in court in the apparent narration. And if he said 'Any woman I marry is divorced', it applies to one woman unless he intends all women. And if he said 'If I marry a woman from the daughters of so-and-so, she is divorced' and so-and-so had a daughter, then a daughter was born to him, and the swearer married her, they said: He does not break his oath. It is required that the daughter exist at the time of the oath; what occurs after the oath is not included in the oath, just as if he swore not to marry from among the people of this house, and that house had no people, then people came to live in it, and the swearer married a woman, he does not break his oath. It is required that the people exist at the time of the oath, except that this answer agrees with the saying of Muhammad. As for the analogy of the saying of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, it includes in this oath those who existed at the time of the oath and those who occur after it, like one who swore not to speak to the son of so-and-so, and so-and-so had no son, then a son was born to him, and the swearer spoke to him; he breaks his oath according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, but does not break according to Muhammad. And if he said 'By Allah, I will not marry a woman from the people of Kufa', then he married a woman from the people of Kufa who was born after the oath, he breaks his oath. Muhammad distinguished between this and the daughter of so-and-so because the people of Kufa are countless, so the motive for the oath was not anger directed at the people but a meaning in Kufa, so it includes existing and future women. Unlike the daughter of so-and-so because the motive for the oath was anger from so-and-so, so it includes existing not future. And if he swore not to marry from among the women of the people of Basra, then he married a slave girl born in Basra, raised in Kufa, and settled there, he breaks his oath according to Abu Hanifa because with him the consideration is the place of birth. And if he swore 'If you two (parents) marry a woman to me, she is divorced' and they married a woman to him by his command, they said: This oath is not valid and she is not divorced. And Shaykh al-Imam Abu Bakr Muhammad ibn al-Fadl said: It is valid and she is divorced, and this is correct.
Source pages p. 15

Commentary
This passage from Al-Khaniyya presents several rulings on oaths involving marriage to women of specific groups, along with intra-Hanafi disputes. The first ruling: if a man says 'Every woman I marry is divorced' and intends a specific type, his intention is not accepted in court according to Zahir al-Riwayah. If he says 'Any woman', it applies to one unless he intends all. Next, a dispute over whether women born after the oath are included: according to Muhammad, only those existing at the time are included, but Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf include future ones based on analogy with swearing not to speak to a son who does not yet exist. However, Muhammad distinguishes: if the group is general (like Kufans), the motive is the place, so future women are included; if specific to a person's daughters, the motive is anger at that person, so only existing women are included. The last ruling concerns an oath made to parents; the valid opinion (from Muhammad ibn al-Fadl) is that it is valid and divorce occurs.

Key Terms
ظاهر الرواية (zahir al-riwayah): The apparent narration, i.e., the well-known transmitted rulings from the founding imams.
الحامل على اليمين (al-hamil ala al-yamin): The motive or cause that prompted the oath.
حنث (hanth): Breaking the oath, which here triggers divorce.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The number of existing women at the time of the oath.
B) The motive: for Kufa, the motive is an attribute; for daughters, it is personal anger.
C) The scholarship of the swearer.
D) The length of the oath period.

Key Takeaway
The scope of an oath on a group hinges on the motive: if personal anger, only existing members; if an attribute, all members.

Words of Condition and Family Terms
Rulings on condition words and interpretation of kinship terms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the words of condition (alfaz al-shart) and apply the Hanafi rule that the term 'family of so-and-so' does not include daughters of daughters.

Core Idea
Words like 'in', 'idha' suspend divorce; 'family' excludes granddaughters.

Conditional divorce oaths depend on precise wording. This lesson introduces the standard words of condition (alfaz al-shart) and then examines a key interpretive rule: the term 'family of so-and-so' (ahl bayt fulan) does not extend to daughters of daughters. Understanding this distinction prevents unintended divorce.

Guided Path
1
Identify the words of condition
List the Arabic words that serve as tools for suspending a divorce oath: 'in', 'idha', 'idha ma', 'kull', 'kullama', 'mata', 'mata ma'.
These are the standard alfaz al-shart in Hanafi fiqh.
2
Apply the family term rule
If a man swears regarding 'the family of so-and-so', only direct descendants (children) are included, not grandchildren through daughters. Marrying a daughter of a daughter does not break the oath.
This is based on 'urf (custom) and linguistic usage.
3
Analyze a time-limited oath
If the oath is 'if I marry within five years', and he marries in the fifth year, divorce occurs because the period ends at the start of the fifth year.
Analogous to renting a house for five years.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تطلق وألفاظ الشرط أن وإذا وإذا ما وكل وكلما ومتى ومتى ما ففيها أن أهل بيت فلان فتزوج بنت بنت فلان لا يحنث لأن هذا الاسم لا يتناول أولاد البنات. ولو قال إن تزوجت امرأة إلى خمس سنين فهي طالق فتزوج في السنة الخامسة طلقت لأنها لا تنتهي قبل السنة الخامسة كما لو أجر داره إلى خمس سنين. ولو قال إن أكلت من خبز والدي ما لم أتزوج فاطمة فكل امرأة أتزوجها فهي طالق فأكل ثم تزوج فاطمة بعد الأكل طلقت. ولو قال كل امرأة أتزوجها ما لم أتزوج فاطمة فهي طالق فماتت فاطمة أو غابت فتزوج غيرها طلقت في الغيبة ولا تطلق في الموت، أما في الغيبة فلأنه ما تزوج فاطمة حال بقاء اليمين فيحنث، وأما في الموت فلا يحنث في قول أبي حنيفة ومحمد لأن عندهما يمينه تبطل بالموت فلا يحنث بعده . ولو قال كل امرأة أتزوجها فقد بعت طلاقها منك بدرهم ثم تزوج بامرأة فقالت التي كانت عنده حين علمت بنكاح غيرها قبلت أو قالت طلقتها أو قالت اشتريت طلاقها طلقت التي تزوجها، وإن قالت التي كانت عنده قبل أن يتزوج أخرى قبلت لا يصح قبولها لأن ذلك قبول قبل الإيجاب اهـ . وفي الكافي للحاكم : لو قال يوم أتزوجك فأنت طالق وأنت طالق وأنت طالق ثم تزوجها طلقت واحدة في قول أبي حنيفة وثلاثاً عندهما . ولو قال. أتزوجك فأنت يوم أتزوجك فأنت طالق يوم أتزوجك فأنت طالق ثم تزوجها طلقت ثلاثاً، وكذلك «إن» و«إذا» و«متى» و«كلما». وإن قال أنت طالق وطالق وطالق يوم أتزوجك ثم تزوجها طلقت ثلاثاً بخلاف ما إذا أخر الطلاق فإن الأولى تقع فقط .اهـ. ثم قال : لو قال إذا تزوجت امرأة فهي طالق فتزوج امرأتين في عقدة واحدة فإحداهما طالق والخيار له، وإن نوى امرأة وحدها لم يدين في القضاء. ولو قال إن تزوجت امرأة وحدها لم تطلق واحدة منهما فإن تزوج أخرى بعدها طلقت اهـ. وفي القنية : قال لأجنبية إن دخلت الدار فأنت طالق من جهتي أو طلقتك وصار كأنه قال إن دخلت الدار وتزوجتك فأنت طالق . ولو قال لأجنبية إن ولدت فأنت طالق مني فتزوجها فولدت طلقت .اهـ. وهو مشكل ولو زاد قوله من جهتي كما لا يخفى.
She is divorced? And the words of condition are: 'in', 'idha', 'idha ma', 'kull', 'kullama', 'mata', 'mata ma'. Among them: 'If he said the family of so-and-so' and he married the daughter of the daughter of so-and-so, he does not break his oath because this name does not include daughters of daughters. And if he said 'If I marry a woman within five years, she is divorced', and he married in the fifth year, she is divorced because it does not end before the fifth year, just as if he rented his house for five years. And if he said 'If I eat from my father's bread as long as I have not married Fatima, then every woman I marry is divorced', and he ate then married Fatima after eating, she is divorced. And if he said 'Every woman I marry as long as I have not married Fatima is divorced', and Fatima died or went absent, and he married another, she is divorced in the case of absence and not divorced in the case of death. As for absence, because he did not marry Fatima while the oath remained, so he breaks it. As for death, he does not break according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad because according to them his oath becomes void by death, so he does not break afterward. And if he said 'Every woman I marry, I have sold her divorce to you for a dirham', then he married a woman, and the woman who was with him said when she learned of the marriage to another: 'I accept' or said 'I divorced her' or said 'I bought her divorce', the woman he married is divorced. But if the woman who was with him said before he married another 'I accept', her acceptance is not valid because that is acceptance before the offer. End. And in Al-Kafi al-Hakim: If he said 'On the day I marry you, you are divorced, and you are divorced, and you are divorced' then he married her, she is divorced once according to Abu Hanifa and three times according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad). And if he said 'I marry you, and you on the day I marry you are divorced, on the day I marry you are divorced', then he married her, she is divorced three times. Similarly with 'in', 'idha', 'mata', 'kullama'. And if he said 'You are divorced, and divorced, and divorced on the day I marry you' then he married her, she is divorced three times, unlike if he delayed the divorce then the first applies only. End. Then he said: If he said 'If I marry a woman, she is divorced', and he marries two women in one contract, one of them is divorced and he has the choice. And if he intended only one woman, he is not believed in court. And if he said 'If I marry a woman alone', not one of them is divorced; if he marries another after that, she is divorced. End. And in Al-Qunyah: He said to a non-mahram woman: 'If you enter the house, you are divorced from my side' or 'I divorce you' and it becomes as if he said 'If you enter the house and I marry you, you are divorced.' And if he said to a non-mahram woman 'If you give birth, you are divorced from me', then he marries her and she gives birth, she is divorced. End. And it is problematic even if he added the phrase 'from my side', as is not hidden.
Source pages p. 16

Commentary
This section from the sharh lists various rulings on conditional divorce. First, the words of condition are given (in, idha, etc.). Then a series of scenarios: if someone swears regarding 'family of so-and-so', daughters of daughters are not included. If the condition has a time limit (within five years), divorce occurs if marriage happens before the deadline. If the condition is linked to an act (eating bread) with a later marriage to Fatima, divorce occurs when that later marriage happens. If the condition is 'as long as I have not married Fatima', death of Fatima voids the oath (according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad), so marriage after death does not trigger divorce. The passage then discusses a sale of divorce for a dirham: acceptance before the offer is invalid. Dispute: if multiple divorces are stacked on a future condition, Abu Hanifa counts only one, while the Sahibayn count three. Similarly, if he says 'you are divorced, and divorced, and divorced on the day I marry you', three divorces occur. But if he delays the divorces, only the first takes effect. Another ruling: if he says 'If I marry a woman, she is divorced' and marries two in one contract, one is divorced (his choice). Finally, problematic rulings from Al-Qunyah about conditions involving non-mahram women.

Key Terms
ألفاظ الشرط (alfaz al-shart): The words that indicate a condition in an oath, such as 'in', 'idha', etc.
أهل بيت فلان (ahl bayt fulan): The household or family of so-and-so, interpreted as only direct children (sons and daughters) not grandchildren through daughters.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) لعل
D) إن

Key Takeaway
The words 'in', 'idha', 'kullama', etc. create conditional divorce oaths, and 'family of so-and-so' includes only children, not grandchildren through daughters.

Oaths Tied to Acts and Death of the Named Person
Rulings on oaths with compound conditions and the effect of death of the named person.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that an oath conditioned on 'as long as I have not married X' becomes void upon X's death, and distinguish between the rulings of Abu Hanifa/Muhammad and a potential opposing view.

Core Idea
Death of the named person voids the oath; absence does not.

Conditional oaths sometimes involve multiple conditions. This lesson examines a scenario where a man ties his oath to eating bread and then to marrying Fatima, and another where the condition is 'as long as I have not married Fatima.' A dispute arises: if Fatima dies, does the oath remain? Abu Hanifa and Muhammad say the oath becomes void. Understanding this distinction prevents incorrect application.

Guided Path
1
Parse the complex oath
Break down the oath: 'If I eat from my father's bread as long as I have not married Fatima, then every woman I marry is divorced.' The condition has two parts: eating bread, and not having married Fatima before that.
The oath is triggered by eating; the marriage to Fatima after eating fulfills the condition.
2
Analyze the second scenario
Now consider: 'Every woman I marry as long as I have not married Fatima is divorced.' Here, the condition is the absence of marriage to Fatima. If Fatima dies, does the condition still exist?
Abu Hanifa and Muhammad say the oath becomes void because the subject of the condition (Fatima) no longer exists.
3
Distinguish death from absence
If Fatima is absent (e.g., travelled), the oath remains because she is still alive. Marrying another breaks the oath. If she dies, the oath is void.
This is based on the principle that an oath involving a specific person becomes void upon that person's death.

تطلق وألفاظ الشرط أن وإذا وإذا ما وكل وكلما ومتى ومتى ما ففيها أن أهل بيت فلان فتزوج بنت بنت فلان لا يحنث لأن هذا الاسم لا يتناول أولاد البنات. ولو قال إن تزوجت امرأة إلى خمس سنين فهي طالق فتزوج في السنة الخامسة طلقت لأنها لا تنتهي قبل السنة الخامسة كما لو أجر داره إلى خمس سنين. ولو قال إن أكلت من خبز والدي ما لم أتزوج فاطمة فكل امرأة أتزوجها فهي طالق فأكل ثم تزوج فاطمة بعد الأكل طلقت. ولو قال كل امرأة أتزوجها ما لم أتزوج فاطمة فهي طالق فماتت فاطمة أو غابت فتزوج غيرها طلقت في الغيبة ولا تطلق في الموت، أما في الغيبة فلأنه ما تزوج فاطمة حال بقاء اليمين فيحنث، وأما في الموت فلا يحنث في قول أبي حنيفة ومحمد لأن عندهما يمينه تبطل بالموت فلا يحنث بعده . ولو قال كل امرأة أتزوجها فقد بعت طلاقها منك بدرهم ثم تزوج بامرأة فقالت التي كانت عنده حين علمت بنكاح غيرها قبلت أو قالت طلقتها أو قالت اشتريت طلاقها طلقت التي تزوجها، وإن قالت التي كانت عنده قبل أن يتزوج أخرى قبلت لا يصح قبولها لأن ذلك قبول قبل الإيجاب اهـ . وفي الكافي للحاكم : لو قال يوم أتزوجك فأنت طالق وأنت طالق وأنت طالق ثم تزوجها طلقت واحدة في قول أبي حنيفة وثلاثاً عندهما . ولو قال. أتزوجك فأنت يوم أتزوجك فأنت طالق يوم أتزوجك فأنت طالق ثم تزوجها طلقت ثلاثاً، وكذلك «إن» و«إذا» و«متى» و«كلما». وإن قال أنت طالق وطالق وطالق يوم أتزوجك ثم تزوجها طلقت ثلاثاً بخلاف ما إذا أخر الطلاق فإن الأولى تقع فقط .اهـ. ثم قال : لو قال إذا تزوجت امرأة فهي طالق فتزوج امرأتين في عقدة واحدة فإحداهما طالق والخيار له، وإن نوى امرأة وحدها لم يدين في القضاء. ولو قال إن تزوجت امرأة وحدها لم تطلق واحدة منهما فإن تزوج أخرى بعدها طلقت اهـ. وفي القنية : قال لأجنبية إن دخلت الدار فأنت طالق من جهتي أو طلقتك وصار كأنه قال إن دخلت الدار وتزوجتك فأنت طالق . ولو قال لأجنبية إن ولدت فأنت طالق مني فتزوجها فولدت طلقت .اهـ. وهو مشكل ولو زاد قوله من جهتي كما لا يخفى.
She is divorced? And the words of condition are: 'in', 'idha', 'idha ma', 'kull', 'kullama', 'mata', 'mata ma'. Among them: 'If he said the family of so-and-so' and he married the daughter of the daughter of so-and-so, he does not break his oath because this name does not include daughters of daughters. And if he said 'If I marry a woman within five years, she is divorced', and he married in the fifth year, she is divorced because it does not end before the fifth year, just as if he rented his house for five years. And if he said 'If I eat from my father's bread as long as I have not married Fatima, then every woman I marry is divorced', and he ate then married Fatima after eating, she is divorced. And if he said 'Every woman I marry as long as I have not married Fatima is divorced', and Fatima died or went absent, and he married another, she is divorced in the case of absence and not divorced in the case of death. As for absence, because he did not marry Fatima while the oath remained, so he breaks it. As for death, he does not break according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad because according to them his oath becomes void by death, so he does not break afterward. And if he said 'Every woman I marry, I have sold her divorce to you for a dirham', then he married a woman, and the woman who was with him said when she learned of the marriage to another: 'I accept' or said 'I divorced her' or said 'I bought her divorce', the woman he married is divorced. But if the woman who was with him said before he married another 'I accept', her acceptance is not valid because that is acceptance before the offer. End. And in Al-Kafi al-Hakim: If he said 'On the day I marry you, you are divorced, and you are divorced, and you are divorced' then he married her, she is divorced once according to Abu Hanifa and three times according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad). And if he said 'I marry you, and you on the day I marry you are divorced, on the day I marry you are divorced', then he married her, she is divorced three times. Similarly with 'in', 'idha', 'mata', 'kullama'. And if he said 'You are divorced, and divorced, and divorced on the day I marry you' then he married her, she is divorced three times, unlike if he delayed the divorce then the first applies only. End. Then he said: If he said 'If I marry a woman, she is divorced', and he marries two women in one contract, one of them is divorced and he has the choice. And if he intended only one woman, he is not believed in court. And if he said 'If I marry a woman alone', not one of them is divorced; if he marries another after that, she is divorced. End. And in Al-Qunyah: He said to a non-mahram woman: 'If you enter the house, you are divorced from my side' or 'I divorce you' and it becomes as if he said 'If you enter the house and I marry you, you are divorced.' And if he said to a non-mahram woman 'If you give birth, you are divorced from me', then he marries her and she gives birth, she is divorced. End. And it is problematic even if he added the phrase 'from my side', as is not hidden.
Source pages p. 16

Commentary
This section from the sharh lists various rulings on conditional divorce. First, the words of condition are given (in, idha, etc.). Then a series of scenarios: if someone swears regarding 'family of so-and-so', daughters of daughters are not included. If the condition has a time limit (within five years), divorce occurs if marriage happens before the deadline. If the condition is linked to an act (eating bread) with a later marriage to Fatima, divorce occurs when that later marriage happens. If the condition is 'as long as I have not married Fatima', death of Fatima voids the oath (according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad), so marriage after death does not trigger divorce. The passage then discusses a sale of divorce for a dirham: acceptance before the offer is invalid. Dispute: if multiple divorces are stacked on a future condition, Abu Hanifa counts only one, while the Sahibayn count three. Similarly, if he says 'you are divorced, and divorced, and divorced on the day I marry you', three divorces occur. But if he delays the divorces, only the first takes effect. Another ruling: if he says 'If I marry a woman, she is divorced' and marries two in one contract, one is divorced (his choice). Finally, problematic rulings from Al-Qunyah about conditions involving non-mahram women.

Key Terms
يمين تبطل بالموت (yamin tabtulu bi al-mawt): An oath becomes void upon the death of the person who is the subject of the condition.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A man says: 'Every woman I marry as long as I have not married Zaynab is divorced.' Zaynab dies. He marries another woman. According to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, what is the ruling?
A) She is divorced.
B) She is not divorced.
C) The marriage is invalid.
D) He must divorce Zaynab's sister.

Key Takeaway
If a conditional divorce oath is tied to a specific person ('as long as I have not married X'), death of that person voids the oath; absence does not.

Talaq — Divorce
advanced
12 min
Sale of Divorce, Multiple Divorces on Condition, and Problematic Cases
Rulings on selling divorce, stacked divorces, and problematic conditional oaths on non-mahram women.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the rules on offer and acceptance in a conditional sale of divorce, distinguish between the Hanafi and Sahibayn views on multiple divorces stacked on a future condition, and identify problematic cases from Al-Qunyah.

Core Idea
Acceptance before offer invalidates; stacking divorces on a condition: Abu Hanifa counts one, Sahibayn count three.

This lesson covers three distinct areas. First, a unique scenario where a man sells the divorce of future wives for a dirham: the timing of acceptance matters. Second, a key dispute between Abu Hanifa and the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) regarding multiple divorce statements when linked to a future condition like marriage. Third, problematic rulings from Al-Qunyah involving non-mahram women. Understanding these nuances prevents errors in complex divorce oaths.

Guided Path
1
Analyze the sale of divorce
If a man says 'Every woman I marry, I have sold her divorce to you for a dirham', and then marries, the other woman's acceptance must occur after the marriage. Acceptance before is invalid.
This is because the offer is not yet valid before the marriage exists.
2
Understand the dispute on multiple divorces
If a man says 'On the day I marry you, you are divorced, and you are divorced, and you are divorced', then marries. Abu Hanifa counts only one divorce; the two (Sahibayn) count three.
The key is whether the repetition counts as separate oaths or emphasis.
3
Examine the problematic Al-Qunyah cases
If a man says to a non-mahram woman 'If you enter the house, you are divorced from my side' or 'If you give birth, you are divorced from me', then marries her and the condition occurs, divorce occurs. This is problematic because he has no right to divorce before marriage.
The Hanafis treat it as a conditional divorce that becomes effective upon marriage.

The Hanafi Definition of Shart: Linguistic Roots and Technical Precision
Linguistic and usul definition of shart

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi usul definition of shart, distinguish it from its linguistic meanings, and apply the distinction between 'in' and 'idha' in ta'liq.

Core Idea
In Hanafi usul, a condition (shart) is pure suspension without temporal implication, unlike particles like idha.

This lesson unpacks the term 'shart' (condition) as it appears in Hanafi legal theory. We begin with its general linguistic meaning—a binding stipulation in transactions—and then zoom in on the precise technical definition used by usul scholars. The key is a subtle refinement: when we say 'if' (in), the suspension is purely logical and timeless; when we say 'when' (idha), time enters the equation. This distinction has real consequences for suspensive divorces and oaths.

Guided Path
1
1. Identify the term and its linguistic range
Read the Arabic passage and note how 'shart' is defined in language: obligation, stipulation, sign, stream, beginning. This broad lexical field sets the stage.
The proverb 'al-shart amlak alyaka am lak' shows condition as a binding term.
2
2. Locate the usul definition from Al-Talwih
Extract the technical definition: 'ta'liq husul madmun jumla bi husul madmun jumla' (suspending the occurrence of the content of one sentence upon the occurrence of the content of another).
This is the core definition used in Hanafi usul.
3
3. Note the refinement for the particle 'in'
Observe that for 'in' (if), the definition is restricted by adding 'only'—meaning pure suspension without any implication of time or container. For 'idha' and 'mata', such temporal elements are present.
This refinement prevents overlap between pure condition and temporal condition.
4
4. Understand the consequence for ta'liq
Relate this definition to practical ta'liq in divorce: using 'in' creates a pure conditional; using 'idha' may introduce a temporal aspect that affects when the consequence occurs.
Hanafi fiqh treats these differently in rulings.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وألفاظ الشرط إن وإذا وإذا ما وكل وكلما ومتى ومتى ما) وهو في اللغة كما في القاموس إلزام الشيء والتزامه في البيع ونحوه كالشريطة والجمع شروط. وفي المثل الشرط أملك عليك أم لك. وبزغ الحجام بشرط ويشرط فيهما والدون اللثيم السافل . والجمع أشراط وبالتحريك العلامة والجمع أشراط وكل مسيل صغير يجيء من قدر عشرة أذرع وأول الشيء ورزال المال وصغارها والأشراف أشراط أيضاً ضـدا هـ. وعند الأصوليين كما في التلويح تعليق حصول مضمون جملة بحصول مضمون جملة ويزاد في فقط أي من غير اعتبار ظرفية ونحوها كما في «إذا» و «متى» ا وفي المعراج : الشروط هـ. قوله : (ويزاد في أن فقط) أي يزاد على التعريف المذكور لفظ «فقط» في التعليق بـ «إن» أما أن في
His statement: (And the words of condition are 'in', 'idha', 'idha ma', 'kull', 'kullama', 'mata', 'mata ma'). It is in language as in Al-Qamus: binding a thing and committing to it in sale and the like, like a stipulation, plural shurut. In a proverb: 'The condition: I own it for you or you own it for me.' And 'the cupper bleeds by condition' and he stipulates in both. And 'al-dawn' (low) and 'al-lathim' (base) and 'al-safil' (low). The plural is ashrat, and with fatha it means sign, plural ashrat, and every small stream that comes from about ten cubits, and the beginning of a thing, and the small cattle, and 'al-ashraf' also ashrat (opposite). And according to the usuliyyun as in Al-Talwih: suspending the occurrence of the content of one sentence on the occurrence of the content of another sentence, and 'only' is added (i.e., without considering time and the like, as in 'idha' and 'mata'). And in Al-Mi'raj: 'al-shurut' etc. His statement: (And 'only' is added in 'in') i.e., the word 'only' is added to the mentioned definition in suspension with 'in'. As for 'in' in...
Source pages p. 16

Commentary
This commentary from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq defines 'shart' (condition). Linguistically, it means a binding stipulation in transactions. The usul definition is: suspending the occurrence of the content of one sentence (the consequence) on the occurrence of the content of another sentence (the condition). However, for words like 'in' (which does not imply time), the definition is restricted to pure suspension without any temporal aspect; for words like 'idha' and 'mata', they imply time, so the definition is general. The text cites Al-Talwih and Al-Mi'raj. This distinction is important in ta'liq (suspensive divorces and oaths).

Key Terms
الشرط (al-shart): Suspending the occurrence of a consequence on the occurrence of a condition.
التعليق (al-ta'liq): Suspension of a ruling (like divorce) on a future condition.
في فقط (fi faqat): Refinement meaning 'only' added to the definition for the particle 'in'.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the definition in Al-Talwih, what is the technical meaning of shart in usul?
A) A binding stipulation in a contract.
B) Suspending the occurrence of one sentence on the occurrence of another.
C) A sign or marker.
D) A small stream.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, 'shart' is the pure suspension of one proposition on another, with a special refinement for the particle 'in' to exclude temporal implications.

The Rule of Conditional Divorce and Its Particles
States the core rule and lists conditional particles

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the rule of conditional divorce and list the seven conditional particles recognized in Hanafi fiqh.

Core Idea
When a condition is met, the divorce takes effect — the exact wording matters.

Conditional divorce (ta'liq al-talaq) is a distinctive feature of Hanafi fiqh. This lesson introduces the foundational rule: upon fulfillment of a properly expressed condition, the divorce becomes binding. You will learn the seven particles used to express conditions, which are essential for correctly analyzing such cases.

Guided Path
1
Identify the rule
Read the first sentence: 'وجد الشرط تطلق' (He found the condition: she divorces). This states the key principle: when the condition occurs, the divorce takes effect immediately.
The verb 'تطلق' is in the present tense, indicating immediate effect.
2
List the conditional particles
The passage then enumerates seven words: 'أن', 'إذا', 'إذا ما', 'كل', 'كلما', 'متى', 'متى ما'. Memorize these as they are the recognized tools for ta'liq.
These are standard Arabic conditional particles; note that 'كل' and 'كلما' are used for repetitive conditions.
3
Understand the scope
These particles are the only valid ways to create a conditional divorce in Hanafi law. Any other wording may not be effective.
Other schools may accept additional particles, but the Hanafi school is strict on this list.

Source Text

وجد الشرط تطلق وألفاظ الشرط أن وإذا وإذا ما وكل وكلما ومتى ومتى ما ففيها أن أهل بيت فلان فتزوج بنت بنت فلان لا يحنث لأن هذا الاسم لا يتناول أولاد البنات. ولو قال إن تزوجت امرأة إلى خمس سنين فهي طالق فتزوج في السنة الخامسة طلقت لأنها لا تنتهي قبل السنة الخامسة كما لو أجر داره إلى خمس سنين. ولو قال إن أكلت من خبز والدي ما لم أتزوج فاطمة فكل امرأة أتزوجها فهي طالق فأكل ثم تزوج فاطمة بعد الأكل طلقت. ولو قال كل امرأة أتزوجها ما لم أتزوج فاطمة فهي طالق فماتت فاطمة أو غابت فتزوج غيرها طلقت في الغيبة ولا تطلق في الموت، أما في الغيبة فلأنه ما تزوج فاطمة حال بقاء اليمين فيحنث، وأما في الموت فلا يحنث في قول أبي حنيفة ومحمد لأن عندهما يمينه تبطل بالموت فلا يحنث بعده . ولو قال كل امرأة أتزوجها فقد بعت طلاقها منك بدرهم ثم تزوج بامرأة فقالت التي كانت عنده حين علمت بنكاح غيرها قبلت أو قالت طلقتها أو قالت اشتريت طلاقها طلقت التي تزوجها، وإن قالت التي كانت عنده قبل أن يتزوج أخرى قبلت لا يصح قبولها لأن ذلك قبول قبل الإيجاب اهـ.
He found the condition: she divorces. The words of condition are: 'in', 'idha', 'idha ma', 'kull', 'kullama', 'mata', 'mata ma'. Among them: if he said 'the people of so-and-so's house' and then married the daughter of so-and-so's daughter, he does not break his oath because this name does not include the daughters' children. If he said 'If I marry a woman within five years, she is divorced' and then married in the fifth year, she is divorced because the five years do not end before the fifth year, just as if he rented out his house for five years. If he said 'If I eat from my father's bread as long as I have not married Fatima, then every woman I marry is divorced' and he ate, then married Fatima after eating, she is divorced. If he said 'Every woman I marry as long as I have not married Fatima is divorced' and then Fatima died or disappeared, and he married another, she divorces in the case of disappearance but not in the case of death. As for disappearance, because he did not marry Fatima while the oath remained, so he violates it. As for death, he does not violate it according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, because in their view his oath becomes void upon death, so he does not violate it after. If he said 'Every woman I marry, I have sold her divorce to you for a dirham' and then married a woman, and the one who was with him at the time of learning of the other marriage said 'I accept' or 'I divorced her' or 'I bought her divorce', the woman he married is divorced. If the one who was with him said 'I accept' before he married another, her acceptance is not valid because it is acceptance before the offer. End.
Source pages p. 16

Commentary
This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq presents the core rule: conditional divorce takes effect upon occurrence of the condition. The author lists the standard conditional particles used in divorce: in, idha, idha ma, kull, kullama, mata, mata ma. Then follow illustrative examples. The first example shows that the word 'ahl' (people) refers only to direct descendants, not grandchildren, so marrying a granddaughter does not break an oath about 'ahl'. The second example clarifies that 'to five years' includes the fifth year itself, analogous to a rental contract. The third example shows that a prior condition (eating) overrides a later condition (marrying Fatima). The fourth example distinguishes between death and disappearance of the named person: upon death, the oath becomes void (according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad), so no violation; upon disappearance, the oath remains, so marrying another is a violation. The final example deals with a sale of divorce: the offer to sell divorce is like an offer to buy; acceptance before the offer is invalid. The commentator then adds that the wife's acceptance is only effective after the husband marries another, as per the standard rules of offer and acceptance in sales.

Key Terms
تعليق (ta'liq): Suspension of divorce upon occurrence of a future condition.
شرط (shart): A condition upon which the divorce is contingent.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the rule of conditional divorce?
A) The divorce occurs only if the husband repeats it after the condition.
B) The divorce occurs when the condition is met.
C) The divorce is invalid if conditional.
D) The divorce occurs only if the wife agrees.

Key Takeaway
Conditional divorce in Hanafi fiqh is valid and becomes effective upon the fulfillment of the condition expressed using one of the seven recognized particles.

Interpreting Conditions: Wording and Time Limits
Provides two examples on interpreting conditional wording

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the rules for interpreting conditional wording, especially regarding family relationships and time periods.

Core Idea
Words like 'ahl' and 'to five years' have specific legal meanings that determine whether a condition is fulfilled.

Conditional divorce often hinges on precise wording. This lesson examines two key examples from the passage: the word 'ahl' (people of a household) does not include grandchildren, so marrying a granddaughter does not violate an oath about 'ahl'. Also, a condition like 'within five years' includes the fifth year itself, similar to rental contracts. These examples train you to interpret conditions literally.

Guided Path
1
Analyze the 'ahl' example
The passage says if a man says 'the people of so-and-so's house' and marries the daughter of so-and-so's daughter, he does not break his oath because 'ahl' does not include grandchildren. Understand that 'ahl' refers to direct descendants (children), not grandchildren.
This is a linguistic point: 'ahl' in Arabic customarily means immediate family.
2
Analyze the five-year example
If a man says 'if I marry a woman within five years, she is divorced' and marries in the fifth year, she is divorced. The 'five years' includes the fifth year, analogized to a rental contract for five years, which includes the fifth year.
The analogy to rental shows consistency in Hanafi contract interpretation.
3
Apply the interpretive principle
The principle is: words are given their customary legal meaning. 'Ahl' is not extended to grandchildren; time periods include the final unit.
This principle applies to all conditional contracts, not just divorce.

Multiple Conditions and Changes in the Condition Subject
Presents examples of sequential conditions and effect of death/disappearance

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply rules for sequential conditions and analyze the impact of death or disappearance of the condition subject on conditional divorce.

Core Idea
A condition can override another; death of the named person voids the oath, but disappearance does not.

This lesson covers more complex scenarios. The first example shows that a prior condition (eating) can coexist with a later condition (marriage), and when both occur, the divorce takes effect. The second example distinguishes between death and disappearance of the named person (Fatima): upon death, the oath becomes void according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, so marrying another does not trigger divorce; upon disappearance, the oath remains and divorce occurs. These rules illustrate how changes in the condition subject affect the ruling.

Guided Path
1
Analyze the prior condition example
A man says: 'If I eat from my father's bread as long as I have not married Fatima, then every woman I marry is divorced.' He eats, then marries Fatima. The divorce occurs because the condition 'eating' was fulfilled and then the marriage. Understand that the condition 'as long as I have not married Fatima' is a temporary suspension that ends upon marriage.
The 'as long as' (ma lam) creates a conditional loop that is resolved by the first condition (eating).
2
Analyze death vs disappearance
Another man says: 'Every woman I marry as long as I have not married Fatima is divorced.' Fatima then dies or disappears. He marries another woman. In case of disappearance, he must divorce because the oath remains. In case of death, the oath becomes void, so no divorce. The difference is that death makes fulfillment impossible, thus the oath lapses.
This is the position of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad. Some scholars may differ on death.

One Divorce or Three? Abu Hanifa vs. Sahibayn on Repeated Talaq
Intra-Hanafi dispute on number of divorces from repeated talaq under a single condition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why Abu Hanifa rules one divorce while the Sahibayn rule three when a husband repeats 'you are divorced' under a single conditional statement.

Core Idea
Repetition of talaq under one condition is either emphasis (Abu Hanifa) or separate pronouncements (Sahibayn).

When a man says 'The day I marry you, you are divorced, and you are divorced, and you are divorced,' how many divorces actually occur? The answer depends on whether the repetition is seen as mere emphasis or as independent pronouncements. This seemingly technical question reveals a deeper principle about how we interpret repeated legal expressions under a single condition. The Hanafi school itself disagrees: Abu Hanifa counts one, while his two foremost students count three.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and conditional phrase
The man utters a conditional statement: 'The day I marry you' (condition) followed by 'you are divorced' repeated three times. Focus on the linguistic structure: the condition is mentioned once, and the talaq is repeated three times consecutively.
The repetition is of the talaq phrase, not the condition.
2
State the two Hanafi positions
Abu Hanifa: one divorce. Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad): three divorces. Note that this is an intra-madhhab dispute within the Hanafi school itself.
The difference is not about the validity of the condition but about the number of divorces triggered.
3
Understand the reasoning behind each position
Abu Hanifa views the repetition as emphasis (ta'kid) – each 'you are divorced' reinforces the same divorce under the same condition. The Sahibayn view each as a separate pronouncement, each conditioned on the same event, thus three divorces occur simultaneously.
The divergence is rooted in how they interpret repeated commands or declarations in legal language.
4
Contrast with explicit repetition of the condition
If the man repeats the entire conditional phrase (e.g., 'The day I marry you, you are divorced; the day I marry you, you are divorced') then all agree three divorces occur. This confirms that the dispute is specific to repetition of only the talaq part.
When the condition is repeated, each talaq is tied to a distinct condition, so no ambiguity.
5
Determine the mufta bihi (operative ruling)
Later Hanafi practice tends to follow the Sahibayn in this context, meaning three divorces occur and the marriage is irrevocably ended. However, the text presents both views without explicit tarjih.
A mufti should be aware of the disagreement and may adopt either view depending on circumstances.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي الكافي للحاكم : لو قال يوم أتزوجك فأنت طالق وأنت طالق وأنت طالق ثم تزوجها طلقت واحدة في قول أبي حنيفة وثلاثاً عندهما . ولو قال. أتزوجك فأنت يوم أتزوجك فأنت طالق يوم أتزوجك فأنت طالق ثم تزوجها طلقت ثلاثاً، وكذلك «إن» و«إذا» و«متى» و«كلما». وإن قال أنت طالق وطالق وطالق يوم أتزوجك ثم تزوجها طلقت ثلاثاً بخلاف ما إذا أخر الطلاق فإن الأولى تقع فقط .اهـ. ثم قال : لو قال إذا تزوجت امرأة فهي طالق فتزوج امرأتين في عقدة واحدة فإحداهما طالق والخيار له، وإن نوى امرأة وحدها لم يدين في القضاء. ولو قال إن تزوجت امرأة وحدها لم تطلق واحدة منهما فإن تزوج أخرى بعدها طلقت اهـ.
In al-Kafi by al-Hakim: If he said 'The day I marry you, you are divorced, and you are divorced, and you are divorced' then married her, she is divorced once according to Abu Hanifa, and three times according to them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad). If he said 'I marry you, you are (divorced), the day I marry you, you are divorced, the day I marry you, you are divorced' then married her, she is divorced three times, and similarly with 'in', 'idha', 'mata', and 'kullama'. If he said 'You are divorced, and divorced, and divorced the day I marry you' then married her, she is divorced three times, contrary to when he delays the divorce, then only the first occurs. End. Then he said: If he said 'If I marry a woman, she is divorced' and married two women in one contract, one of them is divorced and he has choice (to specify which). If he intended one woman specifically, it is not considered in judicial ruling. If he said 'If I marry a woman alone' he does not divorce either of them; if he marries another after that, she is divorced. End.
Source pages p. 16

Commentary
This passage from al-Kafi elaborates on multiple divorces through conditional statements. The first scenario: a man says 'The day I marry you, you are divorced, and you are divorced, and you are divorced.' Abu Hanifa rules that only one divorce occurs because the repetition is for emphasis and all refer to the same condition. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) rule that three divorces occur because each 'you are divorced' is a separate pronouncement conditioned on the same event. The next scenario uses explicit repetition of the conditional phrase, which yields three divorces by consensus. The third scenario: if the condition is delayed ('you are divorced... the day I marry you'), the divorces are counted as three because the condition applies to each. But if the divorces are stated first and then the condition, only the first takes effect. The second half of the passage discusses marrying two women in one contract: if the condition is general, only one divorces and the husband chooses which; if he intended one woman, that intention is not considered in court, but if he says 'a woman alone' neither divorces, but a later marriage triggers the condition.

Key Terms
تعليق (ta'liq): Making divorce conditional on a future event.
تكرير (takrir): Repetition of the talaq phrase.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, if a man says 'If you go outside, you are divorced, you are divorced' and the wife goes outside, how many divorces occur?
A) One divorce
B) Two divorces
C) Three divorces
D) No divorce

Key Takeaway
When talaq is repeated under a single condition, the number of divorces is disputed: one (Abu Hanifa) or three (Sahibayn); the mufta bihi is often three.

Delaying the Condition and Marrying Two Women in One Contract
Rules for order of talaq and condition, and for conditional talaq when marrying two women in one contract

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the rule for when talaq is stated before the condition vs. after, and determine the number of divorces when marrying two women in one contract under a conditional talaq.

Core Idea
Order matters: talaq before condition counts all, talaq after condition only the first; a general condition on 'a woman' gives choice to the husband.

The source continues with two more scenarios that clarify how the order of talaq and condition affects the count. First, if the husband says 'You are divorced, and divorced, and divorced, the day I marry you,' all three divorces occur because the condition applies to each. But if he delays the condition (i.e., says the condition first), only the first divorce takes effect. Second, if he says 'If I marry a woman, she is divorced' and marries two women in a single contract, only one of them is divorced, and he can choose which one. These rules show the Hanafi attention to linguistic structure and intent.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two ordering scenarios
Scenario A: talaq is stated before the condition (e.g., 'You are divorced, and divorced, and divorced, the day I marry you'). Scenario B: condition is stated before talaq (e.g., 'The day I marry you, you are divorced').
The passage also contrasts 'if he delays the divorce' (meaning the condition is stated first) with the first scenario.
2
Apply the rule for order
In Scenario A (talaq before condition), all three divorces occur because the condition is understood to apply to each talaq. In Scenario B (condition first, talaq delayed), only the first talaq occurs because the condition triggers only the first immediate talaq.
The difference is subtle: when talaq precedes, it's as if each talaq is waiting for the condition; when condition precedes, it binds only the first talaq.
3
Analyze the two-women-in-one-contract scenario
If he says 'If I marry a woman, she is divorced' and then marries two women simultaneously in one contract, only one woman is divorced. The husband has the choice (khayar) to decide which one. If he intended a specific woman in his heart, that intention is not considered in judicial ruling (qada').
The phrase 'a woman' is indefinite and general, so it applies to one instance only.
4
Differentiate the 'woman alone' phrase
If he says 'If I marry a woman alone' (meaning 'a single woman'), then neither of the two women is divorced because 'alone' implies a restriction to one woman. However, if he later marries another woman, that later marriage triggers the divorce.
The word 'alone' (wahdaha) changes the meaning to a condition of marrying a woman without any other, so the first marriage to two does not satisfy it.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي الكافي للحاكم : لو قال يوم أتزوجك فأنت طالق وأنت طالق وأنت طالق ثم تزوجها طلقت واحدة في قول أبي حنيفة وثلاثاً عندهما . ولو قال. أتزوجك فأنت يوم أتزوجك فأنت طالق يوم أتزوجك فأنت طالق ثم تزوجها طلقت ثلاثاً، وكذلك «إن» و«إذا» و«متى» و«كلما». وإن قال أنت طالق وطالق وطالق يوم أتزوجك ثم تزوجها طلقت ثلاثاً بخلاف ما إذا أخر الطلاق فإن الأولى تقع فقط .اهـ. ثم قال : لو قال إذا تزوجت امرأة فهي طالق فتزوج امرأتين في عقدة واحدة فإحداهما طالق والخيار له، وإن نوى امرأة وحدها لم يدين في القضاء. ولو قال إن تزوجت امرأة وحدها لم تطلق واحدة منهما فإن تزوج أخرى بعدها طلقت اهـ.
In al-Kafi by al-Hakim: If he said 'The day I marry you, you are divorced, and you are divorced, and you are divorced' then married her, she is divorced once according to Abu Hanifa, and three times according to them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad). If he said 'I marry you, you are (divorced), the day I marry you, you are divorced, the day I marry you, you are divorced' then married her, she is divorced three times, and similarly with 'in', 'idha', 'mata', and 'kullama'. If he said 'You are divorced, and divorced, and divorced the day I marry you' then married her, she is divorced three times, contrary to when he delays the divorce, then only the first occurs. End. Then he said: If he said 'If I marry a woman, she is divorced' and married two women in one contract, one of them is divorced and he has choice (to specify which). If he intended one woman specifically, it is not considered in judicial ruling. If he said 'If I marry a woman alone' he does not divorce either of them; if he marries another after that, she is divorced. End.
Source pages p. 16

Commentary
This passage from al-Kafi elaborates on multiple divorces through conditional statements. The first scenario: a man says 'The day I marry you, you are divorced, and you are divorced, and you are divorced.' Abu Hanifa rules that only one divorce occurs because the repetition is for emphasis and all refer to the same condition. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) rule that three divorces occur because each 'you are divorced' is a separate pronouncement conditioned on the same event. The next scenario uses explicit repetition of the conditional phrase, which yields three divorces by consensus. The third scenario: if the condition is delayed ('you are divorced... the day I marry you'), the divorces are counted as three because the condition applies to each. But if the divorces are stated first and then the condition, only the first takes effect. The second half of the passage discusses marrying two women in one contract: if the condition is general, only one divorces and the husband chooses which; if he intended one woman, that intention is not considered in court, but if he says 'a woman alone' neither divorces, but a later marriage triggers the condition.

Key Terms
أخر الطلاق (akhkhara al-talaq): To delay the divorce pronouncement (i.e., to state the condition first and then the talaq).
عقدة واحدة (uqda wahida): A single marriage contract involving two women simultaneously.
الخيار له (al-khiyar lahu): He has the choice (to decide which woman is divorced).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A man says to his wife, 'You are divorced, you are divorced, you are divorced, the day I marry you.' He then marries her. How many divorces occur?
A) One
B) Two
C) Three
D) None

Key Takeaway
Order matters: talaq before condition counts all; condition before talaq counts only the first. A general condition on 'a woman' gives one divorce with choice when marrying two in one contract.

Conditional Divorce Addressed to an Ajnabiyya: Ruling and Its Problematic Aspect
Rules for conditional divorce uttered to a non-wife before marriage

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a conditional divorce uttered to a non-wife before marriage and explain why the ruling is considered problematic.

Core Idea
A conditional divorce to an ajnabiyya is valid if tied to future marriage, yet this ruling is fraught with legal difficulty.

This lesson examines a specific rule from al-Qunyah regarding divorce uttered conditionally to a woman who is not yet one's wife. The passage presents two cases where such statements are interpreted as referring to future marriage, resulting in divorce when the condition is fulfilled after marriage. However, the commentator explicitly marks this ruling as problematic, inviting us to probe its underlying reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the cases
Read the two scenarios: (1) saying 'If you enter the house, you are divorced from my side' and (2) saying 'If you give birth, you are divorced from me.' Note that both are addressed to an ajnabiyya (a non-wife).
The ruling interprets the utterance as implicitly conditioned on future marriage.
2
Understand the interpretation
The author of al-Qunyah treats the first statement as equivalent to 'If you enter the house and I marry you, you are divorced.' The second is taken literally, but the condition (giving birth) can only occur after marriage, so future marriage is implied.
This interpretive move makes the conditional divorce effective upon marriage.
3
Recognize the problematic element
The commentator (Ibn Nujaym) says the ruling is 'mushkil' (problematic) even if 'from my side' is added. Consider: the condition was uttered before the woman became his wife—does a condition have legal effect on a non-existent relationship?
The problem lies in attaching a divorce condition to a future, uncertain marriage.
4
Consider the implications
If the ruling is accepted, a man can bind himself to divorce a future wife by a pre-marital condition. If rejected, such statements are legally irrelevant. The Hanafi school's conflict on this point reveals deeper usul issues.
This leads to a legitimate ikhtilaf (scholarly disagreement) on the validity of such conditional divorces.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي القنية : قال لأجنبية إن دخلت الدار فأنت طالق من جهتي أو طلقتك وصار كأنه قال إن دخلت الدار وتزوجتك فأنت طالق . ولو قال لأجنبية إن ولدت فأنت طالق مني فتزوجها فولدت طلقت .اهـ. وهو مشكل ولو زاد قوله من جهتي كما لا يخفى.
In al-Qunyah: He said to an alien woman: 'If you enter the house, you are divorced from my side' or 'I divorced you' and it becomes as if he said 'If you enter the house and I marry you, you are divorced.' If he said to an alien woman: 'If you give birth, you are divorced from me' then he married her and she gave birth, she is divorced. End. It is problematic, even if he added 'from my side', as is clear.
Source pages p. 16

Commentary
This short passage from al-Qunyah deals with conditional divorce addressed to a non-wife (ajnabiyya). If a man says to a woman he is not married to 'If you enter the house, you are divorced from my side', this is interpreted as 'If you enter the house and I marry you, you are divorced.' Thus, if he later marries her and she enters the house, divorce occurs. Similarly, if he says 'If you give birth, you are divorced from me', then marries her and she gives birth, she is divorced. The commentator notes that this ruling is problematic (mushkil) because the condition was stated before marriage, and adding 'from my side' does not resolve the issue, as is obvious. The point of difficulty is that the condition is attached to a future marriage, which some scholars consider invalid because the woman is not yet his wife at the time of utterance. However, the ruling in al-Qunyah appears to accept it.

Key Terms
أجنبية (ajnabiyya): A woman not married to the speaker; a non-wife.
تعليق بالطلاق (ta'liq bi al-talaq): Suspending the occurrence of divorce upon the fulfillment of a condition.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) When he marries her, regardless of entry
C) If she enters the house after marriage
D) The divorce is invalid because the condition was stated before marriage

Key Takeaway
A conditional divorce uttered to an ajnabiyya can be binding if interpreted as tied to future marriage, but the ruling is legally problematic and subject to ikhtilaf.

Definition and Types of Conditions (Shurut)
Defines 'shart' linguistically and terminologically, and classifies it

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the linguistic and technical definitions of 'shart' and identify the characteristics of each of the four types of conditions.

Core Idea
A condition (shart) does not cause the ruling; its absence prevents it, but its presence does not guarantee the ruling.

The concept of 'shart' (condition) is fundamental in Hanafi fiqh and usul al-fiqh. This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq defines the term both linguistically and as a technical term, then breaks down conditions into four categories. Understanding these categories is crucial for correctly applying conditions in legal reasoning, especially when differentiating between causes (sabab), conditions (shart), and preventers (mani').

Guided Path
1
Identify the linguistic meaning
The passage gives multiple linguistic meanings from al-Qamus: a stipulation in a contract, a sign, a small stream, the beginning of something, etc. Note the range of meanings.
Linguistic variety shows that 'shart' is a homonym in Arabic.
2
Understand the usul definition
The author defines shart as the suspension of the occurrence of the content of one sentence upon the occurrence of the content of another sentence, without consideration of circumstantiality (e.g., 'idha' and 'mata').
This is the generic definition used in Hanafi usul.
3
Learn the four types
The passage classifies conditions into shari'i (legal, e.g., wudu for prayer), 'aqli (rational, e.g., life for knowledge), 'urfi (customary, e.g., climbing for ascending the roof), and lughawi (linguistic, e.g., conditional divorce). Contrast them: legal conditions do not necessitate the conditioned; linguistic conditions do.
Shar'i conditions are 'suspensive' – they must be present but do not ensure the act's validity.
4
Apply the comparison with sabab and mani'
The passage concludes by distinguishing shart from sabab (cause) and mani' (preventer): for mani', its existence prevents the ruling; for shart, its non-existence prevents the ruling; for sabab, both existence and non-existence are considered (the ruling is tied to the cause).
This distinction is vital for understanding legal causality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وألفاظ الشرط إن وإذا وإذا ما وكل وكلما ومتى ومتى ما) وهو في اللغة كما في القاموس إلزام الشيء والتزامه في البيع ونحوه كالشريطة والجمع شروط. وفي المثل الشرط أملك عليك أم لك. وبزغ الحجام بشرط ويشرط فيهما والدون اللثيم السافل . والجمع أشراط وبالتحريك العلامة والجمع أشراط وكل مسيل صغير يجيء من قدر عشرة أذرع وأول الشيء ورزال المال وصغارها والأشراف أشراط أيضاً ضـدا هـ. وعند الأصوليين كما في التلويح تعليق حصول مضمون جملة بحصول مضمون جملة ويزاد في فقط أي من غير اعتبار ظرفية ونحوها كما في «إذا» و «متى» ا وفي المعراج : الشروط شرعية وعقلية وعرفية ولغوية. فالشرعية كالوضوء وستر العورة واستقبال القبلة وطهارة الثوب والمكان واليدن فيتوقف وجود الصلاة عليها ولا يلزم من وجودها وجود الصلاة. والعقلي كالحياة مع العلم فيلزم من وجود العلم الحياة من غير عكس. والعرفية ويقال لها الشرطية العادية كالسلم مع صعود السطح فيلزم من الصعود وجوده من غير عكس. واللغوية مثل التعليقات فيلزم من وجود الشرط وجود المشروط . قالوا : وهو حقيقة السبب وبهذا قال النحويون في الشرط والجزاء مع السببية للأول والمسببة للثاني والمعتبر من المانع وجوده ومن الشرط عدمه ومن السبب وجوده وعدمه ا هـ .
His statement: (And the words of condition are: 'in', 'idha', 'idha ma', 'kull', 'kullama', 'mata', 'mata ma'). In language, as in al-Qamus, it is the imposition of a thing and its undertaking in sale and the like, like a stipulation (sharita), plural shurut. In a proverb: 'The condition is yours to impose or yours to accept.' And 'the cupper made a condition' and 'he conditions' in both senses, and 'ad-dun' the lowly. The plural is ashrat, and with fatha it means 'sign', plural ashrat, and every small stream that comes from about ten cubits, and the beginning of a thing, and the small cattle and their young, and also 'the noble' are ashrat as a contrast. According to the usuliyyun, as in al-Talwih: it is the suspension of the occurrence of the content of one sentence upon the occurrence of the content of another sentence, and 'fis' is added only, meaning without consideration of circumstantiality and the like, as in 'idha' and 'mata'. And in al-Mi'raj: Conditions are of four types: shari'i, 'aqli, 'urfi, and lughawi. The shari'i like wudu, covering the awra, facing the qibla, purity of garment, place, and body: the existence of prayer depends on them, but their existence does not necessitate the existence of prayer. The 'aqli like life with knowledge: knowledge necessitates life, not vice versa. The 'urfi, also called ordinary conditional, like climbing with ascending the roof: ascending necessitates climbing, not vice versa. The lughawi like ta'liqat (conditional divorce): the existence of the condition necessitates the existence of the conditioned. They said: it is the reality of cause (sabab), and this is what the grammarians say about the conditional and the consequent with causality for the first and being caused for the second. And what is considered from the preventer (mani') is its existence, from the condition (shart) its non-existence, and from the cause (sabab) both its existence and non-existence. End.
Source pages p. 16

Commentary
This passage is a detailed commentary on the definition of 'shart' (condition). It begins with linguistic definitions from al-Qamus, showing the word's various meanings: a stipulation in a contract, a sign, a small stream, the beginning of something, etc. Then it gives the terminological definition from usul al-fiqh as the suspension of one thing upon another. It notes that with 'in' the condition is pure, without consideration of adverbial meaning. Then it classifies conditions into four types: (1) Shar'i (legal): these are prerequisites for worship like wudu for prayer; their absence prevents prayer but their presence does not guarantee prayer. (2) 'Aqli (rational): like life is a condition for knowledge; knowledge implies life but not vice versa. (3) 'Urfi (customary/ordinary): like climbing a ladder is a condition for ascending the roof; ascent implies climbing but not vice versa. (4) Lughawi (linguistic): these are like the conditions in oath and divorce; the existence of the condition necessitates the existence of the conditioned. The passage concludes with a comparison of three concepts: mani' (preventer) – its existence prevents the ruling; shart (condition) – its non-existence prevents the ruling; sabab (cause) – its existence and non-existence are both considered (i.e., the ruling is tied to the cause). This is crucial for understanding legal causality in Hanafi usul.

Key Terms
شرعي (shar'i): A legal condition that is a prerequisite for an act of worship, like wudu for prayer.
عقلي ('aqli): A rational condition, like life being a condition for knowledge.
عرفي ('urfi): A customary condition, like climbing being a condition for ascending the roof.
لغوي (lughawi): A linguistic condition, as in conditional sentences: existence of condition necessitates the conditioned.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which type of condition is characterized by 'the existence of the condition necessitates the existence of the conditioned'?
A) Shar'i
B) 'Aqli
C) 'Urfi
D) Lughawi

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi concept of 'shart' (condition) is divided into four types, each with a distinct logical relationship between condition and conditioned, and must not be confused with cause (sabab) or preventer (mani').

The Conditional Word 'Law' in Divorce: Hanafi Acceptance vs. Opposition
Dispute over validity of 'law' as conditional particle for divorce

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school accepts 'law' as a valid conditional particle for divorce and evaluate the opposing view that it negates the oath.

Core Idea
Though 'law' implies non-existence, the Hanafi school treats it as a conditional for anticipated events.

The word 'law' (if) is commonly used in Arabic for hypothetical situations, but its legal effect in conditional divorce (ta'liq) is debated. The author of Kanz al-Daqa'iq omitted 'law' from his list of conditional words, suggesting it cannot form a valid oath because it indicates certainty of non-existence. However, the Hanafi madhhab, as recorded by later authorities like al-Tamartashi and Abu Yusuf, accepts 'law' as a conditional particle that suspends divorce upon the occurrence of the condition. This lesson unpacks the dispute, the evidence, and the practical consequences.

Guided Path
1
State the issue
Identify the central question: can the word 'law' be used to create a conditional divorce (ta'liq)? The author of Kanz omitted it; the Hanafi school includes it.
The dispute is about the legal validity of 'law' as a particle of condition in oaths.
2
Identify the Hanafi position and its evidence
The Hanafi position is that 'law' functions as a condition in meaning, even if not in grammatical form. Evidence includes rulings from Abu Yusuf, al-Hawi, and al-Muhit. Examples show that 'law' suspends divorce upon the occurrence of the condition.
Key example: 'You are divorced if you enter the house' – divorce occurs only upon entry.
3
Analyze the opposing view
The opposing view holds that 'law' indicates the condition is already known to be non-existent (e.g., 'if only' suggests regret for the condition's absence), so it cannot serve as a valid oath that suspends upon uncertainty.
This is the reason some scholars and the author of Kanz omitted 'law' from the list.
4
Examine the Hanafi response
The Hanafi response: 'law' is used for anticipated events (amr mutaraqqab muntazir), and thus it fits the definition of a condition (shart) that is 'at risk of existence' (ala khatar al-wujud). The madhhab accepts it based on usage and necessity.
Al-Muhit explicitly states that 'law' is used for something anticipated.
5
Determine the practical consequence
The practical difference: Hanafis enforce conditional divorce with 'law' (e.g., upon entry, divorce occurs); those who reject 'law' would not consider such an utterance as a valid ta'liq, so no divorce would occur even if the condition is met.
This is the thamarah al-khilaf.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال قبله : إنما قال ألفاظ الشرط دون حروفه كما قال بعضهم لأن عامتها اسم كـ«متى» و «إذا» ا هـ . وليس مقصود المؤلف الحصر الألفاظ الستة وقد ذكر في جوامع الفقه «لو» ولو «لا». وفي فتح القدير : وإنما لم يذكر المصنف «لو» لأن مقصوده ينافيه أعني التعليق على ما على خطر الوجود لأنها أفادت تحقق عدمه فلا يحصل معنى اليمين ولعدم حصوله لم تذكر «لما» وإن كان «لو» دخلت فأنت طالق تعليق للطلاق كما ذكره التمرتاشي ويروى عن أبي يوسف لكنه ليس معناها الأصلي ولا المشهور ولذا قال بعضهم : لا يتعلق. وفي الحاوي في فروعنا : قال أنت طالق لو تزوجتك تطلق إذا تزوجها، ولو قال أنت طالق لولا دخولك أو لولا أبوك أو مهرك لا يقع، وكذا في الإخبار بأن قال طلقتك أمس لولا كذا ا هـ . ولا محل للتردد لأن المذهب أن «لو» بمعنى الشرط. قال في المحيط وكلمة «لو» بمعنى الشرط فإنها تستعمل هذه الكلمة لأمر مترقب منتظر فصار بمعنى الشرط الذي هو مترقب الثبوت وعلى خطر الوجود فتوقف عليه حتى لو قال لامرأته أنت طالق لو دخلت الدار لم تطلق حتى تدخل، ولو قال أنت طالق لو حسن خلقك سوف أراجعك طلقت الساعة لأن لو دخلت على المراجعة، وكذا لو قدم أبوك راجعتك. وعن أبي يوسف أنت طالق لو دخلت الدار لطلقتك فهذا رجل حلف بطلاق امرأته ليطلقها إن دخلت الدار فإذا دخلت لزمه أن يطلقها ولا يقع إلا بموت أحدهما كقوله إن لم آت البصرة ا هـ . وفي المعراج وإنما لم يذكر المصنف كلمة «لو» مع أنها للشرط وضعاً ذكره في شرح المفصل باعتبار أنه يعمل عمل الشرط معنى لا لفظاً وغيرها يعمل معنى ولفظاً حتى تجزم في مواضع الجزم وفي غير مواضع الجزم لزم دخول الفاء في جزائهن بخلاف «لو» انتهى. ولم يذكر «من» مع من الجوازم ومعنى ومن مسائلها فرع غريب في المعراج : أنها لفظاً غيرها فيقتصر على ما مر قوله : (والمعتبر من المانع وجوده لأنه ما يلزم من وجوده العدم فالمعتبر في المنع وجوده إذ لا يلزم من عدمه وجود والشرط بالعكس فيلزم من عدمه العدم ولا يلزم من وجوده الوجود فالمعتبر عدمه، وأما السبب فيلزم من وجوده الوجود ومن عدمه العدم لكن هذا في المساوي وإلا فقد يكون له أسباب فلا يلزم من عدم أحدها عدم تأمل .
And he said before it: He only said 'words of condition' without 'particles of condition' as some said, because most of them are nouns like 'mata' and 'idha'. End. And the author's intent is not to limit to six words; he has mentioned in Jami' al-Fiqh 'law' and 'law la'. In Fath al-Qadir: The author did not mention 'law' because his purpose contradicts it, namely suspension upon something that is at risk of existence, because 'law' indicates the realization of its non-existence, so the meaning of oath is not obtained, and because it is not obtained, 'lama' is not mentioned either. Even if 'law' is used (e.g., 'if you enter, you are divorced'), it is a suspension of divorce as mentioned by al-Tamartashi and narrated from Abu Yusuf, but it is not its original or common meaning, and therefore some said it does not suspend. In al-Hawi from our branches: He said 'you are divorced if I marry you' – she divorces when he marries her. If he said 'you are divorced were it not for your entry' or 'were it not for your father' or 'your mahr', it does not occur, similarly in reporting that he said 'I divorced you yesterday were it not for such-and-such'. End. There is no place for hesitation because the madhhab is that 'law' is in the meaning of condition. He said in al-Muhit: The word 'law' is in the meaning of condition; it is used for something anticipated and expected, so it becomes in the meaning of condition that is anticipated to occur and at risk of existence, so it is suspended on it. So if he said to his wife 'you are divorced if you enter the house', she is not divorced until she enters. If he said 'you are divorced if your character becomes good, I will take you back', she is divorced immediately because 'law' is attached to taking back. Similarly, if he said 'if your father comes, I will take you back'. From Abu Yusuf: 'you are divorced if you enter the house, I would divorce you' – this is a man who swore by divorce of his wife that he would divorce her if she entered the house; when she enters, it becomes necessary for him to divorce her, and it does not occur except by the death of one of them, like his statement 'if I do not come to Basra'. End. In al-Mi'raj: The author did not mention the word 'law' although it is for condition by placement; he mentioned in Sharh al-Mufassal that it works as a condition in meaning but not in wording, while others work both in meaning and wording, so that they cause jussive in places of jussive and in other places require the entry of 'fa' in their answers, unlike 'law'. End. And he did not mention 'man' among the jussive particles, and its meaning, and among its issues is a strange branch in al-Mi'raj: it (man) is wording different from others, so it is limited to what has passed. His statement: (And what is considered from the preventer is its existence, because what necessitates non-existence from its existence, so what is considered in prevention is its existence, since non-existence does not necessitate existence; and the condition is the opposite, so its non-existence necessitates non-existence, but its existence does not necessitate existence; so what is considered is its non-existence. As for the cause, its existence necessitates existence, and its non-existence necessitates non-existence, but this is in equivalent cases; otherwise, there may be multiple causes, so non-existence of one does not necessitate non-existence. Ponder.)
Source pages p. 17

Commentary
This extended passage addresses the status of the word 'law' (if) in conditional divorce. The author of Kanz al-Daqa'iq listed six conditional words but omitted 'law'. The commentator explains why: 'law' suggests that the condition is already known to be non-existent, which contradicts the nature of an oath (yamin) that suspends upon something uncertain. However, the Hanafi madhhab accepts 'law' as a conditional word for ta'liq, as evidenced by rulings from Abu Yusuf and the texts of al-Tamartashi and al-Hawi. The passage clarifies that 'law' works as a condition in meaning but not in grammar (it does not cause jussive mood). Examples illustrate: if one says 'you are divorced if you enter the house,' she divorces upon entry; if 'you are divorced if your character improves, I will take you back,' she is divorced immediately because the condition is attached to taking back, not the divorce. The passage also contrasts 'law' with other particles that are both meaning and wording. Finally, it recaps the distinction between mani' (preventer), shart (condition), and sabab (cause) from the previous lesson, emphasizing that a preventer's existence blocks the ruling, a condition's non-existence blocks it, and a cause's existence brings it about. This is a key usul concept for understanding legal causality in Hanafi fiqh.

Key Terms
لو (law): A conditional particle meaning 'if', used for anticipated or expected events, accepted in the madhhab for ta'liq.
تعليق (ta'liq): Suspending a legal effect (like divorce) on the occurrence of a future condition.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Immediately upon utterance
B) When she enters the house
C) Never, because 'law' indicates the condition is impossible
D) Only if he also says 'I swear by divorce'

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, 'law' (if) is a valid conditional particle for divorce, and divorce takes effect upon occurrence of the condition.
