The Four Types of Conditions in Hanafi Legal Theory
Defines four condition types and their logical relationships

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the four types of conditions (shar'i, 'aqli, 'urfi, lughawi) and explain the logical relationship of each, as well as differentiate between shart, sabab, and mani'.

Core Idea
A condition's absence prevents the ruling; a cause's presence brings it; a preventer's presence blocks it. Not all conditions necessitate their result.

This passage presents a foundational classification in Hanafi usul: the four types of conditions. Understanding these distinctions is crucial for correctly applying rulings in fiqh, especially in chapters like divorce (talaq) where conditional oaths are frequent. We will unpack each type and the logical relationships involved.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four types of conditions
List the four types: shar'i (legal), 'aqli (rational), 'urfi (customary), lughawi (linguistic). Note that the passage also calls 'urfi 'adi (ordinary).
Each type has a unique logical relationship between condition and conditioned.
2
Analyze the logical relationship for each type
For shar'i: existence of condition is necessary but not sufficient for the ruling. For 'aqli: condition is necessary and sufficient in one direction (e.g., knowledge necessitates life, but not vice versa). For 'urfi: condition is necessary and sufficient in one direction (e.g., ladder for climbing). For lughawi: condition necessitates the conditioned (e.g., in conditional divorce).
This is the core of the classification.
3
Distinguish shart, sabab, and mani'
The passage concludes by defining the roles: the existence of mani' prevents the ruling; the non-existence of shart prevents the ruling; the existence or non-existence of sabab (cause) brings the ruling.
This clarifies the functional difference between these three concepts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الطلاق باب التعليق
في شرعية وعقلية وعرفية ولغوية. فالشرعية كالوضوء وستر العورة واستقبال القبلة وطهارة الثوب والمكان واليدن فيتوقف وجود الصلاة عليها ولا يلزم من وجودها وجود الصلاة. والعقلي كالحياة مع العلم فيلزم من وجود العلم الحياة من غير عكس. والعرفية ويقال لها الشرطية العادية كالسلم مع صعود السطح فيلزم من الصعود وجوده من غير عكس. واللغوية مثل التعليقات فيلزم من وجود الشرط وجود المشروط . قالوا : وهو حقيقة السبب وبهذا قال النحويون في الشرط والجزاء مع السببية للأول والمسببة للثاني والمعتبر من المانع وجوده ومن الشرط عدمه ومن السبب وجوده وعدمه ا هـ .
Book of Divorce: Chapter of Conditional Oaths. [Conditions] are: Shar'i (legal), 'Aqli (rational), 'Urfi (customary), and Lughawi (linguistic). The shar'i is like wudu, covering the 'awrah, facing the qibla, purity of garment, place, and body; the existence of prayer is dependent on them, but their existence does not necessitate the existence of prayer. The 'aqli is like life with knowledge; the existence of knowledge necessitates life, not vice versa. The 'urfi, also called 'adi (ordinary), is like the ladder with climbing the roof; climbing necessitates existence [of the ladder] without vice versa. The lughawi is like conditional oaths; the existence of the condition necessitates the existence of the conditioned. They said: It is the reality of cause, and the grammarians said this regarding the condition and its result, with causality for the first and being caused for the second. What is considered from the preventer (mani') is its existence, from the condition (shart) its non-existence, and from the cause (sabab) its existence or non-existence.
Source pages p. 17

Commentary
This passage is a definition and classification of conditions (shurut) in Hanafi legal theory. The author distinguishes four types of conditions that affect the validity of acts of worship and other rulings. Shar'i conditions are legal prerequisites like purity before prayer; they are necessary but not sufficient. 'Aqli conditions are rational necessities like life before knowledge. 'Urfi conditions are customary such as a ladder being necessary for climbing. Lughawi conditions are linguistic dependencies such as the existence of a condition entailing its consequent in conditional sentences. The passage also clarifies the distinction between condition (shart), cause (sabab), and preventer (mani'): a condition's non-existence prevents the ruling, a cause's existence brings about the ruling, and a preventer's existence blocks the ruling.

Key Terms
الشرط (al-shart): A condition that must be present for the existence of the ruling; its absence prevents the ruling.
السبب (al-sabab): A cause that necessitates the ruling when present; its absence does not necessitate the absence of the ruling.
المانع (al-mani'): A preventer that blocks the ruling when present; its absence allows the ruling.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) The existence of the condition does not necessitate the consequence.
C) The non-existence of the consequence necessitates the existence of the condition.
D) The relationship is the same as shar'i conditions.

Key Takeaway
The four types of conditions (shar'i, 'aqli, 'urfi, lughawi) have distinct logical relationships, and understanding them is essential for correctly applying conditional rulings in Hanafi fiqh.

The Particle 'Law' in Conditional Divorce
Analyzes the particle 'law' as a conditional in divorce oaths, with rulings and examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the particle 'law' in conditional divorce and apply it to distinguish valid and invalid ta'liq.

Core Idea
'Law' is a conditional particle in meaning: it suspends divorce on a possible future event, but not on past impossibilities.

This lesson examines the legal treatment of the particle 'law' (لو) in conditional divorce. While grammatically 'law' often implies impossibility, Hanafi jurists, particularly based on a narration from Abu Yusuf, treat it as a conditional particle that can create a valid suspension (ta'liq) for future possible events. We will explore the textual evidence, the intra-madhhab difference, and practical examples to apply this ruling correctly.

Guided Path
1
1. Identify the Ruling
State the Hanafi position: 'law' can create a valid conditional divorce (ta'liq) when used for a possible future event. The divorce does not occur immediately but is suspended upon the condition.
This ruling is based on a riwaya from Abu Yusuf, not on the original linguistic meaning of 'law'.
2
2. Trace the Evidence
Identify the source of this ruling: a narration from Abu Yusuf, supported by the reasoning in al-Muhit that 'law' is used for an expected matter (amr mutaraqqab muntazar).
The evidence is not from Quran or Sunnah but from Hanafi juristic analysis and riwaya.
3
3. Apply the Distinction
Distinguish between 'law' used for a possible future condition (e.g., 'You are divorced law I marry you' – divorce occurs if marriage takes place) and 'law' used for an impossible or past condition (e.g., 'You are divorced law for your father' – no divorce).
Key test: Is the condition something that can realistically happen in the future?
4
4. Examine the Ikhtilaf
Note the intra-Hanafi disagreement: some earlier Hanafis rejected the conditional nature of 'law' because its original meaning is not conditional. The relied-upon (mufta bihi) position is that it is conditional for possible events.
Al-Timirtashi and later Hanafi authorities accept the riwaya from Abu Yusuf.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإنما لم يذكر المصنف «لو» لأن مقصوده ينافيه أعني التعليق على ما على خطر الوجود لأنها أفادت تحقق عدمه فلا يحصل معنى اليمين ولعدم حصوله لم تذكر «لما» وإن كان «لو» دخلت فأنت طالق تعليق للطلاق كما ذكره التمرتاشي ويروى عن أبي يوسف لكنه ليس معناها الأصلي ولا المشهور ولذا قال بعضهم : لا يتعلق. وفي الحاوي في فروعنا : قال أنت طالق لو تزوجتك تطلق إذا تزوجها، ولو قال أنت طالق لولا دخولك أو لولا أبوك أو مهرك لا يقع، وكذا في الإخبار بأن قال طلقتك أمس لولا كذا ا هـ . ولا محل للتردد لأن المذهب أن «لو» بمعنى الشرط. قال في المحيط وكلمة «لو» بمعنى الشرط فإنها تستعمل هذه الكلمة لأمر مترقب منتظر فصار بمعنى الشرط الذي هو مترقب الثبوت وعلى خطر الوجود فتوقف عليه حتى لو قال لامرأته أنت طالق لو دخلت الدار لم تطلق حتى تدخل، ولو قال أنت طالق لو حسن خلقك سوف أراجعك طلقت الساعة لأن لو دخلت على المراجعة، وكذا لو قدم أبوك راجعتك. وعن أبي يوسف أنت طالق لو دخلت الدار لطلقتك فهذا رجل حلف بطلاق امرأته ليطلقها إن دخلت الدار فإذا دخلت لزمه أن يطلقها ولا يقع إلا بموت أحدهما كقوله إن لم آت البصرة ا هـ . وفي المعراج وإنما لم يذكر المصنف كلمة «لو» مع أنها للشرط وضعاً ذكره في شرح المفصل باعتبار أنه يعمل عمل الشرط معنى لا لفظاً وغيرها يعمل معنى ولفظاً
The author did not mention 'law' because his purpose contradicts it, i.e., conditional oaths upon something with risk of existence, because it (law) indicates the verification of its non-existence, so the meaning of an oath is not achieved. Due to its non-occurrence, 'lama' was not mentioned either. Although 'law' entered, 'You are divorced law' is a conditional divorce as al-Timirtashi mentioned, and it is narrated from Abu Yusuf, but it is not its original nor common meaning, so some said: it does not occur. In al-Hawi in our branches: if he said 'You are divorced law I marry you', she is divorced if he marries her; if he said 'You are divorced law for your entry' or 'law for your father' or 'your dowry', it does not occur; similarly in reporting: 'I divorced you yesterday law for something' etc. There is no room for hesitation because the madhhab is that 'law' is conditional. In al-Muhit: the word 'law' is conditional; it is used for an expected matter, so it becomes conditional, having a risk of existence, so the divorce depends on it. If he says to his wife 'You are divorced law you enter the house', she is not divorced until she enters. If he says 'You are divorced law your character improves, I will take you back', she is divorced immediately because 'law' entered upon returning; similarly 'if your father comes, I take you back'. From Abu Yusuf: 'You are divorced law you enter the house I will divorce you' – this is a man who swore by divorcing his wife to divorce her if she enters, so if she enters, he must divorce her, and it does not occur except by death of one of them, like his saying 'if I do not come to Basra'. In al-Mi'raj: the author did not mention the word 'law' though it is conditional by usage; in Sharh al-Mufassal it is mentioned that it works as a condition in meaning not in wording, while others work both in meaning and wording.
Source pages p. 17

Commentary
This passage discusses the particle 'law' (لو) in conditional divorce. The author explains why al-Marghinani (or al-Quduri) did not include 'law' in his list of conditional particles: because 'law' often implies the impossibility or non-existence of the condition, which contradicts the purpose of ta'liq (suspension on a possible event). However, the Hanafi madhhab accepts that 'law' can function as a conditional particle, as reported from Abu Yusuf. Examples are given: 'You are divorced law I marry you' – if he marries her, divorce occurs; 'law for your father' does not cause divorce. There is also a distinction between 'law' used to express a past unreal condition (which does not result in divorce) and 'law' used to express a future possible condition (which does). The passage also cites al-Muhit and al-Mi'raj to clarify the grammatical and legal treatment of 'law'. The relied-upon position is that 'law' is a conditional particle in meaning, and therefore it can create a valid ta'liq.

Key Terms
لو (law): A conditional particle that denotes an expected condition with a risk of existence, but often implies its non-fulfillment.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi madhhab relies on the riwaya from Abu Yusuf to treat 'law' as a conditional particle for future possible events, making divorce suspended upon fulfillment.

Repetition of Divorce in Group Conditions
Analyzes whether a condition addressed to a group is repeated for each occurrence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that a condition addressed to a group is repeated for each occurrence, identify the proof from Quran 5:95 and Imam Muhammad's example, and explain the tarjih over opposing views.

Core Idea
When addressed to a group, 'man' in a conditional triggers repetition of the consequence with each occurrence.

This lesson examines a specific branch of conditional divorce: if a man says to his wives 'Whoever of you enters the house is divorced,' and one wife enters multiple times, does she receive one divorce or multiple? The Hanafi school, based on custom, Quranic usage, and the practice of the Prophet's companions, holds that the divorce repeats with each entry. We will walk through the proof and address the disagreement with some Hanbalis and grammarians.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Scenario and the Hanafi Position
Read the first part of the passage: a man says to his wives 'Whoever of you enters the house is divorced.' One wife enters repeatedly. State the Hanafi ruling explicitly: she is divorced each time.
2
Locate the Primary Dalil
The passage cites Quran 5:95: 'And whoever among you kills it intentionally...' (man qatalahu minkum). Understand how this verse indicates generalization of the consequence to each act of killing.
The lam istighraq in 'al-sayd' makes the prohibition general, but the condition 'man' is repeated.
3
Examine Imam Muhammad's Example from al-Siyar al-Kabir
Imam Muhammad gives the case of a commander saying 'Whoever kills a slain, he gets his spoils.' If one kills two, he gets both spoils. This shows that 'man' in a conditional statement implies repetition.
This is a clear precedent from the Zahir al-Riwayah.
4
Address the Opposing Argument
Some Hanbalis and grammarians claim that only 'kullama' necessitates repetition. The passage responds by showing that 'idha' in the Quran (e.g., 6:68, 6:54) indicates repetition, and that 'man' can also carry that meaning in context.
The author notes that grammarians do not consider 'kullama' a conditional particle; jurists include it due to its conditional meaning.
5
Conclude with Tarjih
The Hanafi position is preferred because it is based on custom, revealed texts, and the practice of the early community. Therefore, the mufta bihi is that the condition repeats with each occurrence.
This ruling is from Zahir al-Riwayah, specifically Imam Muhammad's statement.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
رجل قال لنسوة له من دخلت منكن الدار فهي طالق فدخلت واحدة مراراً طلقت بكل مرة لأن الدخول لما أضيف إلى جماعة فيراد به تعميمه عرفاً مرة بعد مرة كقوله تعالى ومن قتله منكم متعمداً [المائدة : ۹٥] فإنه أفاد عموم الصيد ولهذا ذكر محمد في السير الكبير: لو قال الأمير من قتل قتيلاً فله سلبه فقتل واحد قتليين فله سلبهما. قيل لا حجة لمحمد في الاستشهادين لأن الصيد في قوله لا تقتلوا الصيد عام باعتبار اللام الاستغراقية والقتيل عام لوقوعه في سياق الشرط ولو استشهد بقوله تعالى وإذا رأيت الذين يخوضون﴾ [الأنعام: ٦٨] الآية وإذا جاءك الذين يؤمنون بآياتنا ﴾ [الأنعام: ٥٤] الآية. فإن «إذا» في ذلك تفيد التكرار. وعن بعض الحنابلة أن متى تقتضي التكرار والصحيح أن غير «كلما» لا يوجب التكرار ا.هـ. والحاصل أن أدوات الشرط إن و «من» و «ما» و «مهما» و «أي» و «أين» و «أنى» و «متی» و «متی ما» و «حيث» و «حيثما» و «إذا» و «إذا ما» و «إيان» و «كيفما» عند الكوفيين ولم يذكر النحاة كلا» و «كلما فيها لأنهما ليسا من أدوات الشرط وإنما ذكرهما الفقهاء لثبوت معنى الشرط معهما وهو التعليق بأمر على خطر الوجود وهو الفعل الواقع صفة الاسم الذي أضيف إليه. قالوا: وكلها جازمة إلا «لو» و «إذا» والمشهور أنه إنما يجزم بـ«إذا» في الشعر وكذا «لو» . والمراد بـ«إن المكسورة فلو فتحها تنجز وهو قول الجمهور لأنها للتعليل، ولا يشترط وجود العلة وهذا مذهب البصريين واختاره محمد. ومذهب الكوفيين أنها بمعنى إذا واختاره الكسائي وهو منهم وتمامه في المعراج. وأشار بقوله «ألفاظ الشرط إلى أنه لا يتحقق التعليق إلا بالفاء في الجواب في موضع وجوبها إلا يتقدم الجواب فيتعلق بدونها على خلاف في أنه حينئذ هو الجواب أو يضمر الجواب بعده والمقدم دليله. وأما الفقيه فنظره من جهة المعنى فلا عليه من اعتبار الجواب كذا في فتح القدير. وكون الأول هو الجواب مذهب الكوفيين وكونه دليلاً عليه مذهب البصريين. فإن قلت: ما فائدة الاختلاف بين أهل البلدين؟ قلت: يجوز عند البصريين «ضربت غلامه إن ضربت زيداً اعلى إن ضمير غلامه لزيد لرتبة الجزاء عند البصريين بعد الشرط، ولا يجوز عند الكوفيين لرتبته قبل الأداة كما أشار إليه الرضي. وفي الألفية لابن مالك .
A man said to his wives: 'Whoever of you enters the house is divorced.' Then one entered repeatedly; she is divorced each time because the entry, when attributed to a group, is intended to be generalized customarily time after time, like His statement 'And whoever among you kills it intentionally' [5:95]; it indicates generality of hunting. For this reason Muhammad mentioned in al-Siyar al-Kabir: If the commander says 'Whoever kills a slain, he gets his spoils,' then someone kills two slain, he gets both of their spoils. It was said: There is no proof for Muhammad in these two examples because 'the hunted' in 'Do not kill the hunted' is general due to the lam istighraq, and 'the slain' is general due to occurring in the context of a condition. If he had cited His statement 'And when you see those who engage in idle talk' [6:68] and 'And when those who believe in Our verses come to you' [6:54], then 'idha' in those indicates repetition. From some Hanbalis: 'mata' necessitates repetition; the correct view is that other than 'kullama' does not necessitate repetition. In summary, the conditional particles are: in, man, ma, mahma, ay, ayna, anna, mata, mata ma, haythu, haythuma, idha, idha ma, iyaan, kayfama according to the Kufans. The grammarians did not mention 'kulla' and 'kullama' because they are not conditional particles; the jurists mentioned them because the meaning of condition is established with them, i.e., suspension upon something with risk of existence, which is the verb that is the attribute of the noun it is added to. They said: All are jussive except 'law' and 'idha'; the well-known view is that 'idha' is only jussive in poetry, as is 'law'. The meaning of 'in' with kasra: if it is fatha, it is immediate (tanjiz), which is the opinion of the majority because it is for causal explanation, and the existence of the cause is not required; that is the Basran madhhab and Muhammad chose it. The Kufan madhhab is that it means 'idha', and al-Kisa'i chose it. Its completion is in al-Mi'raj. His statement 'words of condition' indicates that ta'liq is only realized with the fa in the answer in places where it is obligatory, unless the answer precedes, then it is suspended without the fa, with a disagreement on whether then the answer is the answer or the answer is implicit thereafter and the preceding is its evidence. The faqih looks at the meaning, so he does not care about considering the answer, as in Fath al-Qadir. That the first is the answer is the Kufan madhhab; that it is evidence for it is the Basran madhhab. If you ask: What is the benefit of the difference between the two groups? I say: According to the Basrans, it is permissible to say 'I beat his slave if I beat Zayd' because the pronoun of his slave refers to Zayd due to the rank of the answer after the condition according to the Basrans; it is not permissible according to the Kufans because its rank is before the particle, as al-Radi indicated. In the Alfiyya of Ibn Malik.
Source pages p. 17

Commentary
This passage delves into a specific branch: if a man addresses his wives with a conditional statement using 'man' (whoever), such as 'whoever of you enters the house is divorced,' and one woman enters multiple times, she is divorced each time because the condition is generalized to each occurrence. The author provides proof from the Quran (5:95) where 'man qatalahu minkum muta'ammidan' indicates that each killing incurs a penalty, implying repetition. He also cites Imam Muhammad's example from al-Siyar al-Kabir regarding spoils of war. There is a rebuttal that the Quranic examples are different due to grammatical generality, but the author defends the position by citing other verses with 'idha' that indicate repetition. The passage then lists all the conditional particles recognized by the Kufan grammarians and notes that 'kulla' and 'kullama' are not strictly conditional particles but are used by jurists because they imply a conditional meaning. It also discusses the jussive nature of these particles, noting that 'law' and 'idha' are not jussive except in poetry. Finally, it addresses a grammatical debate between Basran and Kufan scholars about the order of condition and answer, and its practical implication in permissibility of pronoun reference.

Key Terms
التكرار (al-takrar): The repetition of the condition's consequence (divorce) each time the condition is fulfilled.
كلما (kullama): A particle that inherently implies repetition of the condition and its consequence.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a man says to his wives 'Whoever of you enters the house is divorced' and one woman enters twice, how many divorces occur according to the Hanafi school?
A) One divorce.
B) Two divorces.
C) No divorce because the condition is not repeated.
D) One divorce, but it becomes irrevocable.

Key Takeaway
When a conditional divorce is addressed to a group using 'man', each occurrence of the condition triggers a separate divorce according to the Hanafi school, based on custom and revealed texts.

Talaq — Divorce

Repeated Conditional Divorce: Ruling and Linguistic Basis
Explaining Hanafi ruling on repeated divorce due to general conditional phrase

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on repeated conditional divorce when a general conditional phrase is used and explain the linguistic principle of generality by cause (al-umum bi al-illah) that supports it.

Core Idea
A general conditional phrase triggers divorce each time the condition is fulfilled due to generality of cause, not wording.

Conditional divorce (ta'liq al-talaq) can lead to multiple divorces if the condition is phrased generally. The Hanafi school derives this from Quranic usage and the principle that repetition follows from the cause (illah) rather than the literal wording. This lesson unpacks the ruling, its proofs, and the underlying grammatical principles.

Guided Path
1
Identify the case and the ruling
The passage begins with a specific case: a man says to his wives 'Whichever of you enters the house is divorced.' One wife enters multiple times. The Hanafi ruling is that each entry triggers a separate divorce.
The phrase 'whichever of you' (man dakhala min kunna) is general, attaching entry to a plural group.
2
Examine the Quranic proof
The author cites Quran 5:95 (man qatalahu) and 6:68, 6:54 (idha) to show that general conditional expressions imply repetition. In 6:68, 'idha' with a general noun (the ones who engage in discourse) indicates the prohibition applies each time.
The Hanafi emphasis is on the generality of the noun (al-'umum bi al-ism) not the particle itself.
3
Understand the example from al-Siyar al-Kabir
Imam Muhammad gives the example of a commander saying 'Whoever kills a slain, for him is its spoils.' If one kills two slain, he gets both spoils. This illustrates repetition due to the general conditional 'man'.
This example from Zahir al-Riwayah supports the ruling.
4
Distinguish between conditional particles
The passage lists conditional tools and notes that only 'kullama' (whenever) necessitates repetition by itself. Other particles like 'man' and 'idha' may imply repetition due to the generality of the noun they qualify. The Hanafi position is affirmed against some Hanbalis who claim 'mata' implies repetition.
The principle 'al-umum bi al-illah la bi al-sighah' (generality by cause not by wording) is key.
5
Apply the principle to the ruling
The ruling that each entry causes a divorce is based on the generality of the cause (the entry) across multiple occurrences, not on the specific particle used. The cause is repeated, so the consequence repeats.
This principle also governs other areas of fiqh where cause-based generality applies.

رجل قال لنسوة له من دخلت منكن الدار فهي طالق فدخلت واحدة مراراً طلقت بكل مرة لأن الدخول لما أضيف إلى جماعة فيراد به تعميمه عرفاً مرة بعد مرة كقوله تعالى ومن قتله منكم متعمداً [المائدة : ۹٥] فإنه أفاد عموم الصيد ولهذا ذكر محمد في السير الكبير: لو قال الأمير من قتل قتيلاً فله سلبه فقتل واحد قتليين فله سلبهما. قيل لا حجة لمحمد في الاستشهادين لأن الصيد في قوله لا تقتلوا الصيد عام باعتبار اللام الاستغراقية والقتيل عام لوقوعه في سياق الشرط ولو استشهد بقوله تعالى وإذا رأيت الذين يخوضون﴾ [الأنعام: ٦٨] الآية وإذا جاءك الذين يؤمنون بآياتنا ﴾ [الأنعام: ٥٤] الآية. فإن «إذا» في ذلك تفيد التكرار. وعن بعض الحنابلة أن متى تقتضي التكرار والصحيح أن غير «كلما» لا يوجب التكرار ا.هـ. والحاصل أن أدوات الشرط إن و «من» و «ما» و «مهما» و «أي» و «أين» و «أنى» و «متی» و «متی ما» و «حيث» و «حيثما» و «إذا» و «إذا ما» و «إيان» و «كيفما» عند الكوفيين ولم يذكر النحاة كلا» و «كلما فيها لأنهما ليسا من أدوات الشرط وإنما ذكرهما الفقهاء لثبوت معنى الشرط معهما وهو التعليق بأمر على خطر الوجود وهو الفعل الواقع صفة الاسم الذي أضيف إليه. قالوا: وكلها جازمة إلا «لو» و «إذا» والمشهور أنه إنما يجزم بـ«إذا» في الشعر وكذا «لو» . والمراد بـ«إن المكسورة فلو فتحها تنجز وهو قول الجمهور لأنها للتعليل، ولا يشترط وجود العلة وهذا مذهب البصريين واختاره محمد. ومذهب الكوفيين أنها بمعنى إذا واختاره الكسائي وهو منهم وتمامه في المعراج. وأشار بقوله «ألفاظ الشرط إلى أنه لا يتحقق التعليق إلا بالفاء في الجواب في موضع وجوبها إلا يتقدم الجواب فيتعلق بدونها على خلاف في أنه حينئذ هو الجواب أو يضمر الجواب بعده والمقدم دليله. وأما الفقيه فنظره من جهة المعنى فلا عليه من اعتبار الجواب كذا في فتح القدير. وكون الأول هو الجواب مذهب الكوفيين وكونه دليلاً عليه مذهب البصريين. فإن قلت: ما فائدة الاختلاف بين أهل البلدين؟ قلت: يجوز عند البصريين «ضربت غلامه إن ضربت زيداً اعلى إن ضمير غلامه لزيد لرتبة الجزاء عند البصريين بعد الشرط، ولا يجوز عند الكوفيين لرتبته قبل الأداة كما أشار إليه الرضي. وفي الألفية لابن مالك : 
واقرن بفا حتماً جواباً لو جعل شرطا لان أو غيرها لم ينجعل وتوضيحه كما في «المغني» أنها واجبة في جواب لا يصلح أن يكون شرطاً قال : وهو منحصر في ست مسائل إحداها أن يكون الجواب جملة اسمية نحو إن تعذبهم فإنهم عبادك [المائدة: ۱۱۸] الثانية أن يكون فعلها جامداً نحو إن تبدو الصدقات فنعما هي [البقرة: ۲۷۱] الثالثة أن يكون فعلها انشائياً نحو إن كنتم تحبون الله فاتبعوني [آل عمران: ۳۱] الرابعة أن يكون فعلها ماضياً لفظاً ومعنى نحو إن يسرق فقد سرق أخ له من قبل [يوسف: ٧٧] الخامسة أن يقترن بحرف الاستقبال نحو من يرتدد منكم عن دينه فسوف يأتي الله بقوم يحبهم ﴾ [البقرة : ۲۱۷] ونحو وما يفعلوا من خير فلن يكفروه) [آل عمران: ١١٥] السادسة أن يقترن بحرف له الصدر كرب وإنما دخلت في نحو ومن عاد فينتقم الله منه [المائدة : ٩٥] لتقدير الفعل خبر المحذوف فالجملة اسمية وقد مر أن «إذا» الفجائية تنوب عن الفاء نحو وإن تصبهم سيئة بما قدمت أيديهم إذ هم يقنطون [الروم: ٣٦] وإن الفاء قد تحذف للضرورة كقوله :
من يفعل الحسنات الله يشكرها
وعن المبرد أنه منع من ذلك حتى في الشعر وزعم أن الرواية «من يفعل الخير فالرحمن يشكره». وعن الأخفش أن ذلك واقع في النثر الفصيح وأن منه قوله تعالى إن ترك خيراً الوصية للوالدين ﴾ [البقرة : ۱۸۰] وتقدم تأويله. وقال ابن مالك : يجوز في النثر نادراً ومنه حديث اللقطة فإن جاء صاحبها وإلا استمتع بها » (۱) وكما تربط الفاء الجواب بشرطه كذلك تربط شبه الجواب بشبه الشرط وذلك في نحو الذي يأتيني فله درهم» ا هـ ما في المغني: وذكر المرادي في شرح الألفية أحد عشر موضعاً لوجوب الاقتران بالفاء وهي: الجملة الاسمية والفعلية الطلبية والفعل غير المتصرف والمقرون بالسين أو سوف أو قد أو منفياً بما أو لن وإن والمقرون بالقسم المقرون برب قال : فهذه الأجوبة تلزمها الفاء لأنها لا يصلح جعلها شرطاً وخطيب التمثيل سهل ا هـ . وهذا لا يخالف قول المغني أنها منحصرة في ست لأن حرف الاستقبال شامل للسين وسوف ولن وماله الصدر شامل للقسم ورب. والأضبط
أن العموم بالعلة لا بالصيغة فيهما من ترتب الحكم وهو الجزاء في الأول ومنع القعود على المشتق منه وهو القتل والخوض فيتكرر به .انتهت وسيأتي ذكر هذا الفرع ثانياً في القولة التي بعد هذه وأن الحق أن ما هنا على أحد القولين . قوله : (وذكر المرادي في شرح الألفية أحد عشر موضعاً) نظمها في الفتح بقوله :
(۱) رواه أبو داود في كتاب اللقطة باب .١ أحمد في مسنده (١١٥/٤) (١٢٦/٥).
Book of Divorce: Chapter on Suspension. A man said to his wives: 'Whichever of you enters the house is divorced.' One woman entered repeatedly. She is divorced each time because when the entry is attributed to a group, it is intended to be general customarily, time after time. Like His saying, exalted: 'And whoever kills it among you intentionally' [5:95], for it indicates the generality of game. For this reason Muhammad mentioned in al-Siyar al-Kabir: If the commander says 'Whoever kills a slain, for him is his spoils' and one kills two slain, he has both spoils. It is said: There is no proof for Muhammad in the two examples because 'game' in His saying 'Do not kill game' is general by the lam of totality, and 'slain' is general by occurring in the context of condition. And if he used as proof His saying 'And when you see those who engage in [offensive] discourse' [6:68] and 'And when those who believe in Our verses come to you' [6:54], for 'when' (idha) in that indicates repetition. And from some Hanbalis that 'mata' requires repetition, but the correct is that other than 'kullama' does not necessitate repetition. End. The summary is that the conditional tools are: 'in', 'man', 'ma', 'mahma', 'ayy', 'ayna', 'anna', 'mata', 'mata ma', 'haythu', 'haythuma', 'idha', 'idha ma', 'iyyan', 'kayfama' according to the Kufans, and the grammarians did not mention 'kulla' and 'kullama' among them because they are not conditional tools, but the jurists mention them because the meaning of condition is established with them, which is suspension of a matter on a risky existence, that is the verb acting as an adjective of the noun to which it is added. They said: All of them are jussive except 'law' and 'idha', and the famous is that 'idha' is only jussive in poetry, likewise 'law'. And what is meant by 'in' with kasra is that if it is with fatha it becomes immediate, and that is the view of the majority because it is for causation, and existence of the cause is not required. This is the madhhab of the Basrans and Muhammad chose it. The madhhab of the Kufans is that it means 'idha', and al-Kisa'i chose it and he is among them, and its completion is in al-Mi'raj. He indicated by his saying 'conditional phrases' that suspension is only realized by the fa in the response in the place where it is obligatory, unless the response precedes, then it is suspended without it, with a dispute whether that response is the actual response or a response is implied after it and the preceding is its evidence. As for the jurist, his view is from the meaning side, so he is not concerned with considering the response. Thus in Fath al-Qadir. And that the first is the response is the madhhab of the Kufans, and that it is evidence for it is the madhhab of the Basrans. If you say: What is the benefit of the difference between the two schools? I say: It is permissible according to the Basrans 'I beat his slave if you beat Zayd' on the basis that the pronoun of his slave refers to Zayd because the rank of the consequence according to the Basrans is after the condition, and it is not permissible according to the Kufans because its rank is before the tool as al-Radi indicated. And in Ibn Malik's Alfiyya: 'And obligatorily connect with fa the response, if [the response] is made a condition, for 'lan' or other does not become [conditional].' And its clarification as in al-Mughni: It is obligatory in a response that is not valid to be a condition. He said: It is restricted to six cases: first, the response is a nominal sentence, like 'If You punish them, indeed they are Your servants' [5:118]; second, its verb is defective, like 'If you disclose charities, then good is it' [2:271]; third, its verb is performative, like 'If you love Allah, then follow me' [3:31]; fourth, its verb is past in word and meaning, like 'If he steals, a brother of his has stolen before' [12:77]; fifth, it is accompanied by a future particle, like 'Whoever among you reverts from his religion, then Allah will bring a people He loves' [2:217], and 'And whatever good they do, never will it be denied them' [3:115]; sixth, it is accompanied by a particle that governs the nominative, like 'rabb' (Lord). And indeed the fa entered in like 'And whoever returns, then Allah will take vengeance on him' [5:95] for the estimation of the verb as the predicate of the elided [subject], so the sentence is nominal. And it has passed that 'idha' fuja'iyya substitutes for the fa, like 'And if evil touches them for what their hands have put forth, lo, they despair' [30:36]. And indeed the fa may be omitted for necessity, like the line: 'Whoever does good deeds, Allah thanks him.' And al-Mubarrad forbade that even in poetry, claiming the narration is 'Whoever does good, then the Merciful thanks him.' And al-Akhfash said that occurs in eloquent prose, including His saying 'If [the deceased] leaves wealth, the will for parents...' [2:180] and its interpretation preceded. Ibn Malik said: It is permissible in prose rarely, including the hadith of luqtah: 'Then if its owner comes, otherwise enjoy it.' And just as the fa connects the response to its condition, likewise it connects the like of a response to the like of a condition, such as in 'The one who comes to me, for him is a dirham.' End from al-Mughni. And al-Muradi mentioned in Sharh al-Alfiyya eleven positions for the obligation of connection with fa: the nominal sentence, the verbal demand sentence, the non-conjugated verb, that accompanied by sa or sawfa or qad, or negated by ma or lan or in, and that accompanied by a qasam (oath) accompanied by rabb. He said: These responses require the fa because they cannot be made conditions. And the example is easy. End. This does not contradict al-Mughni's statement that they are restricted to six, because the future particle includes sa and sawfa and lan, and what governs the nominative includes qasam and rabb. And the more precise [view] is that generality is by cause not by wording in both: from the ordering of the ruling which is the consequence in the first and the prohibition of sitting on the derived noun which is killing and engaging in discourse, so it is repeated by it. End. And this sub-branch will be mentioned again later in the coming statement, and that the truth is that what is here is one of two views. His statement: (And al-Muradi mentioned in Sharh al-Alfiyya eleven positions) al-Fath organized them in verse.
Source pages p. 18

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq is an expansion on the rules of conditional divorce (ta'liq al-talaq). The author begins with a ruling: if a man says to his wives 'Whichever of you enters the house is divorced' and one wife enters multiple times, she is divorced each time. The reasoning is that the conditional phrase uses 'man' (whoever) which, when attached to a plural pronoun ('min kunna'), is understood as general and repetitive customarily. This is supported by a Quranic verse (5:95) where 'man' indicates generality, and by Imam Muhammad's example in al-Siyar al-Kabir about a commander promising spoils for kills. The text then addresses objections: some say those examples are not general because of the definite article or conditional context. A stronger proof is from verses using 'idha' (when) which imply repetition. The author notes that some Hanbalis claim 'mata' implies repetition, but the correct Hanafi position is that only 'kullama' necessitates repetition. The passage then delves into the grammatical classification of conditional particles (adawat al-shart), listing those recognized by Kufan grammarians, and emphasizing that the jurists include 'kulla' and 'kullama' due to their conditional meaning. A key point is the necessity of the particle 'fa' in the response clause (jawab al-shart) for a valid suspension, except when the response precedes. The dispute between Basrans and Kufans about the status of the preceding response is noted, but the jurist's concern is the meaning. The author uses al-Mughni's classification of six cases requiring 'fa', and al-Muradi's eleven. Ultimately, this grammatical analysis underpins the fiqh ruling: general conditional phrases trigger repeated consequences.

Key Terms
التعليق (al-ta'liq): Suspending the occurrence of a legal consequence (like divorce) upon the fulfillment of a condition.
أدوات الشرط (adawat al-shart): Words used to introduce a condition (shart) that suspends a consequence. They include 'in', 'man', 'ma', 'mahma', etc.
الفاء (al-fa'): The particle 'fa' is mandatory in the response (jawab) of a conditional sentence when the response cannot itself be a condition. It connects the consequence to the condition.
العموم بالعلة لا بالصيغة (al-umum bi al-illah la bi al-sighah): Generality is derived from the underlying cause (illah) rather than the literal wording (sighah). Here, the repetition of divorce is due to the generality of the cause (the entry) not the particle form.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a man says to his wives, 'Whichever of you enters the house is divorced,' and one wife enters three times, how many divorces occur according to the Hanafi madhhab?
A) One divorce
B) Three divorces
C) No divorce because the condition is not repeated
D) It depends on the wording of the condition

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, a general conditional phrase results in repeated consequences each time the condition is fulfilled, based on the principle of generality by cause (al-umum bi al-illah).

The Obligation of Fa in Conditional Divorce and Its Consequences
Explains the obligation of fa and its legal effect in conditional divorce.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the grammatical rule for the obligatory fa in conditional divorce and apply the Hanafi ruling when fa is omitted.

Core Idea
Omitting the obligatory fa makes the divorce immediate, not suspended.

In conditional divorce, the particle 'fa' connects the condition to its consequence. This lesson examines the grammatical rule inherited from Arabic grammar and its legal impact. You will explore the six cases where fa is mandatory, the intra-Hanafi dispute with Abu Yusuf, and the final mufta bihi ruling.

Guided Path
1
Understand the grammatical foundation
The fa is required when the response (jawab) cannot itself be a condition. The grammatical classification determines when fa is obligatory.
This is based on Basran grammar, which the Hanafi school adopts.
2
Identify the six (or eleven) cases
Familiarize yourself with the types of sentences that require fa: nominal, frozen verb, performative, past tense, future particles, and particles with initial authority.
The passage also mentions Al-Radi's concise four categories.
3
Note the exceptions and grammatical disputes
There are cases where fa can be omitted (poetry, necessity) or substituted by other particles like idha fujaiyyah. Grammarians disagree on the permissibility of optional omission.
This dispute directly feeds into the legal ruling.
4
Trace the legal consequence
If fa is omitted where required, the conditional structure fails; the divorce becomes immediate (mutanajjiz) upon utterance.
This is the relied-upon Hanafi position (mufta bihi).
5
Examine the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Abu Yusuf holds that the omission is permissible and the divorce remains suspended, because the fa can be implied. The madhhab sides with Abu Hanifa (immediate divorce).
The dispute traces to the Basran vs. Kufan grammatical schools.

كتاب الطلاق باب التعليق
١٩
واقرن بفا حتماً جواباً لو جعل شرطا لان أو غيرها لم ينجعل وتوضيحه كما في «المغني» أنها واجبة في جواب لا يصلح أن يكون شرطاً قال : وهو منحصر في ست مسائل إحداها أن يكون الجواب جملة اسمية نحو إن تعذبهم فإنهم عبادك [المائدة: ١١٨] الثانية أن يكون فعلها جامداً نحو إن تبدو الصدقات فنعما هي [البقرة: ٢٧١] الثالثة أن يكون فعلها انشائياً نحو إن كنتم تحبون الله فاتبعوني [آل عمران: ٣١] الرابعة أن يكون فعلها ماضياً لفظاً ومعنى نحو إن يسرق فقد سرق أخ له من قبل [يوسف: ٧٧] الخامسة أن يقترن بحرف الاستقبال نحو من يرتدد منكم عن دينه فسوف يأتي الله بقوم يحبهم ﴾ [البقرة : ٢١٧] ونحو وما يفعلوا من خير فلن يكفروه) [آل عمران: ١١٥] السادسة أن يقترن بحرف له الصدر كرب وإنما دخلت في نحو ومن عاد فينتقم الله منه [المائدة : ٩٥] لتقدير الفعل خبر المحذوف فالجملة اسمية وقد مر أن «إذا» الفجائية تنوب عن الفاء نحو وإن تصبهم سيئة بما قدمت أيديهم إذ هم يقنطون [الروم: ٣٦] وإن الفاء قد تحذف للضرورة كقوله :
من يفعل الحسنات الله يشكرها
وعن المبرد أنه منع من ذلك حتى في الشعر وزعم أن الرواية «من يفعل الخير فالرحمن يشكره». وعن الأخفش أن ذلك واقع في النثر الفصيح وأن منه قوله تعالى إن ترك خيراً الوصية للوالدين ﴾ [البقرة : ١٨٠] وتقدم تأويله. وقال ابن مالك : يجوز في النثر نادراً ومنه حديث اللقطة فإن جاء صاحبها وإلا استمتع بها » (۱) وكما تربط الفاء الجواب بشرطه كذلك تربط شبه الجواب بشبه الشرط وذلك في نحو الذي يأتيني فله درهم» ا هـ ما في المغني: وذكر المرادي في شرح الألفية أحد عشر موضعاً لوجوب الاقتران بالفاء وهي: الجملة الاسمية والفعلية الطلبية والفعل غير المتصرف والمقرون بالسين أو سوف أو قد أو منفياً بما أو لن وإن والمقرون بالقسم المقرون برب قال : فهذه الأجوبة تلزمها الفاء لأنها لا يصلح جعلها شرطاً وخطيب التمثيل سهل ا هـ . وهذا لا يخالف قول المغني أنها منحصرة في ست لأن حرف الاستقبال شامل للسين وسوف ولن وماله الصدر شامل للقسم ورب. والأضبط

والأخصر ما ذكره الرضي أنها واجبة في أربعة مواضع: أحدها الجملة الطلبية كالأمر والنهي والاستفهام والتمني والعرض والتحضيض والدعاء الثاني الجملة الانشائية كنعم وبئس وما تضمن معنى انشاء المدح والذم وكذا عسى وفعل التعجب والقسم الثالث الجملة الاسمية. الرابع كل فعلية مصدرة بحرف سوى لا ولم في المضارع سواء كان الفعل المصدر ماضياً أو مضارعاً . وظاهره أن الطلبية لا تدخل تحت الانشائية ولذا صرح بعده بما يفيد التغاير فقال : إن الجملة الانشائية متجردة عن الزمان والطلبية متمحضة للاستقبال وتمامه فيه. وفي شرح التوضيح من بحث الصلة الانشائية ما قارن لفظها معناها والطلبية ما تأخر وجود معناها عن وجود لفظها اهـ. وهذا كله عند النحاة، وأما في علم المعاني فالطلبية من أقسام الانشائية لأنها ما ليس لها خارج تطابقه أو لا تطابقه والخبرية ما لها خارج تطابقه أو لا

ينتصرون
تعلم جواب الشرط حـتـم قـرانـه بفاء إذا ما فعله طلباً أتى كذا جامداً أو مقسماً كان أو بقدر رب وسيف أو بسوف اذر يا فتى أو اسمية أو كان منفي ما وإن ولن من يحد عما حددناه قد عتى
ولو
Book of Divorce, Chapter of Suspension. And link with fa necessarily the response if one makes a condition, because if [the particle] 'anna or other than it [is used] it does not become [a valid conditional response]. Its clarification is as in Al-Mughni: it is obligatory in a response that is not suitable to be a condition. He said: it is confined to six cases: first: the response is a nominal sentence, e.g., 'If You punish them, indeed they are Your servants' [5:118]; second: its verb is frozen, e.g., 'If you disclose the charities, excellent is it' [2:271]; third: its verb is performative, e.g., 'If you love Allah, then follow me' [3:31]; fourth: its verb is past in form and meaning, e.g., 'If he stole, his brother had stolen before' [12:77]; fifth: it is accompanied by a future particle, e.g., 'Whoever among you reverts from his religion, then Allah will bring a people whom He loves' [5:54] and 'Whatever good they do, they will not be denied it' [3:115]; sixth: it is accompanied by a particle that occupies the initial position, like 'rubba'. The fa enters in 'Whoever returns, then Allah will take vengeance on him' [5:95] because the verb is estimated as a predicate after an omitted subject, so the sentence is nominal. It has been mentioned that 'idha' of suddenness substitutes for fa, e.g., 'And if a calamity befalls them because of what their hands have forwarded, at once they become despondent' [30:36]. And the fa may be omitted due to necessity, as in his saying: 'Whoever does good deeds, Allah thanks him.' Al-Mubarrad prohibited this even in poetry and claimed the narration is 'Whoever does good, then the Merciful thanks him.' Al-Akhfash said this occurs in eloquent prose, including the verse 'If he leaves wealth, the bequest for parents' [2:180] and its interpretation precedes. Ibn Malik said it is permissible in prose rarely, including the hadith of the lost item: 'If its owner comes, otherwise, benefit from it.' And just as fa links the response to its condition, it links the quasi-response to the quasi-condition, as in 'The one who comes to me, then for him is a dirham.' End quote from Al-Mughni. Al-Muradi mentioned in his commentary on the Alfiyyah eleven places where linking with fa is obligatory: the nominal sentence, the verbal demand sentence, the inflexible verb, the one accompanied by 'sa' or 'sawfa' or 'qad' or negated by 'ma' or 'lan' or 'in', and the one accompanied by a oath with 'rubba'. He said: these responses are required to have fa because they are not suitable to be a condition. And the example of simile is easy. This does not contradict Al-Mughni's statement that they are confined to six, because the future particle includes 'sa', 'sawfa', and 'lan', and the particle with authority includes the oath and 'rubba'. The more precise is that the generality is by cause not by wording in both: from the ordering of the ruling which is the recompense in the first, and the prohibition of settling on the derived term which is killing and engaging, so it is repeated. End. This branch will be mentioned again in the following phrase, and that the truth is that here is on one of the two opinions. His statement: 'Al-Muradi mentioned in his commentary on the Alfiyyah eleven places' which were arranged in verse in Al-Fath: 'Learn that the response of the condition must be connected with fa; if it is a demand action, likewise a frozen or sworn verb, or with 'qad', 'rubba', 'sa', or 'sawfa', my boy, or a nominal sentence, or it is negated by 'ma' and 'lan' and 'in'; whoever deviates from what we have defined has transgressed.' The most concise is what Al-Radi mentioned: it is obligatory in four places: first: the demand sentence, like command, prohibition, interrogation, wish, exhortation, incitement, and supplication; second: the performative sentence like 'ni'ma' and 'bi'sa' and what implies the meaning of praise and blame, likewise 'asa', the verb of wonder, and oath; third: the nominal sentence; fourth: every verbal sentence prefixed with a particle other than 'la' and 'lam' in the present tense, whether the prefixed verb is past or present. Apparently, the demand sentence is not included under the performative, so he clarified afterwards what indicates the difference, saying: the performative sentence is free of time and the demand sentence is purely for future. Its completion is in it. In Sharh al-Tawdih, from the discussion of the performative clause: 'what its word accompanies its meaning' and the demand clause: 'what the existence of its meaning is delayed after the existence of its word'. End. All this is according to grammarians. But in the science of meanings, the demand clause is a division of the performative because it has no external reality that it matches or does not match, while the declarative clause has an external reality that it matches or does not match. By what we have established, it is clear that Al-Zayla'i's statement that its places are seven, and some arranged them saying: demand, nominal, frozen, with 'ma', 'qad', 'lan', and 'bi-al-tanfis', is deficient. And the verifier's addition in Fath al-Qadir that what Al-Muradi mentioned is not precise; the truth is what we have transmitted from Al-Radi. So when that is known, it branches that if one does not bring fa in a place where it is obligatory, then it becomes immediate, as in 'If you enter the house, you are divorced.' If he intended suspension, he is truthful in his intention; likewise if he intended postponement. From Abu Yusuf: it is suspended, considering his speech as beneficial, so the fa is implied. I say: the difference is based on the permissibility of omitting it optionally; the Kufans allowed it, upon which Abu Yusuf branched, and the Basrans prohibited it, upon which the madhhab branched. Al-Radi has narrated the dispute of the Kufans as mentioned. If you say: against the Basrans is the verse 'And if you obey them, then indeed you are polytheists' [6:121]; I say: Al-Radi answered it by presuming an oath, and it is possible that the verse 'And when Our verses are recited to them as clear proofs, their argument is nothing' [45:25] is similar, i.e., presuming an oath, and it is possible that 'idha' is for mere time without observing the condition, as not observed in 'And those who, when tyranny strikes them, they...' [42:39] and 'And when they are angry, they forgive' [42:37]. He answered with the waw. And in a place where fa is obligatory, it becomes immediate even if he intended suspension with an oath. In Al-Mi'raj: if he intended postponement, it is said it is valid and the waw is considered as the waw of beginning, but it is weak because the waw of beginning is not used at the beginning of speech. End. The apparent in Al-Muhit is that if he intended suspension, he is not trustworthy in his statement: 'The intention of suspension is not valid.' 'They will be victors' 'Learn that the response of the condition must be connected with fa; if it is a demand action, likewise a frozen or sworn verb, or with 'qad', 'rubba', 'sa', or 'sawfa', my boy, or a nominal sentence, or it is negated by 'ma' and 'lan' and 'in'; whoever deviates from what we have defined has transgressed, and if...'
Source pages p. 19

Commentary
This passage is an expansion by the commentator on the ruling regarding the particle 'fa' in conditional divorce. The base text likely stated that the response (jawab) of a condition must be linked with 'fa' necessarily. The commentator elaborates by quoting Al-Mughni, explaining that 'fa' is obligatory when the response is not suitable to be a condition itself. He lists six cases from Al-Mughni: nominal sentences, frozen verbs, performative verbs, past tense verbs in form and meaning, verbs with future particles (sa/sawfa/lan), and verbs with particles that occupy the sentence-initial position (like rubba). He then discusses exceptions like the sudden 'idha' substituting for 'fa', and the omission of 'fa' in necessity (e.g., poetry). He cites disputes among grammarians (Mubarrad, Akhfash, Ibn Malik). Then he mentions Al-Muradi's eleven cases and a poetic summary from Al-Fath. He then prefers Al-Radi's concise four categories. Crucially, the fiqh consequence is stated: if one omits the obligatory 'fa', the conditional statement becomes immediate (mutanajjiz) — that is, the divorce occurs immediately upon utterance, not suspended on the condition. However, if the speaker intended suspension, he is truthful in his intention (i.e., his claim is accepted). But there is an intra-Hanafi dispute: Abu Yusuf holds that even without 'fa', the statement can be suspended, considering the speaker's intention, effectively implying the 'fa'. The commentator explains that this dispute is rooted in the grammatical disagreement between the Kufan school (which allows optional omission) and the Basran school (which does not). The relied-upon position (al-madhhab) is the Basran view, so the divorce becomes immediate if 'fa' is omitted, regardless of the speaker's intention. However, some later texts like Al-Muhit state that the intention of suspension is not valid at all. The passage concludes with a poetic summary. This is a classic example of how grammatical rules directly impact fiqh rulings in conditional divorce.

Key Terms
الفاء (al-fa'): The Arabic particle 'fa' used to connect the response of a condition to its condition, indicating consequence.
الجواب (al-jawab): The response clause in a conditional sentence, which is linked to the condition by 'fa' when not suitable as a condition itself.
التنجز (al-tanajjuz): Immediate effect of a divorce pronouncement, as opposed to suspended (mu'allaq).

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) The divorce is suspended upon entry.
B) The divorce is immediate upon utterance.
C) The divorce is invalid.
D) The divorce depends on his intention.

Key Takeaway
Omitting the obligatory fa in conditional divorce renders the divorce immediate, regardless of the speaker's intention, according to the relied-upon Hanafi position.

The Four Positions of the Obligatory Conditional Fā' in Hanafi Talaq
Defining the four grammatical positions where the conditional fā' is obligatory.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the four positions where the conditional fā' is obligatory and explain the legal consequence of its omission in a suspended divorce.

Core Idea
The conditional fā' is wajib in four sentence types; omission can turn a suspended divorce into an immediate one.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Rā'iq defines the grammatical rule for when the fā' is mandatory in conditional sentences—a rule with direct legal consequences in Hanafi divorce law. By understanding these four categories, you will see how a simple grammatical structure can determine whether a divorce is suspended (mu'allaq) or immediate (munajjaz).

Guided Path
1
List the four positions
Identify the four categories given by al-Radi: requestive (talabiyya), creative (inshā'iyya), nominal, and verbal without lā/lam.
Memorize these as the foundation.
2
Distinguish talabiyya and inshā'iyya
Note that talabiyya is future-oriented while inshā'iyya is timeless; in balāgha, both are considered inshā'iyya.
This distinction resolves apparent contradictions in grammatical classifications.
3
Understand the legal implication
In Hanafi talaq, a conditional sentence without the fā' is considered immediate (munajjaz) because the answer is not explicitly linked to the condition.
The fā' signals conditionality; without it, the statement is interpreted as a direct pronouncement.
4
Evaluate the critique of al-Zayla'i
Al-Zayla'i claimed seven positions; the author shows his list is deficient and affirms al-Radi's four.
The critique highlights the importance of precise grammatical analysis in legal rulings.
5
Apply to a talaq example
Given a husband's statement, determine if the fā' is needed and what the legal ruling is.
Practice will solidify the connection between grammar and fiqh.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والأخصر ما ذكره الرضي أنها واجبة في أربعة مواضع: أحدها الجملة الطلبية كالأمر والنهي والاستفهام والتمني والعرض والتحضيض والدعاء الثاني الجملة الانشائية كنعم وبئس وما تضمن معنى انشاء المدح والذم وكذا عسى وفعل التعجب والقسم الثالث الجملة الاسمية. الرابع كل فعلية مصدرة بحرف سوى لا ولم في المضارع سواء كان الفعل المصدر ماضياً أو مضارعاً . وظاهره أن الطلبية لا تدخل تحت الانشائية ولذا صرح بعده بما يفيد التغاير فقال : إن الجملة الانشائية متجردة عن الزمان والطلبية متمحضة للاستقبال وتمامه فيه. وفي شرح التوضيح من بحث الصلة الانشائية ما قارن لفظها معناها والطلبية ما تأخر وجود معناها عن وجود لفظها اهـ. وهذا كله عند النحاة، وأما في علم المعاني فالطلبية من أقسام الانشائية لأنها ما ليس لها خارج تطابقه أو لا تطابقه والخبرية ما لها خارج تطابقه أو لا تطابقه. وبما قررناه ظهر أن قول الزيلعي أن مواضعها سبع ونظمها بعضهم فقال : طلبية واسمية وبجامد وبما وقد ولـن وبـالـتـنـفـيـس قاصر عن الاستيفاء وزيادة المحقق عليه في فتح القدير ما ذكره المرادي ليس تحريراً والحق ما أسلفناه عن الرضي
The most concise is what al-Radi mentioned: that it (the fā') is obligatory in four places: first, the requestive sentence (jumla talabiyya) like command, prohibition, interrogation, wishing, presentation, urging, and supplication; second, the creative sentence (jumla inshā'iyya) like ni‘ma and bi’sa and what contains the meaning of creating praise or blame, and likewise ‘asā, the verb of wonder, and oath; third, the nominal sentence; fourth, every verbal sentence introduced by a letter other than lā and lam in the imperfect, whether the source verb is past or imperfect. It is apparent that the requestive is not included under the creative, and therefore he stated afterward what indicates differentiation: the creative sentence is devoid of time, and the requestive is exclusively for the future; and its completion is in it. In Sharh al-Tawdih, from the discussion of the creative relative clause: 'the one whose expression coincides with its meaning, and the requestive is the one whose meaning's existence is delayed after the existence of its expression.' This altogether is according to grammarians; as for the science of meanings (bayan), the requestive is a subdivision of the creative because it is what has no external that corresponds or does not correspond, while the declarative is what has an external that corresponds or does not correspond. By what we have established, it becomes clear that al-Zayla'i's statement that its places are seven, and someone composed them saying: 'requestive, nominal, and with the rigid, and with mā, qad, lan, and with tanfīs' is deficient in comprehensiveness, and what the verifier (muhaqqiq) added upon him in Fath al-Qadir—what al-Muradi mentioned—is not a proper investigation; the truth is what we have previously cited from al-Radi.
Source pages p. 20

Commentary
This passage establishes the grammatical rule regarding the obligation of the fā' (فاء) in conditional sentences (jumal shartiyya), a rule that has direct legal consequences in Hanafi law of suspended divorce (ta‘līq). The author, citing al-Radi, identifies four categories of sentences where the fā' is wajib: (1) requestive sentences (talabiyya) including commands, prohibitions, questions, etc.; (2) creative/exclamatory sentences (inshā'iyya) like praise, blame, wonder, oaths; (3) nominal sentences (ismiyya); and (4) verbal sentences beginning with any particle except لَا and لَم in the imperfect tense. The passage clarifies that talabiyya and inshā'iyya are distinct categories in grammar but united in 'ilm al-ma‘ānī (rhetoric). The author then critiques al-Zayla'i's claim of seven positions, asserting that the correct view is al-Radi's four. This foundational definition determines when the absence of the fā' changes a conditional divorce from suspended (mu‘allaq) to immediate (munajjaz).

Key Terms
الجملة الطلبية (al-jumla al-ṭalabiyya): The requestive sentence: a sentence that expresses a demand (command, prohibition, interrogation, etc.) and is exclusively future in meaning.
الجملة الانشائية (al-jumla al-inshā'iyya): The creative sentence: a sentence where the word and its meaning coincide, such as praise or wonder, and it is devoid of time.
الفاء (al-fā'): The conditional fā' that connects the condition (shart) to its answer (jawāb), obligatory in certain sentence types to indicate the answer's dependence on the condition.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to al-Radi, how many positions require the fā' in a conditional sentence?
A) Seven
B) Four
C) Two
D) Six

Key Takeaway
The fā' is wajib in four specific sentence types; its absence can change a conditional divorce from suspended to immediate.

Omission of the Conditional Fā': Ruling and Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on the effect of omitting the fā'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the default Hanafi ruling when the fā' is omitted in a conditional divorce and explain Abu Yusuf's dissenting view based on grammatical principles.

Core Idea
Omission of the fā' makes a divorce immediate; intention of suspension is only accepted religiously.

When a husband says 'If you enter the house, you are divorced' without the fā' (the 'then' particle) in the answer, does the divorce take effect immediately or remain suspended until the condition occurs? This lesson explores that question through an intra-Hanafi dispute that hinges on a grammatical disagreement between the Basran and Kufan schools. Understanding this will help you see how deep linguistic analysis shapes fiqh rulings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the issue
The act is a husband uttering a conditional divorce phrase ('in dakhalti al-dar anti taliq') without the fā' in the answer. The issue is whether the absence of fā' makes the divorce immediate (tanzīz) or suspended (ta‘līq).
2
State the default ruling
According to the majority Hanafi position (following Basran grammar), the divorce is immediate because the conditional structure is incomplete without the fā'. The husband's intention to suspend is accepted only diyānatan, not in judicial court.
This is the muftā bihi.
3
Present Abu Yusuf's opposing view
Abu Yusuf holds that the divorce is suspended, based on the Kufan grammatical view that the fā' can be optionally omitted. He treats the sentence as a valid conditional by implying the fā'.
4
Analyze the grammatical basis of the dispute
The Basran view denies the permissibility of omitting the fā' by choice; the Kufans allow it. The author refutes Quranic counterexamples by explaining that those verses involve an implicit oath or use 'idhā' for mere time, not conditional fā' omission.
This shows how fiqh rulings rest on grammatical judgments.
5
Note the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence)
The practical difference: under Abu Yusuf's view, the wife is not divorced until the condition occurs; under the majority, she is divorced immediately unless the husband's claim of suspension is believed in a religious court.
This affects the wife's marital status and any subsequent actions.

فإذا عرف ذلك تفرع عليه أنه لو لم يأتي بالفاء في موضع وجوبها فإنه يتنجز كـ إن دخلت الدار أنت طالق فإن نوى تعليقه دين، وكذا إن نوى تقديمه. وعن أبي يوسف أنه يتعلق حملاً لكلامه على الفائدة فتضمر الفاء. قلت: الخلاف مبني على جواز حذفها اختياراً فأجازه أهل الكوفة وعليه فرع أبو يوسف، ومنعه أهل البصرة وعليه تفرع المذهب، وقد حكى الرضي خلاف الكوفيين كما ذكره، فإن قلت: يرد على البصريين قوله تعالى وإن أطعتموهم إنكم لمشركون ﴾ [الأنعام: ۱۲۱] قلت : قد أجاب عنه الرضي بأنه بتقدير القسم ويجوز أن يكون قوله تعالى وإذا تتلى عليهم آياتنا بينات ما كان حجتهم [الجاثية : ٢٥] مثله أي بتقدير القسم، ويجوز أن تكون «إذا» لمجرد الوقت من دون ملاحظة الشرط كما لم يلاحظ في قوله تعالى والذين إذا أصابهم البغي هم. [الشورى: ٣٩] وقوله تعالى وإذا ما غضبوا هم يغفرون) [الشورى: ٣٧] ا هـ . أجاب بالواو وفي موضع وجوب الفاء تنجز وإن نوى تعليقه بدين. وفي المعراج : ولو نوى تقديمه قيل يصح وتحمل الواو على الابتداء وفيه ضعف لأن واو الابتداء لا تستعمل في أول الكلام ا . اهـ. وظاهر ما في المحيط أنه لو نوى تعليقه لا يدين فإنه قال : ولا تصح نية التعليق أصلاً لأنه يحتاج إلى اسقاط حرف الواو ثم إلى اضمار حرف الفاء ولأن الإضمار إنما يصح متى أظهر ما أضمر لا يختل الكلام وهنا لو ظهر ما أضمر اختل الكلام لأنه يصير «إن دخلت الدار فو أنت طالق
When that is known, it branches from it that if one does not bring the fā' in its obligatory place, then it (the divorce) takes effect immediately, like 'If you enter the house, you are divorced' — if he intends suspension, he is believed religiously; likewise if he intends advancement. From Abu Yusuf: it is suspended, as his words are construed beneficially, so the fā' is implied. I say: the disagreement is built upon the permissibility of omitting it by choice; the Kufans allowed it, and Abu Yusuf built upon that; the Basrans forbade it, and the madhhab is built upon that. Al-Radi has related the Kufans' disagreement as mentioned. If you say: an objection to the Basrans is Allah's saying: 'And if you obey them, you are indeed polytheists' (6:121). I say: al-Radi answered it by turning on an implicit oath, and it is permissible that His saying: 'And when Our clear signs are recited to them, their argument is' (45:25) is similar, i.e., with an implicit oath, and it is permissible that 'idha' is for mere time without taking condition into account, as not taken into account in His saying: 'and those who, when tyranny strikes them, they defend themselves' (42:39), and His saying: 'and when they become angry, they forgive' (42:37). He answered with wāw. And in the place where the fā' is obligatory, it (the divorce) takes effect immediately, even if he intends suspension religiously. In al-Mi'raj: if he intends advancement, it is said it is valid and the wāw is construed as initial wāw, but there is weakness in this because the initial wāw is not used at the beginning of speech. And the apparent meaning of what is in al-Muhit is that if he intends suspension, he is not believed religiously, for he said: 'And the intention of suspension is not valid at all because it requires dropping the wāw and then implying the fā', and because implication is only valid when if one makes explicit what is implied, speech is not corrupted; here, if what is implied is made explicit, speech would be corrupted because it would become: 'If you enter the house, fā-you are divorced.'
Source pages p. 20

Commentary
This passage applies the grammatical rule from the previous lesson to a specific case in the law of suspended divorce (ta‘līq al-ṭalāq). If a husband says 'If you enter the house, you are divorced' (إن دخلت الدار أنت طالق) without the fā' in the answer, the default ruling is immediate divorce (tanzīz), because the absence of the fā' makes the sentence declarative rather than conditional. However, if the husband intended suspension (ta‘līq), he is believed in religious court (diyānatan) but not in judicial court (qaḍā’an). The author then presents the intra-Hanafi dispute: Abu Yusuf holds that the divorce is suspended because he treats the husband's speech as beneficial, implying the fā'. This difference stems from a grammatical disagreement between the Kufan school (allowing optional omission of the fā') and the Basran school (disallowing it). The Hanafi madhhab follows the Basran view. The author refutes an objection using Quranic verses (6:121, 45:25) where the fā' appears to be omitted; al-Radi explains these as involving an implicit oath or 'idhā' for mere time. The passage concludes that in the obligatory position of the fā', the divorce takes effect immediately even if the husband intends suspension. Further, if he intends advancement (taqdīm), it is weak. The opinion from al-Muhīt denies the validity of suspension entirely because implying the fā' corrupts the speech.

Key Terms
تنجيز (tanzīz): The divorce takes effect immediately upon utterance, not suspended on a future condition.
تعليق (ta‘līq): Suspensive divorce: the divorce is conditional upon a future event and does not occur until that event happens.
إضمار الفاء (iḍmār al-fā'): Implying the fā' in the sentence to create a conditional structure, allowed by Kufans but rejected by Basrans.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the majority Hanafi position, what is the ruling when a husband says 'In dakhalti al-dar anti taliq' (without fā') and does not express any intention?
A) The divorce is suspended until she enters the house.
B) The divorce is immediate and valid.
C) The divorce is invalid due to the missing fā'.
D) The divorce depends on the court's interpretation.

Key Takeaway
Without the fā', the divorce is immediate; any intention of suspension is only accepted religiously.
